THE RED PAGE.
An Australian in China .
THE tragedy of Peking seems to have been
one of those heroic events which set the
w'orld aflame, and when pictured by
the genius of bard or orator become indel-
ible in history. The human power of exaltation
in the face of death must have been seen at
its highest on that day at the end of June
■when the breach was made in the Legation
walls, and the little remnant of Europeans,
knowing the worst had come, slew wife and
child after one last heartbreaking embrace, then
turned their faces to the grim fanatic foe, and
died fighting. It is a hard tiling to say that those
Europeans reaped what Europe has sowed ; but it
is true. Ever since we have known her,
China has asked only one thing—to be
let alone. As Justin McCarthy phrased it,
her prayer to Europe has been the prayer
of Diogenes to Alexander, “Get out of
my sunshine ! ” It would seem as if all through
the centuries the Chinese had been given some
vague instinct, some inconscient animal pre-
monition, of the trouble which the foreigners are
bringing to their country. But Europe would
never let. China alone. At first the religious
greed of proselytes, then greed of trade, now
greed of territory, have striven to pass, and to
elude, and to batter down the closed gate of
China.
The Chinese are the most inoffensive of races.
Their moral character is high. Through their
land, where they have not been spoiled by Euro-
peans, the stranger walks in peace and safety.
Left to themselves, they would never, as a nation,
commit one act of aggression against the Occiden-
tal nations. If there is any force in the belief that
a man and a nation are entitled to possess their
own land, enjoy their own liberty, as long as they
do not interfere with the possessions and liberties
of others, then the Chinese have fully earned the
quiet they desire. Subjected to continual annoy-
ance and attack in their homes and in their cities,
is it any wonder that the worm should turn in
savage and pitiful reprisal ?
■ China mixes myriads of men of various races
afid religions, in a vast territory where exist
all conditions of soil and climate. It is no
wonder that the Government should often
be unable to control the mass of its sub-
jects. But the Chinese have learnt how to
control themselves. Their civilisation is no
fieSs stable than ancient. Confucius inculcated
the best principles of Jesus half-a-thousand years
before Jesus was born. Commerce, banking,
literature, art, the sciences of engineering and
architecture—these were taught and practised in
China when the Briton was a painted barbarian.
The Chinese communities have for ages been hard-
labouring, intelligent and happy. The Chinese
character is the admiration of every un-
biassed traveller. In China learning is
reverenced, integrity is honoured, family life
is, for the most part, a pattern of piety
and love. Is it any wonder that the Chinese,
considering the Europeans who come among them,
knowing that contact with Europe has brought
them little but social degradation and civil tur-
moil, should resent even well-meant endeavours to
revolutionise their religion, tlieir character, their
mode of life ?
These are views which might have' been ex-
pressed, and in part have been expressed, by
George Morrison, the Australian who was among
those killed at Peking. It is irony which no one
would more quickly appreciate than he, that a
man so much in sympathy with the Chinese in
their resistance to Europe, should be one of the
innocent victims of the European aggression.
And though doubtless he fought with the best,
and killed as many “Boxers ” as he could, selling
bis life dearly, his was just the mind which would
rise superior to his individual environment, and in
its last moment of consciousness reflect sardonic-
ally that maybe the gods are just.
George Ernest Morrison av»s born at Geelong,
“Victoria, in 1862. His father was principal of the
Scots College at Geelong, and his good Scots
name gave promise of his grit and manhood. He
was a fine fellow. Not a writer ; he only wrote
incidentally, though his writing is g-ood reading.
Morrison was an adventurer, an explorer, an ad-
ministrator and a man of action. When he had
done with boyhood they tried to make a doctor of
him at Melb. University, and at first failed badly.
He bothered little with book-learning. According
to his own story, in the examination . for the
Second-Year medicine, hoping the more to im-
press the Professors, he entered his name for
honours—and they rejected him in the preliminary
pass.
It seems that in the examination in Materia Medica I
had, among other trifling lapses, prescribed a dose of
Oleum Grotonis of “ one-half to two drachms carefully in-
creased.” I confess that I had never heard of the
wretched stuff ; the question was taken from far on in
the text-hook, and, unfortunately, my reading had not
extended quite so far. When a deputation from my
family waited upon the examiner to ascertain the cause
of my misadventure, the only satisfaction we got was the.
obliging assurance “ that you might as well let a mad dog
loose in Oollias-street ” as allow me to become a doctor
And then the examiner produced my prescription. But 1
thought I saw a faint chance of escape. I pointed a ner-
vous finger to the two words “ carefully increased,” and
pleaded that that indication of caution ought to save me.
“Save you it might,” -he shouted, with unnecessary
vehemence ; “ but, God bless my soul, man ! it would not
save your patient. ” The examiner Wfs a man intemperate
of speech ;so 1 left the University. It was a severe blow
to the University, but the University survived it.
And Morrison followed his beat. He undertook
a canoe voyage from Albury to the mouth of the
Murray. He tramped round the coast from Mel-
bourne to Adelaide. He tramped across Australia
from Carpentaria Gulf to Melbourne—which was
something of a. feat in 1882. Then be went
wandering among the islands off N.E. Australia.
In New Guinea in 1883 lie was crippled by a native
apear ; and'he walked ill till the spearhead was
but out by an E&inburgh surgeon nine months
later. In ’B7 he took bis degree of M.D. Re-
turning to the East, he saw Japan, and per-
formed the , journey which made him famoiis—
sailing and riding and walking from Shanghai,
3000 miles across Southern and Western China, to
Burma. This is the journey recorded -m “An
In China. ’ * In Calcutta he yearly died
of'fever. His: pluck and constitution and his
name of Morrison pulled him through, mid he was
aoon bank in China, travelling' in Siam and
and l&t©r aor® , iS iVlancnuna. Jxon-
don Times stationed him as i*s correspondent at
Beking with a Prime Minister S salary, lie did
splendid work, often securing better and earlier
news than the British Embassy. Only the other
day he was back visiting his people m Australia,
jte returned to Peking to the death which
Bowlby his predecessor as Times correspondent m
China, suffered at the hands of the Chi nose.
“An Australian in China” is George Mor-
rison’s single book ; for the letters ®to Melbourne
papers recording his earlier journeys have not
been reprinted, and his special correspondence
remains in the Times files. His friends should
make a posthumous volume or two from these
sources.
Morrison reached China with all an Australian’s
prepossession against the Chinese as industrial
competitors who in Australia and America reduce
white men’s wages. He found the Chinese in
their own land a friendly and civilised .race.
(Those who come to Australia are, as a rule, of an
inferior class —some of them the scourings of the
treaty ports.)
I went to China possessed with the strong racial anti-
pathy to the Chinese common to my countrymen, but
that feeling has long since given way to one of lively sym-
pathy and gratitude, and I shall always look back with
pleasure to this journey, during which I experienced,
while traversing provinces as wide as European • king-
doms, uniform kindness and hospitality, and the most
charming courtesy. In my case, at least, the Chinese did
not forget their precept, “ Deal gently with strangers
from afar.”
Yet Morrison remained firmly convinced of the
necessity of excluding the Chinese from Australia.
“ It is this class of Chinese,” he says, referring to
the Cantonese of the S.E. provinces,
who have already driven us out of the Northern Terri-
tory of Australia, and whose unrestricted entry into the
other colonies we must prevent at all hazards. We can-
not compete with Chinese ; we cannot intermix orliiarry
with them; they are aliens in language, thought,
and customs; they are working Z animals of low
grade but great vitality. The Chinese is temperate,
frugal, hard-working, and law-evading, if not law-abiding
—we all acknowledge that. He can outwork an English-
man, and starve him out of the country- no one can deny
that. To compete successfully with a Chinaman, the
artisan or labourer of our own flesh and blood would
require to be degraded into a mere mechanical beast of
labour, unable to support wife or family, toiling seven
days in the week, with no amusements, enjoyments, or
comforts of any kind, no interest in the country, contri-
buting no share towards the expense of government,
living on food that he would now reject with loathing,
crowded with his fellows ten or fifteen in a
room that he would not now live in alone, ex-
cept with repugnance. Admitted freely into Australia,
the Chinese would starve out the Englishman, in accord-
ance with the law of currency—that of two currencies in
a country the baser will always supplant the better.
“In Victoria,” says Professor Pearson, “ a single trade —
that of furniture-making—was taken possession of and
ruined for white men within the Space of something like
five years.” In the small colon}' of Victoria there are
9377 Chinese in a population' of 1,150,000; in all China,
with its population of 350,000,000, there are only 8081
foreigners (Dyer Ball), a la%e proportion of whom are
working for China’s salvation.
There is not room for both in Australia. Which is to
be our colonist, the Asiatic or the Englishman ?
» » * *
This was written in 1895. Since then the immi-
gration of Chinese into Australia has been to some
extent checked ; but, into Queensland particu-
larly, the immigration of races inferior to Chinese
continues. And in Queensland last month, the
Queensland Government, which squanders hundreds
of thousands of pounds in propping up the pri-
vately-owned Q.N. Bank, was discovered saving
shillings by letting a contract for the erection of
telegraph-poles to Chinese, at a trifle less than the
amount previously paid to white men !
Apart from its record of scenes and incidents
(the whole journey from Shanghai in China
to Bhamo in Burma cost less than £2O) Morri-
son’s book is interesting on two main topics—
missions and the opium traffic. He points out
the ludicrous futility of the missionaries’ work.
Many of the missionaries live in luxury; but the
1511 Protestant missionaries labouring in China
could convert in 1893 only 3127 Chinese, at a cost
of £350,000 —which is at the rate of a fraction
more than two converts per missionary per
annum, at a cost of £lll per convert. The figure
is high ; particularly as many of the converts are
“ rice Christians,” whose religion is trustworthy
only as long as they get daily rations from the
mission—-and the last moral state of the renegade
from Confucius is frequently worse than the nSt.
In the matter of the opium traffic Morris ex-
poses the folly of believing that the moderate use
of opium is injurious t» health, or that the
Chinese will ever stop using it. Tlieir home-
grown opium far exceeds in quantity that im-
ported from India. But the officials certainly
wish to stop the importation from India for reasons
of Protection, because it interferes with the local
monopoly. #
* * « *
Morrison is not a writer born ; but he lias the
open mind, the humourous eye, and his writing is
good reading. And it is hard to die at 38 when a
man is so well able to enjoy life, and feels within
him so much power of doing and achieving. Death
gave Morrison many chances, and at the last took
him suddenly where no peril to life was looked-
for. It doesn’t seem playing fair. But
The Ball no question makes of Ayes and Noes,
But Here or There strikes as the Player goes;
And He that toss’d you down into the Field,
He knows about it all—He knows—HE knows !
The Coming Australian.
[Many Australian medical practitioners possess valuable
data for generalising abou-t the Australian race—the way
in which native-born whites of European descent are
affected by climate, by occupation, by social conditions, by
that environment which includes all these factors and others
too intangible to he defined, yet all-important in fixing
the character and destiny of the race. If leading generali-
sations could be established, even as theories, the deduc-
tions would be of the utmost service to physicians, legis-
lators, sociologists and ethnologists. But it is very rare
to find a medical petitioner who will take the pains to
survey his experience, tabulate his knowledge, and pub.
lieh the results. Dr. R. Humphry Marten is therefore to
be thanked and. commended for the excellent presidential
address on “ The Effects of Migration from the Northern
to the Southern Hemisphere which he delivered- before
the S.A. branch of the Bri Sish Medical Association on 28th
June. By the courtesy of Hr. S. T. Knaggs, editor of The
Australasian Mediectl Gazette, an abridged portion of this
address is reprinted below. Some medical terms have
been translated Into common speech.]
I HAVE practised our.profession in Australia
for the hat twelve years (said Dr. Marten)
but have been intimately associated
with Australians for sixteen years before
coming here myself. Everyone will admit
that the climate of Australia is absolutely differ-
ent from that of northern Europe, whence most
of the people of this continent have been derived ;
and it has been laid down as an axiom, “ That the
physiological functions of most persons,, wlio quit
their native land and settle in a wholly different
region, must undergo a considerable change if
new conditions are not to have injurious
effects upon them.” And although our northern
ancestors and their descendants, who are
in the northern hemisphere, living under
conditions common to all, may undergo evolution,
it would be in a direction common to the whole
species ; but those who migrate to the southern
hemisphere, and especially if there be little inter-
breeding between the two races, and with the
different environments, would be expected to de-
velop on divergent lines of evolution.
My first experience of Australians commences
with boys whom I met at a boarding-school near
London, and what struck me most was the bronzed
skin and athletic forms of the southerners, who
were fairly hard-working, but who had no great
mental powers beyond the English boys of their
own age.
The next Australians were those whom I came
in contact with when studying medicine in Lon-
don ; it was then very noticeable that, although
of the average age of the students, the
Australians looked much older for their years
than we did; their hirsute appendages were much
greater ; their development, especially of their
bodies and limbs, was complete; in fact, they
seemed, both in personal appearance and general
knowledge of the world, to be from three to five
years in advance of the English undergraduates.
And their mental condition was more stable, they
were able to grasp and assimilate facts much more
readily, and for the first few years of the course
were able to take the lead ; but in the last year or
two it was noticeable that persons from the cooler
climates gradually caught up to and finally passed
their southern contemporaries, and the men who
had gained the gold medals in the first few years
were generally left behind in the final struggles.
The reason for this being that the Australians,
coming from a warm climate, had come to
maturity more quickly than those who bad de-
veloped more slowly, but perhaps, I may be
excused for saying, more surely than the
southerners.
I have always likened in my own mind the
development of the two peoples to the growth of
the oak-trees in. the two hemispheres. It is an
established fact, that in the cold climate the -
oaks grow more slowly, but in the end produce
much more durable timber than their quickly-
growing desendants in this climate. Here the
oaks grow up with great rapidity, but I very
much doubt if they will, in the end, live to the
great age of the northern oaks; and there is
very little doubt that their timber is not so
strong or lasting. And I believe it is the same
with the Australian-born descendants of the early
colonists, and unless they occasionally cross with
the northern races they will tend to become a tall,
thin race, the climate apparently being unsuitable,
withoutfrequent interbreeding, to the development
of a sturdy, thick-set, durable people. The
southerners were like my schoolfellows, grand
athletes, as well as good workers. One man I re-
member in particular, who played cricket for an
Australian Eleven at that time visiting England,
was so engrossed in his work that it is said of him
that he actually read his Ellis’ Anatomy whilst
waiting for his innings at Lords’ cricket ground,
and I think there is no doubt that is true.
There was nothing striking in their language, it
appeared to be exactly like our own ; but they
were always pining for the sun, and detested the
cold and damp, sunless north. ■■
The next Australians I came in contact with
were at Cambridge. They had the same advanced
style about them, but it was not so noticeable to
me, as I was their senior. Whatever they under-
took they did well—if they worked they read well,
if they took to any particular branch of sport they
as often as not came out on the top, and if they
were lazy and went to the bad, they did it
extremely well, far out-running their northern
confreres even in this line ; but they had a
redeeming point here, which, as a rule, was not so
apparent among the English-—namely, after having
idled away their time for a number of months, or
years, they had the power of suddenly turning •
over a new leaf, and taking an extremely good
position in later life. I noticed this in several
instances, and when they had once turned they
stuck to their work with a great pertinacity.
So much for the Australians I met with in the
colder climates, who were, as a rule, of the thin,
muscular, wiry type, generally showing the effects
of the sun on their complexions, some being fair,
and others, perhaps the majority, had dark hair
with dark eyes.
* * * *
I have now spent twelve years in South Aus-
tralia, and no one will deny that the inhabitants
are the most hospitable of people. They will
make an extremely fine race of people in the
future, and in time to come will have to be
reckoned with as one of the great nations. The
general commendations they have received and
the responsible positions which have been allotted
to them during the Transvaal campaign, all point
to their development into some of the finest
soldiers in the world —they have any amount
of self - confidence, and a way peculiar to
themselves of getting out <of tight corners.
From observations I have noted since re-
siding in Australia, I would say they are an
energetic race, probably more so than their
northern ancestors, with good mental capacity,
easily taking in knowledge and retaining it, and
viewing all the wonders of the world im a very
matter-of-fact sort of way.
Taking them as a whole, there are more of the
dark-complexioned and dark-haired people than
the light-haired and blue-eyed Saxons. Whether
this is in any way due to the increased exposure
to the sun, and whether they will eventually be-
come a dark-skinned race, remains to be proved.
Their hair is generally straight, but tends to
coarseness, and is prone to leaye a tonsure -pretty
early in life; this may be due in part to the neces-
sity there is for keeping the head ao.oonstantly
covered from thb sun. Their bodies and their
faces are more prone to a luxurious hirsute growth
than the northerners, due- to «he greater hyper-
semia* of the skin from the increased exposure to
the solar rays. They are also more inclined to go
grey earlier than their ancestors.
The eyes as a rule tend to grey and dark brown.
The blue, so common among the Anglo-Saxons at
home, is rather the exception.
The eyebrows and eyelashes are often exces-
sively developed, especially amongst the true bush-
men ; and I think this is probably a provision of
nature to protect these sensitive organs, not only
from the excessive glare of the sun but also from
the dust. You often see at the outer angles of the
orbits, where the constantly contracting orbicu-
laris muscle [circular muscle of the eye] has
developed wrinkles, that there are white and red
lines interspersed, this being due to the sun burn-
ing the tops of the wrinkles and leaving the
creases white. It often produces a peculiar ap-
pearance on a cloudy day when the eye is opened
to its full.
* « * *
The nose, so far, has no characteristic shape
such as a Roman or a Grecian, and fortunately it
shows no tendency, and probably never will do, to
the Platyrrhinic [broad, flat-nosed] type so marked
in the early inhabitants of this vast land. It is
thin, comes to a fairly sharp angle at the bridge,
with skin stretched tightly across it; the nostrils
are not large, but the groove round the outer side
of the ala nasi [fleshy wing of the nose] is well
marked, and here there is often a goodly growth
of hair. The nostrils, especially of the true bush-
man, often have a great capillary growth, another
provision of nature against dust.
The lips are finely cut, and occasionally dry and
sunburnt, and I never remember seeing the
mucous membrane of the lips freckled except in
this country.
The cheeks of bearded men show a profusion of
thick hair, often growing high up on the malar
[cheek] bones.
The foreheads are generally broad and high and
fairly straight. They show no great development
of the superciliary ridges [over the eyebrows],
which are so characteristic of the Australian
aboriginals.
The malar bones are well developed, and the
zygomatic archest prominent, more so than in
Europe, this being due, it is said, to the greater
work the masticatory muscles have to do in biting
the food, especially meat; the chins are rather
pointed, tending to give the face an ovoid [egg-
shaped] foikn. The orbital angles are the same as
in Europe.

Various Verses.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Grief.
The stars are nodding across the river
That marks my Night from the Day that’s gone.
The night wind croons with a little shiver
A dead love’s song as it wanders on.
The stars’ warm tears on the grass are clinging—
The grass that kissed at her passing feet.
The river mourns, as it sweeps a-singing,
For someone’s voice that was rich and sweet.
[ The stars hung lower to hear her laughter;
The river flashed her a loving gleam;
Her lips were sweet, but this dull, grey after
Is bitterer far than the bitterest dream.
The stars shine clear, but her eyes flashed clearer
With love that once in their lives men see,
And I hold one thought of her whispers dearer
Than any song of the stream can be.
O stars that float in the deep, dark river !
O stream that winds with her lissome grace !
Help me to pray to the Great Love-Giver
That I may dream of a dead girl’s face.
M.L.
QUILP N.
The Last Toast.
They were our little playmates
In the far-away cradle days,
With their toddling gait and their lisping speech
And their wonderful baby w«ys ;
And the daisies grew for their dimpled hands,
And the sunbeams shone for their curls—
And they were our little playmates,
Let us drink to them now—“ The Girls! ”
And the days went by and we found them fair,
And we found them, oh ! so sweet!
And we fell in love with their rippling hair
And the trip of their dainty feet ;
And the beauty the good gods gave them
They doubled with silk and pearls—
And the days went by and we found them fair—
Let us pledge them to-night —“ The Girls 1 ”
And some of those wayward misses
Have grown to be widow and wife ;
Some are only a dream of kisses
To mock at a lone man’s life.
But whether they played for pastime,
Or whether they fought us fair,
Let us pledge them once in the ruby wine,
For the sake of the days that were !
. . •
Now fill to the brim with hemlock !
And lips that are tired of wine
Shall drink to the hearts that were broken
In days of the dear lang syne—
Shall drink to the buried laughter,
Shall drink to the old loves dead ;
For the world has stolen our playmates,
And the girl of our heart is wed !
N.S.W.
WILL OGILVIE.
Fate.
At bed and board I meet his face,
He stands wherever I may be ;
My secret thoughts are his to read,
He constantly companions me.
Though I should glove my hand, he puts
Within my grasp an edged sword ;
I fill my mouth with honey-speech, *
And lo ! he sends the acid word.
I had a whole heart once, could love,
Have faith in men, God’s works adore ;
He came to concentrate my gaze
Upon the canker at the core.
I cannot run, he trips me up ;
Nor climb—he forthwith drags me down ;
And yet, and yet, less worth than mine
Oft sought the laurel or the crown.
There was a dear fight I had fought,
I might have won—gave blow for blow ;
He stole behind and pinned my arms,
And left me open to my foe.
The woman who was born for me
(Warm nourishment of soul and heart),
He brought me face to face with her,
But kept us all the world apart.
I tried to kill him many times,
Wheeled suddenly with dagger-thrust;
He brushed my rebel-stroke aside,
And rolled my valour in the dust.
I must be brave before my friends,
Praise him indeed for what he’s not;
Or they, linked arm-in-arm, may say—
“ The fellow quarrels with the lot.”
’Tis said that Patience brings amends,
Makes all things right—to love turns hate ;
But though my fiend turn friend—what then ?
What can he give to compensate ?
The love I lost must live in man,
The dearest deeds remain undone,
The laurel-wreath I win be less
Than that I might of old have won.
The noon’s the time to seek the sun ;
An hour or two, and sunken-eyed
He loses charm ; and so in youth
Youth’s pleasures must be satisfied.
Else who can say that he has lived,
And bless the world with yielded breath . . .
Wherefore my foe, my foe may be,
Till comes that mightiest foeman—Death.
N.S.W.
RODERIO QUINN.

Special Notice
The whole contents of this paper are copyright by the
publishers. The right to reproduce or translate any
article, tale, sketch, poem, paragraph, illustration, or
any other part of the contents of this number, is hereby
expressly reserved by the publishers.
The Editor will carefully read and acknowledge in the
“ Correspondence” column all contributions submitted,
whether in the form of Political, Social, or other Articles,
Verse, Short Tales or Sketches (those dealing with Aus-
tralian subjects, and not exceeding two columns in length,
or, say, 8000 words, are specially acceptable), Paragraphs,
Letters, or Newspaper clippings. All communications
will be regarded as strictly confidential.
The Bulletin will return all unsuitable MS. when the
address is specified and stamps are enclosed. But we
desire those sending MS. to distinctly understand that we
are not responsible for its preservation or transmission.

or other topical drawings in line or wash, of sufficient

taining a good idea, but unfitted for direct reproduction
— i.e., any picture which has to be re-drawn—-will be paid
for as a suggestion only. The conditions under which
such artistic contributions will be received are those
applying to MS.

SATURDAY, JULY 28, 1900.
The Pro-Chow Press.
Various persons have already wasted a consider-
able amount of good ink in writing to this office to
ask if The Bulletin —in view of some recent
utterances —seriously advocates the despatch of
Australian contingents to China. This paper
doesn't advocate sending contingents to China—it
only desires to point out that other people who
ought to do so are unaccountably not doing it.
Personally it opposes the idea for exactly the
same reasons which lead it to oppose all contin-
gents : That this country shouldn’t join in wars
which it has no voice in making or unmaking ;
that it shouldn’t cultivate the spirit of armed
swagger; that it shouldn’t interfere in quarrels
which are not its quarrels ; that the collection of
teeth and ears as trophies isn’t a civilised amuse-
ment ; and that a country which fails to pay its
way in time of peace has, by its own showing, no
money to fool away on war. But unless the Jingo
party in Australia is a bombastic and weak-
kneed and altogether pitiable crowd of im-
postors it ought now’ to be up and doing ; and
the contempt which its cold, flat, and hesitating
attitude inspires is beyond words. A few months
ago it heaved the epithet (intended to be deeply
injurious) of “pro Boer” at the section of the
community which did not approve of sending
contingents to South Africa; now, by its own
logic, it has mostly turned Pro-Chow because it
warns the Australian people against contingenting
in China. To be pro-Boer was at least to be
pro a white man who treated his prisoners
kindly, and wasn’t more atrocious than the
average white combatant, and not one-tenth as
atrocious as the average cablegram. To be pro-
Chow is to be pro a yellow fiend who has already
skinned British subjects alive, burnt out their
eyes, or otherwise put them to unspeakable tor-
tures. What a pitiable falling-off it all is !
♦ • • •
Six months, or even three months ago, this
country was deafened with the cry that Australia
was now going to hump her fair share of the
White Man’s Burden; that her appear-
ance in brown clothes on the veldt
had conclusively proved to Britain that
it could always rely on Australia’s assistance
in the hour of need, and to foreign nations that
they had Australia to reckon with if they insulted
the mother-land orits flag ; and that Paul Kruger
had practically federated the British Empire. *
And now a big enemy—not a mere speck like
Paul Kruger— has not only insulted Britain and
its flag, but is alleged to have fried its ambassador
or something like that, and is butchering British
subjects promiscuously, and a scanty British force
is almost reduced to fighting for its life, while the
honor of the flag and the Mother-land has to take
care of itself for the present. And the Australian
Jingo party has dropped its share of the White
Man’s Burden almost unanimously. Its loyalty,
has been put away to keep cool, and its devotion
to the Mother-land is either dead or suspended.
When Britain had one war on hand with almost
the smallest State on earth, and had almost
as big an army in the field as the whole
population, counting men, women and children,
of its opponents’ country, the Jingo faction in
Australia rushed to its assistance (at 4s. fid. a day),
and the Australian soldiery, urged to a mental
drunk by the faction’s shrieks, lighted out en-
thusiastically for the land where there were
gold - fields and prospective land - grants. In
these days whoever disapproved of contingent-
ing was “ disloyal,” and whoever doubted
that the movement was actuated by deep,
true, enduring patriotism was a ruffian of
the deepest dye. Now Britain has two large
wars and a small one on hand. The odds are
against it. It is a case this time, not only of help-
ing the mother-land, and upholding the flag, but
of saving British subjects from an unspeakably
horrible death. It is much more a case of repelling
aggression than the Transvaal war ever was. It
is a matter, not of avenging a mere incidental
defeat like that of Majuba, but of avenging
fellow-citizens who were flayed and had their
feet sawn off and such-like horrors. It
isn’t an instance of helping Britain in a war that
she could finish with one hand whenever she chose
to put forth her strength, but of aiding her against
a foe as large as herself and her allies put together.
Barring the gold-mines and the prospective land-
grants, every reason why Australia could possibly
want to offer troops for the Transvaal is twenty
times a reason for offering troops for China. And
up to time of writing about 200 men have offered
—provided they get 7s. 6d. a day. Arid the press
that was once Jingo and is now pro-Chow almost
unanimously drops a wet blanket on the move-
ment.
* m • «
The so-oalled pro-Boer press opposed the send-
ing of contingents to the Transvaal because it
regarded the war as unjust, because it held that
Australia shouldn’t meddle in outside quarrels,
and because it knew that Great Britain was
quite able to take care of herself. The pro-
Chow press laid it down with astonishing
unanimity that it was Australia’s duty ana
privilege to interfere in the Empire’s outside
quarrels, and it doesn’t for a moment dare to
maintain that the recent doings' of the yellow
man are capable of apology or defence. The pro-
Boer press acted according to its conscience;
the pro-Chow press, by its own showing, acts
against its alleged conscience and according to
nothing visible save its cowardice or its cynical
indifference to its own alleged principles. And
the pro-Chow orators and patriots and flag-flappers
are m the same position as the pro-Chow press.
The fact that they don’t defend the cause of the
Chinamen as other papers did that of the Boers
only makes their treason and their desertion of
their principles all the worse. Ttie Bulletin op-
posed the idea of assisting Britain in what it held
to' be an unjust war; its backed-down Jingo con-
temporaries mostly oppose the idea of aiding
Britain in a war which they hold to be just—which
is all the difference between following a prin-
ciple and suffering a pitiable moral slump. And,
by their wild rage of a few months ago, and
their coldness now, they are doing their best to
put Australia in the disgraceful position of looking
like a country which promises much and does
little ; which has brief hot fits of fury and long
cold fits of indifference ; which is ready to hit a
very small enemy but dislikes to face a large one
—or else which went to war for no principle but
merely actuated by a spasmodic desire for novelty,
or went to war thinking it was a picnic, and
is shocked to find that it isn’t. It was the frenzy
of a little while back which makes the present re-
lapse into the ordinary state of comparative indif-
ference assumes this ridiculous aspect. Only a
few weeks have passed since the duty of helping
the mother country was preached so fervently,
and the falling-away is too sudden to look respect-
able. No person or country should lash itself
into a state of unspeakable curse and frenzy at a
very small antagonist if it is going to look with
blank indifference immediately after at the vastly
greater provocation given by a very big one. It
shouldn’t volunteer so enthusiastically to help
Britain when it has odds of two or three or four to
one in its favor, if it isn’t equally ready to
help when Britain and its not-too-reliable allies
have odds of probably 20 to 1 against them.
The Jingo press and politicians that were—the
pro-Chow press and politicians that are—are
bringing Australasia to this pass, and the situa-
tion is calculated to make the outside world drop
mud on our good name. Even if the very small
Chinese contingents now spoken of do materialise,
and are shipped to the land where prisoners of
war get their feet sawn off, the remarkable back-
wardness of the forward movement will still leave
Australia in a very unenviable aspect.
• * * •
So the situation may be summed up thus: If
the Jingo press and politicians and orators had
worked themselves into the same white heat of
fury about helping the mother-land when she had
a big enemy on hand as they did when she had a
very small one, they would have at least been
people who had the courage of their opinions,
whether the opinions were good or bad. Now
they are mere blatant wind-bags.
M‘Ilwraith.
So Thomas MTlwraith in his turn has met that
unpleasant fact called Death. Well, in his last
moments he had no reason to complain. He led
a good lively life, made friends, beat down baffling
foes, struggled, triumphed, failed, was finally
extinguished in dishonor. Not a great man, but a
big man, decidedly. A man of the Parkes type,
in his best days an embodiment of Force. Like
Parkes, he came of good vital stock, and the
shock of his personality bore down opposition.
little rugged, wholly masterful,
he wanted his own way always, and usually
got it. The average man, timorously expos-
tulating at directors’ meetings over which
MTlwraith presided, fell before his frown
as if struck by a club. MTlwraith, of course,
was by much Parkes’s inferior in the standard of
human values. He lacked Parkes’s emotional
yearnings, aesthetic impulses, power of imagin-
ative flight. Parkes had wings ; he walked, but
he could soar. MTlwraith was intensely prac-
tical ; he moved solidly, stolidly, along the plane
of earth. And he preferred not to bleed his
friends. The writer has heard him quote
Burns when he— MTlwraith, not the writer
—was reasonably drunk. Very likely he
quoted Burns when he was unreasonably
sober —he was Caledonian enough for that and
more. Drunk or sober, every Scotchman will
quote Burns —just because he is Scotch, and
Burns is Burns. But by Poetry, Literature, Art
generally, MTlwraith was totally unimpressed.
These were things you couldn’t add up—plainly
they had no concern with the real business of life.
Porridge makes good brawn and brain; but pota-
toes better stimulate the imagination. Potato-
yeast is a fine ferment.
* * • 9
Literature, however, has a prosy, practical side
called the Press ; and MTlwraith was thoroughly
alive to the business and political uses of the
Press. Newspaper-editors, he found, made excel-
lent tools to work his ends with. And if MTl-
wraith could not appreciate literature, he had
the Scotchman’s reverence for anything that
savours of learning. When that fevered slip of
feu ins, Francis Adams, went to Queensland,
I ‘llwraith became his host and friend; and
Adams repaid him with discriminating eulogy
and advocacy all the more valuable that it was
less discriminating. Doubtless MTlwraith was
hospitable without an eye to results; but doubt-
less he understood and valued the results.
When he invented the political expedient of
“The Australian National Party” as a move in
the game against Griffith, he would have been
quite content that his edifice should be of durable
stone. As that proved inconvenient, he was quite
content that it should be a house of cards. He
preferred to work with good men and good mea-
sures, if that were equally advantageous; but
men and measures were counters in his game.
The essential thing was that he should
win the game, with whatever counters. And he
made his mark on Queensland. His strength
did _ not lie in foresight; but often his
instinct amounted to foresight. His illogical in-
tuitions were sometimes better than logical argu-
ments. His was not an acute mind ; but the sheer
strength of it was superior to acumen. By dint
of heat, his brain shed light. He had a tight
grip on the heavy handful of things he knew,
and in the surety of his knowledge be seemed to
reach conclusions without ratiocination. When
he said, “This will do” or “This will never do,”
he could rarely say why to a colleague’s complete
satisfaction; nevertheless, he was right nine
times out of ten. His Transcontinental Railway
was a statesmanlike idea, though he would have
turned it to personal profit. His annexation of
Papua showed at once his broad sense and
the decision with which he acted on its diotates.
While timid little politicians in N.S. Wales
still cower in the shadow of that social
poison-tree, a centralised department of Public
works, MTlwraith long ago gave local govern-
ment to all Queensland by one comprehensive
measure. When a Governor fresh from a Crown
colony attempted to usurp the Executive’s prero-
gative, MTlwraith fought him and almost
literally slew him. When the Bank of England
opposed obstacles to his finance, MTlwraith
damned the Bank of England in vigorous phrase-
ology ; and, had it been a Queensland institution,
he would have damned it in fact.
• * * * *
In his prime, MTlwraith never met his politi-
cal master, though a revolt of his colleagues com-
bined with his own ill-health to end his rule in
1888. He dominated his directors, his Cabinets,
everybody. Even among his friends there
was a tacit admission that, if there came
an encounter, the friends would give way.
With him it was not Aut Ccesar aut nullus,
for MTlwraith never admitted the alternative of
nullus. He was Caesar. Cjssar out of office,
maybe; C.esar under a cloud ; but always Caisar.
A jollv, good-natured C.esar when things went
well; he had the gift of bonhomie so useful to a
Eublic man; but his square jaw could less easily
e shifted than the gates of Gaza, and his brow
was heavy with a nimbus of potential lightnings.
That is a figurative way of saying that MTlwraith
was a brutal, coarse, strong, greedy, aggressive
man, sticking to the Main Chance like a bull-
dog. The see-saw contest between Griffith
and MTlwraith was one of the most in-
teresting things in Australian political history.
Griffith was as much M‘llwraith’s superior in
intellect as he was his inferior in force of character.
MTlwraith had brains enough to see his objec-
tive and to go straight to it with a “ Faugh-a-
Ballagh ! ” Griffith had so much brains to spare
that he saw a dozen objectives, and while he was
wavering over the momentous Which? MTlwraith
was at the goal. Sometimes it was the wrong
goal; but more often it was the goal which
Griffith himself would have found—after many
days of mental tergiversation. And Griffith
had ideals to realise, scruples to satisfy ; MTl-
wraith had few scruples, no ideal but the ideal of
Success. The “coalition’’ of two such men
was one of the quaintest sacrifices to
the goddess Expediency which this genera-
tion has seen. The conflict between them
was strangely figured in a championship
contest in Sydney some time ago between two
chess players, Jacobsen and Jonas. Jacobsen, a
short, stout, large-headed, thick-necked Napoleon,
a type of impassive Power, sat back in his chair
smoking a pipe, and moved a majestic finger.
Jonas, Bright, thin and nervous, lit cigarettes and
was never still, wrinkling his face and jerking his
limbs in the anxiety of his effort. His play was
the more brilliant, and the more erratic; he
gained, he was winning, then his fire flickered,
and the solid, inscrutable Force in the. opposite
chair wore him down. So, where Griffith made
a dozen sharp thrusts in their political duel,
MTlwraith made one, which was not sharp, but
deadly.
» * • *
Yet Griffith for a time succeeded; and
partly to end his success MTlwraith deliber-
ately did his best to demoralise Queensland’s
political conscience. The man of the eighties
seemed to have lost every vestige of principle
possessed by the leader of the seventies. With
the later proceeds of the famous ten-million
loan he laid the foundation of a gigantic scheme of
bribery and corruption. The Q.N. Bank was to
rule Queensland, and MTlwraith and his asso-
ciates were to rule the Q.N. Bank. So, into that
institution which MTlwraith appeared to con-
trol absolutely, Govt, money was poured by
millions, to be lent in advances and overdrafts to
almost anybody who could wield political influ-
ence for MTlwraith. . MTlwraith himself
obtained advances of hundreds of thou-
sands for himself or his enterprises from
the money borrowed on the province’s credit.
The overdrafting Treasurer traded on the tax-
payers—or on the Bank’s depositors and share-
holders if the Bank ever settles up—just as much
as if he had opened the Treasury door, or the
Bank door, and taken the sovereigns out of
the safe. He gave insufficient securities or
none ; and his vast debts to the bank will never be
repaid. When exposure followed the crash of
1893 MTlwraith discreetly became, in the
euphemistic language of his friends, “a gentle-
man who is away,” and remained so. Broken in
health and fortune, he has spent the last seven
years in trying to rehabilitate the one. piece toge-
ther the remnants of the other. And now he is
dead.
* * • *
But MTlwraith’s deeds live after him.
His canker of rotten finance and unprincipled
legislation lias spread through the whole of the
body politic. Twenty years ago, in Queensland,
it was possible to turn elections by arguing to
truth, to honesty, to constitutional principle.
Queensland general elections nowadays are con-
trolled mainly by the self-interest of voters who have
been or may be purchased—who ask of any man
or any measure, “ What can I make out of Idm ?
What can I get out of it ? ” To this degradation
many have contributed, but MTlwhaith chiefly.
It should be written for his epitaph that, This was
the man who, with great abilities and noble
opportunities, did more than any other to corrupt
the political life of his adopted country.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
The Adelaide Tramway Job.
A pew years ago Kingston included State owner-
ship of the Adelaide tramways in his Ministerial
policy, and Holder, who took over the King-
stonian mantle when Sudden Solomon’s brief
candle blew out, has now a magnificent oppor-
tunity to dish the numerous schemers who are
trying to secure this fine asset. Holder pro-
mised to announce the Govt, policy as regards
this matter on July 10, but postponed the matter
to give the “ local bodies ” interested “ an oppor-
tunity of agreeing upon a scheme.” He is
still waiting for that agreement, though the subur-
ban municipalities and the Adelaide corporation
are at loggerheads and show no more signs of
agreeing with each other than the Pope does with
the boss Presbyterian. The city corporation made
a secret agreement with one Bingham, handing
over its tramway rights to a London syndicate for
40 years in return for a certain small percentage
on profits, and it declined to have anything to do
with any other scheme, being, as it says, pledged
to Bingham. It is now vaguely understood that
Bingham has fallen through, and that the Ade-
laide Corporation keeps on declining out of mere
cussedness. The suburban municipalities have
adopted what they call a Tramways Trust
scheme, and they propose that £400,000 be
borrowed with which to purchase the
existing lines, and that on completion of
such purchase the line's be leased to a company
for 40 years, said co. to provide electric traction
within two years. They suggest that that
awful corporation of sweaters, the Melbourne
Tramway Co., be invited to take over the lease
above mentioned ! They suggest this because
they are satisfied that a Municipal Trust cannot
make the lines pay, which means either that they
never heard of the experience of Glasgow and
other'places, or else they, are convinced of their
own lack of intellectual capacity. They are
understood to be against State ownership for the
same reasons; the fact that Sydney’s State-owned
and State-managed tramways pay handsomely is
evidently unknown to them. Why Holder can
hesitate for a moment to bring about State control
is a mystery. He was a member of the Kingston
Ministry which proposed it, and of Holder’s
present colleagues, Jenkins and Gordon were
colleagues with Kingston then. Moreover, Rail-
ways Commissioner Pendleton, only last month,
reported that—
The purchase of the tramways and their conversion to
an electrical system is a work which may well be under-
taken by Govt., and the result would not only be profit-
able to the State, but more satisfactory to the travelling
public than if the purchase and conversion were carried
out by individuals or syndicates whose possible first desire
would be the transfer of their conversions.
S.A. Government now owns every line of
railway in the province; a year ago it bought
the private line to Glenelg to complete the
policy of State control; and yet the
Adelaide and suburban municipalities, which
strongly oppose State control of tramways,
want to barter away their privileges to a
foreign company. S. A. Register, which
barracks loudly for the syndicate, advocates
allowing “ the pioneering and the possibly
risky experimentalising” to be done by private
capitalists and opposes the “needless intrusion of
the National Government into the arena .... an
innovation which, once permitted, would lead to
harassing and costly confusion,”. But this
ridiculous argument is no more applicable to.
tramway ownership than to that of railways,
telegraphs, telephones and waterworks. Think
of the antediluvian intellect of a paper which thinks.
an electric tram in a city of 150,000 population is a,
risky experiment and a reckless fooling with the
unknown and unexpected ! With a Greater
Adelaide in existence, municipal control of tram-
ways _ would be possible and politic, but a Trust is.
practically impossible with the fifteen or twenty
local bodies now existing and quarrelling within a .
ten-mile radius of Adelaide, and the only alterna-
tive to State control is ownership by a private'
company. The State can raise the money more:
cheaply, therefore it can carry the people:
more cheaply; it has the engineering staff in
existence; and it won’t load the trams with
needless expenses for boodle and first-robbers’
profits. When the municipalities, provided they
can agree, propose to immediately hand over the
great tramway property to a syndicate for 40
years—well, that isn’t the kind of municipal
ownership that is wanted.
The Battle in Boerland.
A multitude in Australia have labored under the
impression that the war in S’Africa was waged
against the Boers. It transpires, at this period,
on the authority of Jingo journalism, that the
valor of the British and the dash of the Australian
Bushmen were requisitioned rather against certain
of the Uitlanders—the white miners and the
skilled artisans. For instance the following is
extracted from the editorial columns of Sydney
Evening News :
Upon returning to the subject of Johannesburg, which
is the uppermost one in connection with South Africa to-
day, one finds that the chief town of the Rand may have
troubles peculiar to itself, as well as the general difficulty
of settling down after the war, which will affect all South
Africa. A correspondent of the Pall Mall Gazette has
written repeated warnings as to one great danger hanging
over the people of Johannesburg, not from Boers but from
others. Says this writer in his latest communication :
“Speaking of the capitalist, one danger may stare
us in the face in the new situation, and that is the com-
mercial and labour conditions that will probably be
created in Johannesburg. You know the history of Kim-
berley. It was once densely populated, and there was
plenty for everybody. The town was a small Johannes-
burg in some respects, until the amalgamation scheme
was instituted and carried into effect, and then the
money-making period came to a close. A ring fence was
drawn around the town, and to-day one only exists, trades
in the town being practically at the mercy and with the
permission of the De Beers Company. It is expected that
Johannesburg will be similarly treated. It is believed
that the mines will amalgamate and the staffs will be re-
duced—l mean the white staffs of engineers and general
workers. That will be blow number one. Then the
amalgamated companies may import all the necessaries for
their “ boys,” who number many thousands ; and the mo-
ment that is done, the food stores, the outfitters, and all the
other traders will begin to collapse ; the hundreds of staffs
employed will be thrown out; and there will be bank-
ruptcy and ruin. Johannesburg will become another
Kimberley then. This is a contingency that the best
men anticipate, and if it arises I predict that within 12
months you wfll have riots that will develop into a civil
war. The working man considers that he has made


Johannesburg, and he will not tamely submit to a state
of affairs that will drive him out of it.” This is a branch
of the question that should be considered b3' those think-
inff of going to South Africa, though it may be presumed
that the Imperial Government would interpose to prevent
any wholesale impoverishment of the working people of
Johannesburg.
After all, the chief difference between the
“disloyal” Bulletin and its Jingo contemporary
is that the former said this sort of thing early and
often, and the latter says it late.
The Buzzing of the Plies.
The Pastoralists’ Union of N.S.W. and other
weighty bodies of great beam and tonnage have
gathered themselves together to complain that
Attorney-General Wise’s Conciliation and Arbi-
tration Bill is dangerous and wrong. The follow-
ing, which sounds like the buzzing of many flies
on a hot day, was a recent deliverance on the sub-
ject by the chairman of the Pastoralists. It
should be read in a dead monotone, without stops,
to get the full flavor:—
They were also threatened with a legalised minimum-
wage, the tendency of which would be to throw the aged
and. less able-bodied out of employment, for where a
minimum-wage was fixed employers would only engage
those from whom they could get the largest return, and
the older and weaker laborer would be passed aside and
become a burden to the State or his friends. If there
were any evils or grievances which the worker wished to
have rectified, the remedy would not be found in this
class of legislation, nor would his condition be ulti-
mately improved by it. Better far for Parliament to
seek to establish confidence in the investment of capital
and the development of industrial enterprise by securing
to every man the right to offer his labor without interfer-
ence by anyone where it was needed. It might be argued
that such an open market for the labor of the working
mm would tend to reduce wages, but that by no means
followed. On the other hand, they should expect to see
a necessary and consequent demand for labor, with in-
creasing wages, and general prosperity in accordance
with the progress of natural laws, especially in a young
country, with its great natural resources and its labor
supply as yet unthreatened by the competition of over-
crowded populations
And all these dronesome old platitudes have buzzed
laboriously on the same window-pane for a
century and got no further forward. They
are like the voices of a dead Genesis
and Exodus crying in a petrified wilderness.
On any wet, dark, windy night, among the
tombs of any cemetery where rich men lie buried,
you can hear the ghosts mechanically wailing to
each other among the lies and the inscriptions
about “ establishing confidence in the investment
of capital,” and the “ development of industrial
enterprise,” and “this country’s great natural
resources.” If you find a fat Tory asleep any-
where after dinner, and hit him suddenly in the
lower chest, you can knock these three dry groans
out of him at once, because he is full of them by
force of habit, and the things that come out of a
man are naturally those that are in him. They
are the cry of an automatic moaning-machine that
is prepared to grieve at a street corner whenever
you put a penny in the slot. Also they are the
Tory’s remedy for everything under the sun efr
elsewhere. He has tried them for about a cen-
tury, and wages grew lower and prospects blacker
and debt larger, but the old infallible remedy
changeth not. It is a shibboleth and an abraca-
dabra. Half the elderly affluent gentlemen who
were tight on Mafeking night were found speaking
severely to a lamp-post' or a horse-trough about
the necessity for establishing confidence in the
investment of capital, or warning a letter-box not
to put its trust in agitators, or to aim at a mini-
mum wage, but to rely on the development of the
resources of this great country.
“The Bulletin” Knows a Bank.
The appeal of Darius Hunt, of Toowoomba (Q.),
to those shareholders who were beguiled, like
himself, by disgracefully unreliable balance-sheets,
to invest their money in the bank of MTlwraith’s
many overdrafts just before it went into revela-
tions, recalls a rush of old memories. Darius
Hunt urges his fellow-sufferers to join him in
another effort to prosecute the heartless, soulless
gang which ruined so many Queenslanders and
others. The shares of the Queensland National
Bank were worth nearly £l2 each as late as 1890.
They are worth nothing now; in fact, they are
fairly worth less than nothing, for the whole paid-up
and callable capital isn’t nearly sufficient to cover
losses. They were kept up for years by reckoning
bad debts as good assets ; by reckoning as income
the irrecoverable interest charged on paper upon
irrecoverable overdrafts; through the aid of
the Queensland Government which floated a
big loan and deposited the _ money in _ the
Q.N. Bank, where most of it is now in a
reconstructed state, by which loan the Q.N. Bank
got some cash to go on with and was enabled to
stave off the hideous exposure a while longer ; and
by the management buying up the bank’s own
shares with the depositors’ money (the event
proved that it wasn’t with the shareholders’
money, for that was all lost) in order to keep up
prices. There was some kind of a trial, The
Bulletin vaguely remembers, for conspiracy, but
Chief Justice Griffith summed up so ably in
favor of the accused directors and others that they
were promptly acquitted. The case put to the
jury was, in effect, “ If these men conspired to
misrepresent the state of the bank, whom did
they conspire against: Was it against themselves,
or against the bank, or the shareholders or the
depositors ? If they didn’t conspire against any-
body there could be no conspiracy.” And it
appeared so obvious that the alleged conspiracy
was not, to any serious extent at least, against the
parties named (assuming the shareholders and
depositors to mean those who were in the bank
before it went wrong) and so much care was taken
not to mention the party really conspired against,
if there was any conspiracy, that the prosecution
ended right there. Of course the manage-
ment didn’t conspire against itself. Nor
against the old shareholders except in the
sense that it prevented them taking steps,
when the collapse first began, to get new
directors of a less hideously incompetent order
who might have prevented the disease growing
worse. Otherwise the old shareholders were
rather benefited than otherwise by having the aw-
ful state of things concealed from them for a time ;
the alleged conspiracy only meant that the man
who might otherwise have gone grey or mad in
’9l didn’t do so till ’95 or ’96, so he really gained
by it, in extra dividends and extra peace of mind.
As for the depositors who were already in the
bank when it went wrong, they were certainly in-
duced by false representations to stay in, but the
jury, decided that that was a mere trifle and didn’t
signify. The persons most distinctly conspired
against from the day when the first unreliable
balance-sheet came out were the public, and they
were hardly mentioned. Every unreliable
balance-sheet was a conspiracy, intentional or
otherwise, to make the outside public buy shares
or put deposits in the rotten bank. From the day
when the Q.N. Bank’s balance-sheets ceased to be
a true statement of the real value of its assets,
every person who bought shares or put his money
in deposit on the strength of these documents
was the victim of something which, if not legally
a conspiracy, certainly should be one. The old
shareholders got something back in dividends
before their scrip became only worth its weight at
the paper-mill or thereabouts. But the people,
aud they were many, who were beguiled into buy-
ing Q.N.B. shares just before the awful news
came out that the scrip was, and had long been,
valueless, had no such alleviation, and their griev-
ance was so vast and outrageous that any decently
civilised country would have tried to do them
some kind of justice. Daiiius Hunt, of Too-
woomba, wants to make another effort at obtain-
ing redress. The Bulletin wouldn’t give much
for Dahius Hunt’s chance.

The Thin End of the Wedge.
New South Wales in general is assured by Premier
Lyne that he is a cordial advocate of the proposal
to put all the 41 municipalities of Sydney under
one municipal government —a decent municipal
government elected by the people, and with such
extensive powers that it can do some really good
work for the unkempt metropolis. His little
patchwork measure for the partial repair of the
municipal state of Sydney itself—one municipality
out of 41—is to be merely regarded as an instal-
ment. In view of the evidence the statement
appears incredible. If Premier Lyne intends to
establish a Greater Sydney Council at any reason-
ably adjacent date, then his partially-repaired
Sydney City Council will be abolished before it
has time to do any good worth mentioning. If he
intends his new City Council to do any serious
food as a municipal governing body, then
e will have to leave it in existence long
enough to give it a fair trial, and that
means indefinitely postponing the establish-
ment of the Greater Sydney Council which is to
replace it. If the idea is to create a body intelli-
gent enough to draw up some useful scheme of
consolidation, then any ordinary individual would
imagine that Mr. Lyne would apply his present
reform to all the 41 councils of Greater Sydney.
Supposing it is intended to leave to the councils
the work of drafting a fair, progressive, and work-
able scheme of consolidation, it is necessary to get,
41 councils with sufficient progressive spirit to see
the need for such a work, and sufficient intelli-
gence to carry it out; and to reform one council
while leaving the other 40 in all their original
narrowness, conservatism, parochialism, bigotry,
and billingsgate brings the reform no nearer. The
measure is in itself an improvement on present
municipal methods, yet its benefits are so care-
fully restricted to one out of the 41 Sydney muni-
cipalities that it would appear that the long, sad-
faced man who runs the affairs of N.S.W. in so
conscientiously wooden a fashion has a bitter per-
sonal grievance against the other 40, as well as
against the country towns in general. His apolo-
gists claim that William is only acting cautiously
and “ getting in the thin end of the wedge,” but
the man who gets in the thin end of the wedge,
and then leaves it at that for some years before
proceeding further, is apt to be so long in getting
the log split that an enraged employer gives him
the sack.

The Underpaid Politician.
South Australian politician Caldwell lately
raised his voice to declare that there should be no
reduction of the salaries of State legislators after
Federation. A reduction in their number, he
said, was justifiable enough, but a reduction in
their wages was an absurdity. The Bulletin
goes further even than this. It believes that the
number of State legislators should be cut down
about one-half, either by reducing the membership
of both Houses 50 per cent, or (if this would make
them ridiculously small) by abolishing the Upper
Houses, and cutting down the Lower ones by
about 25 per cent. And it would increase the
salaries by 50 per cent. Even allowing for the
diminished importance of the State Legislatures
under Federation, the salaries now paid are
absurdly inadequate. After all the purely Fede-
ral work has been transferred to the Commonwealth
Parliament, a local Legislature, even in a small
province, will still have incomparably more busi-
ness to look after than six of the biggest financial
institutions of Australia put together. And it is
business, too, of a vastly more important kind.
Victoria is a small State, yet its State Legislature
will still have to take care of about £70,000,000
worth of assets (railways, public lands and other
kinds), to look after the health and morals of
1,200,000 people, and to administer, probably, an
income of about £4,000,000. A private institution
of far, very far, less than this size would think
itself lucky if it got a good chief manager to do its
business in consideration of getting a life billet at
£SOOO a year; the state expects to get a man to
do its work well at £IO4O a year, with the proviso
that he may be sacked at a moment’s notice,
without compensation, and with the general
understanding that he can only hope to hold the .
billet five or six years at most, after spending the
best part of a lifetime in climbing up to
it. As for its board of directors (which is
Parliament), after paying their inevitable election
expenses they are expected to give practically all
their time and a mature judgment to their huge
task for the wages of a junior clerk; and the
wages are far more precarious than a junior
clerk’s, for he has at least a reasonable certainty
that his billet will be continuous. So members
are mostly divided into five classes : (1) Old men
who have made their pile and give the fg ( g end of
a failing intellect to the country ; (2) Men who
make up the deficiency of salary by the profitable
grinding of axes ; (3) Professional men whose
business lies in the capital and who legislate in
their spare time because it is a cheap way of
advertising their business; (4) Failures who are
flad to get the miserable junior clerk’s pittance
ecause they are worth even less than that out-
side ; (5) A small—very small —crowd of patriots,
misguided or" otherwise, who make the sacrifice
for the good of their ungrateful country. And
however badly the public is served in the worst-
governed State in Australasia it still gets quite as
good a Government as it pays for. Doubtless the
bulk of existing politicians are not worth more
than they get —but the country can’t reasonably
expect to get men who are worth more till it pays
more.

White Man and Chow.'
“ Yang Anyut ” on matters Chinese, as regards
the present far Eastern imbroglio :
How stands the white man in the eyes of an impartial
judge—could one be found ? Let us look back and ask
ourselves : Can the Greatest Empire pride herself on the
result of the war of ’49, which added Hongkong—now
second greatest port in the world—to her possessions ?
When, up to 1860, did Britain do aught to advance that
education and civilisation which, though it would
enlighten the East, would bring no grist to the Empire’s
mill ?
When the bankruptcy of India was threatened by the
exclusion from China of Hindoo-grown opium ; when
ruin stared the Canton and Mincing Lane merchant in
the face—then Britain arose and smote, and India’s
finances were saved. Mincing Lane was propitiated ;
Canton merchants were indemnified for losses they never
suffered ; and Ilong Kong, “ Island of Sweet Waters,”
came under Britain’s sway. When the civil war came—
the people against the corrupt government—on whose
side was Britain ? When poor fanatical Gordon was sent
to China, the emissary of the “ freest country in the
world ” was to use his sword in defence of a corrupt Em-
?eror and a corrupter court. And in the end, what of it ?
he invincible army—the “ Ever Victorious ” —blindly
following “ the man who knew no fear,” triumphed, the
Nine Wangs were beheaded, and the Soochow streets ran
blood till it was dried up by fire. Europe, outside of
Russia, had not then awakened to the “ magnificent pos-
sibilities ”of Cathay. All the while, trading concessions
were being obtained, and foreigners waxed rich—unpro-
tected and unafraid. Then came the “ Gawspel,” and
with it the seeds of mischief.
France went to war with China in ’B2, and a year later
retired with a little land and less honor. In ’95, after Japan
had pluckily tackled and overcome her huge neighbor,
Britain stood by and passively permitted Russia to grab the
spoils ; but now, in her hour of need, Britain calls loudly
on friend Japan to help against the corrupt Chinese Govt,
which she (Britain) has not only countenanced for years,
but has supported with blood and treasure.
Another lesson on the urgent necessity of a Crown
Defender, of equal status with the Crown Prose-
cutor, is furnished by the case of Isabella Dow-
ney, put on trial in Melbourne last week, for the
fourth time, for murder. The paltry sum allowed
by the State for defence purposes (in capital
charges only) was insufficient to fee the barrister
who had thrice previously defended her. The
heavy cost of the three trials had left her desti-
tute. Sue couldn’t supplement the Crown’s
miserable dole out of her own means,
and as her previous barrister wouldn’t take the
ease at the Crown’s starvation figure, she asked
for an adjournment that she might hunt up the
best legal light obtainable at the price. The
beautiful justice of such trials, not in Victoria
alone, but all over Australasia, is apparent
when it is considered that on one side are
the best lawyers obtainable, backed by the
Crown Law Department, the detectives and
police, and, on the other, a penniless woman,
forced to battle for her life as best she can with
only money enough to pay the fee usually charged
by an obscure barrister, and practically nothing
for the hunting-up of the most necessary evidence.
It may happen to any man that he shall have to
defend himself against a charge of which he may
be quite innocent, and perhaps beggar himself in
doing so. Yet it is quite as much the duty of the
Crown to defend as to prosecute, and, in prose-
cuting on its present lines, it only lays itself open
to the charge that it holds every accused person
guilty until he proves himself innocent, and, if he
hasn't the means, or doesn’t know how to go about
it, or is friendless and unknown, so much the
worse for him.

A curious struggle has set in for possession of the
first Governor-General. The Constitution pro-
vides that the permanent seat of Parliament,
Government, and Governor-General is to be in
N.S.W., over 100 miles from Sydney, but that,
till the capital is selected, the Federal Parliament
shall sit in Melbourne. N.S.W. Premier Lyne
claims that, though the Parliament is to meet
temporarily in Melbourne, the clause doesn’t
authorise the Gov.-General to reside temporarily
there, and that he can’t legally perform any
executive act outside N.S.W. Therefore Lyne
wants to house the Gov.-General at Sydney till
the capital is decided on. Victorian Premier
M'Lean replies that, by Lyne’s own reasoning,
the Gov.-General can’t reside in Sydney, for Syd-
ney is barred from being the Federal capital as
much as Melbourne is; but that Lyne, by
his own logic, may put his Excellency up
at Boppy Mountain, or any other place
in N.S.W. outside the 100-mile radius till the
permanent capital is decided on. Meanwhile it
would be interesting to know whence comes the
theory that, because the permanent capital is to
be in N.S.W., all executive acts must be per-
formed in N.S.W. to be legal. For if that is so
then it follows, with equal logic, that all executive
acts, to be legal, must be performed in that
particular part of NsS.W. which is to be the
capital, and as the site of the capital isn’t decided
yet, that is impossible. Therefore no executive
act whatever can be legally done, for they must
all be done at the capital and even the executive
act of fixing the capital being null and void until
after the capital is fixed, everything stops short.
What is the ground-work of the contention that
no executive act is legal unless done in the
province which contains the capital? Are all
British executive acts illegal if they are done out-
side of London, for instance ? And is his Ex. of
such surpassing value that it is worth while
clawing for possession of him in this absurd
fashion?
Kingston’s friend Dashwood, Judge and Govt.
Resident in the Northern Territory of S.A., told
a Parliamentary Select Committee, when in Ade-
laide lately, that blacks had been “shotdown like
crows in the Territory”; and Grainger, M.P.
(Dashwood, to repeat, being Kingston’s friend)
demanded that the Govt, should “call upon Mr.
Dashwood to prove his assertion, or take the
necessary steps to redeem the honor of South Aus-
tralia. ” Judge Dashwood has now sent down his
report; and ‘ ‘ the honor of South Australia ”is still
in need of redemption. The Victoria River
natives had been troublesome; but, says Dash-
wood :
I said it was notorious that they had been shot like
crows by diggers travelling from Queensland to Kimberley,
and that on a great many stations where the blacks
speared cattle they were shot by pastoralists and their
stockmen, and that probably this treatment had had the
effect of making the natives treacherous and vindictive.
And he sticks to what he says. In fact, he adds :
I also said that, in connection with the Daly River
murders, organised parties went out to shoot the natives
right and left, whether they had committed the offence
or not, and that a tribe had been cruelly murdered by
whites.
And he sticks to that, too. Of many police and
other reports quoted in support, this, an official
report written by a mounted-constable, is worthy
of a collector of whiskers from dead Boers:—
What the other parties had done I do not know, but I
believe the natives' have received such a lesson this time
as will exercise a salutary effect over the survivors in the
time to come. One result of the expedition has been to
convince me of the superiority of the Martini-Henry rifle
both for accuracy of aim and quickness of action.

Brisbane Telegraph, a lone, lorn, persistent
weeper over the grave of Parochialism, bids its
country a sad farewell in a leader entitled “ Our
Final Financial Year”—the final one under the
Parochial system, that is. And it writes this
epitaph :
We have had sole control of our own affairs to date, for
more than 40 years ; and have made a very good thing of
it. The legend is that we commenced business with 7s.
6d. in the Treasury. We leave off with a year’s revenue
set down at £4,588,206. If Federation is to do better
than that for us, by all means let us have Federation.
But we feel as if we were being hoodwinked. We feel as
any man would on his having had £lOO of his hard-earned
money, all his savings, violently taken from him by a
bully who says that he has no intention of robbing his
victim, that he takes the money because he can use it
better for its simple-minded owner than he can do for
himself.
The Bulletin urges.this pessimistic paper not to
cry, for the loss isn’t so great as it looks. Foyty
years ago Queensland had 7s. 6d. in its treasury—
a clear balance of 7s. Gd., and no debts, and prac-
fcically all its public lands yet to sell. Now it
hasn’t 7s. 6d. clear. It has sold the best of its
lands and has a debt of about £35,000,000, and the
publio works it has to show for the money are of
so little value as compared with the debt that
in 1899 they only paid £689,436 towards
the interest bill, which was £1,274,244. The
other £584,808 had to be made up by taxation.
So nearly half the debt isn’t represented by pay-
able assets, and Queensland, which was 7s 6d to
the good 40 years ago, is now £15,000,000 or
£16,000,000 to the bad, in addition to having suc-
cessfully disencumbered itself of many of its best
land assets. If the system which produced these
results is going, any sensible paper would be as glad
as if a plague-case was being shifted off the premises,
or a violent maniac who had been in the house a
long time was seen leaving by the front door
with the fixed expression of a lunatic who didn’t
mean to return.
The question of a daily Hansard is “to receive
consideration ” from N.S. W. Govt. If the daily
Hansard is simply to report the froth of dull
members talking at length to their constituents,
then it is not wanted. What is feasible and ad-
visable, however, is to cease advertising in the
ordinary newspapers (which now receive huge
indirect subsidies, mostly wasted, from the State),
and issue a Govt, newspaper—merging Hansard,
the Govt. Gazette, and half-a-dozen little depart-
mental publications into one. The cost of the new
venture would be saved from the present colossal
ad vt.-bill. TheGovT. Newspaper could bemadein-
teresting enough to secure a profitable circulation.
It would have a monopoly, in the first instance, of
Govt, announcements; and the public could be
taught to look to it for all Govt, information. As
things are now, much money is frittered away
annually, and you have to look in a dozen places
for Govt. news.
