“Another Pedagogue ” to The Bulletin: —
Concerning N.S.W. State teachers in schools above
their classification. lie “ Ted ” (B. 14/7/’oo>—a teacher
should not be kept in such a school to the detriment of
teachers holding schools much below their classification,
but it is only fair to give a teacher a chance to qualify
himself for his school, should it go above his classifica-
tion. This is done, I think, in nearly all cases ; but here’s
where the trouble comes in Many such teachers go for
exam., fail, and still retain their'schools. This sort of
thing is kept up year after year, the teacher thinking
that by going for exam, he is entitled to hold his school,
while the department, by keeping him in his school,
seems to say so too. Another item of dissatisfaction :
“ A,” a second-class teacher with 14 or 15 years’ good
service, is often in an Bth class school (£148), while “ B,”
a 8-A teacher with far less service, is very often in a 7th
class school (£l7l) —or sometimes in a 6th class, but only
drawing 7th class pay. Also, many schools have lately
been reduced in class, the teachers not being responsible
for a diminution in attendance, due to the wet
weather or to children leaving at 14. The Minister
recently said it was necessary to lower the class of
school in order to make teachers secure a better attend-
ance of pupils, which amounts simply to fining teachers
because the “ compulsory clauses ” are useless or inopera-
tive. If all teachers were treated alike there would not be
such cause for complaint, but it often happens that one
teacher’s school is reduced and another’s is not—-under
exactly similar circumstances ; which suggests favoritism.
Merit is scarcely recognised in the matter of appoint-
ments. Influence—Departmental influence especially—
does the trick. Witness the rush of teachers to the
“ Education office ” on Saturdays and during vacations,
to back-up their applications by personal appeal and
political influence. This kind of thing is obnoxious to
teachers in general, who, taking them as a whole, are
democratic in ideas—their big (forced) “ contribution ”
to the Jingo Fund notwithstanding. We hear a lot about
the heads of the Education Dept, being good organisers,
but the universal dissatisfaction existing tells another
story. This dissatisfaction will always exist until a better
system of emoluments is adopted, and the only system
likely to be popular is payment-by-classification, with due
consideration to seniority and results.
N.S.W. Parliament should rivet attention on the
Bill Premier Lyne is preparing to deal with the
Governor’s salary and the allowances of his staff.
The bringing down of such a Bill in such a hurry
—at all events, before time is given to planning a
reduction of M’s. P.—is token by many to mean
throwing dust in the public eye. The presence of
a Gov.-General will absolutely dwarf the State
official, and with the fact in evidence it is ques-
tionable whether the post should be valued at over
£lOOO a year. But at present view, if Premier
Lyne comes down with a salary of £5OOO, or £4OOO,
many will treat the reduction as substantial and
the Bill will pass. When the Federation starts,
and someone calls for a cut-down, the cry will be,
“ Oh, we arranged that six months ago, and must
Eve the law a trial.” Members should meet
yne’s Bill with searching criticism. The ques-
tion whether a separate imported State Governor,
“with his staff,” is really wanted should be
threshed out; and, if not imported, whether the
duties —mostly rubber-stamp ones—could not be
combined with those of some other official.
Thus Melbourne Argus
So well do the kanakas like plantation work that a
large proportion of them re-engage after their original
time has expired, or come back again, after a trip to their
native island, preferring civilisation to -savagery.
It is unfortunately true that a large number of
kanakas remain in Queensland after their time
has expired. Some re-engage, it is also true, but
a very large number do no such thing, hence the
frequency of the unspeakable “ walk-about” who
loafs half his time, puts in his leisure in Chinese
dens, arid renders the secluded roads and paths of
a district simply impassable to women and
children. It may be news to the Argus to hear
that certain Q. towns have recently petitioned
visiting Ministers to take steps towards compelling
Tommy Walkabout to do something besides turn-
ing decent streets into a Pandemonium under the
inspiration of Ah Cow’s surreptitious whisky.
Further, the kanakas who do re-engage don’t re-
engage by any means on the old terms. They
work, very often, at a rate only slightly lower
than that of the white laborer j they look down on
the simple “ boy ” just fresh from the islands; and
they cling to Queensland principally because they
are so addicted to European vices that the simple
existence on the islands would be quite intoler-
able to them. “Prefer civilisation to savagery”
do they ? _O, yes ! The civilisation of bad grog,
gaudy raiment, Jap. women, Saturday night
rioting, and sundry other garish and smellful con-
diments.
An interesting statement of the number of last
year’s convictions under the Drunkards and
Liquor Act has been laid on the table of
N.S.W. Leg. Assembly. Plain drunks and
drunks-cum-disorderly-conduct totalled 23,000 or
thereabouts j cases of Sunday-trading, also trading
after hours and selling without a licence, were
numerous enough, but the number of convictions
for sell ini/ adulterated liquor was one. Just think
of it l The Liquor Act of N.S.W. is so carelessly,
not to say corruptly, administered that of
all the dealers who vend wicked beer and

Town Hall Scherek’s Orchestra (Saturday
afternoon).
The “ Rose of Persia,” Hood and Sulli-
van’s new opera, which was staged at Her
Gracious Majesty’s Theatre, Sydney, on Saturday,
is the best novelty J. C. Williamson has produced
since a date so remote that this writer gives it up
as a bad job. It is a real comic opera ; one that
Gilbert might have written in his better days,
and with music such as Sullivan used to compose
regularly before he was blighted with a title and
had a handle sticking out of his name as if he was
a black frying-pan. It is even possible to read
the book at home and enjoy it, and the opera
which can stand being read by candle-light in a
boarding-house is a good opera and has a long life
before it. Its plot isn’t ponderous or elaborate.
Sultan Somebody’s wife grew weary of being
confined in the harem for the term of her natural
life, and in sheer cussedness she broke out, ac-
companied by three slave-girls, and went dis-
guised to look at the dogs in the street. And
being scared to death at the multiplicity of dogs
the party fled for refuge in at the door of Hassan,
a wealthy philanthropist with 25 wives, who
formed the Labor-party of that era, and enter-
tained beggars and deadbeats, and declined to go
into society because it shocked his democratic
instincts. Persian justice had just decided to
execute Hassan as an unusual person, and Hassan
got hideously drunk to avoid thinking about it.
And, being drunk, he conceived the idea that he
was the Persian monarch, and when Sultan Some-
body, in disguise, running after a pretty girl,
burst into the premises, Hassan, the democrat,
thought his monarch was a coal-heaver or some-
thing, andoffered, withanancientPersianhiccup, to
kick him all over his dominions. Wherefore Justice
sat down paralysed, for there weren’t trees enough
in Persia to sufficiently hang such a man upon, or
wells enough to drop him into as lie should be
dropped, considering the size of his sin, or water
enough to boil him properly ip. But the weary,
sated, much - grovelled -to ,monarch conceived
almost a liking for the only man who had ever
used bad language to him, and said he felt
refreshed. And Hassan woke up next morning
on the throne, with a swelled head and the crown
of Persia tottering over his ear, and was assured
on all hands that he was really the Sultan recover-
ing from a prolonged drunk which had affected
his memory, and for 15min. or so he ran the
empire with one damaged eye and a fearful craving
for drink in his mouth. After that period he was
dethroned, but by much hard lying his disrespect
to his monarch was explained. Also, the Sultana’s
conduct was untruthfully accounted for in so many
ways that the Sultan’s head began to go round
and he gave it up.
* • • •
The music of the “ Rose of Persia ” is ex-
cellent throughout, and almost every one of the
songs is more or less worth remembering. That
of Abdallah, the Persian clergyman, “When
Islam first arose, a tower upon a rock,” the
monarch’s plaint about the mixed state of society,
and Hassan’s ditty concerning one Thomas, a
small but cheerful street-Arabian who had no
fixed abode, but earned a precarious living by
running after the tram-car with the morning
paper, are perhaps better worth remembering than
the others, but even the worst composition of the
lot is above the average. The dialogue, too, is
bright, and the opera is full of good acting parts.
Browillow’s Sultan may be fairly classed with
his old Henri of Navarre. Bathurst, as the
Persian clergyman, gets about the best chance
he has had up to date, and proves fully worthy
of it. And Lauri, as Hassan, the philan-
thropist, who is a temporary monarch with a
swelled head, about reaches the high-water mark
of his career as a humorist. As a rich man with
disreputable clothes, low tastes, large feet, and 25
wives, Hassan is a circumstance of great mirth
from beginning to end. Miss Dorothy Vane, as
Hassan’s oldest and angriest wife, has a heavy
part and plays it not more heavily than .might be
expected. Kenningham awakes a brotherly feel-
ing in the heart of a pressman. He is a Persian
reporter, who goes round in brown clothes and a
wonderful tall nat, and narrates things that didn’t
happen for a consideration. As the earliest
sample of the press, he calls for respect, and he
plays the part in a way that does credit to the
profession, but the rush of the opera carries him
off his legs just as he is about to commence a story
which he admits to be unfit for publication, and
the story is unfortunately lost. Miss Winston
Weir lifts up a rather tired voice as a rather
wooden sultana, but considering Dorothy Vane,
winsome Carrie Moore, the ballet, and about £2OOO
worth of scenery, her comparative failure is of less
importance than it might be. “ Rose of Persia ”
is so big a success that it can stand one small
drawback.
** * *
At the risk of wearisome reiteration it is
again mentioned that a “ Rose of Persia ” matinee
takes place at Her Majesty’s to-morrow (Friday).
Proceeds go to the Sydney Mail Fund in aid of
the Children’s Hospital.
36 36 36 36
Alfred Dampier has revived the almost
forgotten “ Duke’s Motto ” at Sydney Criterion.
It is a play of the days of the Regent Orleans, and
is glorified with many titles and garnished with
much cocked-hat. It belongs to that period when
the nobility couldn’t get its proper sleep through
being out murdering other noblemen all night.
The duke’s motto is, “I am here” —which he
wasn’t, though his dead body was present. Dam-
pier carries off the duke’s infant when the duke is
killed in the first act, and the other duke forces
the first duke’s widow to marry him, and hunts
after Dampier and the infant for 15 years. And
when the second duke and his factotum and his
secretary and his bravo and his hunchback and his
hired murderer find Dampier, that gentleman
slays the hunchback, and comes forth two seconds
later in the hunchback’s green clothes and his
whiskers —which were evidently movable. And
as nobody knows the difference, Dampier takes
the hunchback’s place in the second duke’s house-
hold, and spends his time calling out suddenly, “I
am here”—which makes the second duke turn
pale and jump several feet in the air. It might be
supposed that the second duke would stop this
out of Dampier’s salary, but he doesn’t.
» • * •
Dampier, in the hunchback’s clothes and
still quite unsuspected, gradually undermines the
second duke, and by dint of calling out suddenly
round corners that he is here, ultimately restores
the lost girl to her mother —who hasn’t been out-
side the house for 15 years, and looks all the
younger for it. So the daughter, who was handed
out of a window on a dark night to a complete
stranger, who merely said he was there, which he
obviously was, for he couldn’t have said it if he
had been absent infants of noble birth were
habitually handed out of windows at that period—
comes back proudly by the door with the Incrimi-
nating Document in her hand, and re-enters into
her property. The document is her birth-certifi-
cate. In dramas, the fact of a lady haying a
birth-certificate or a dog-registry-receipt in her
hand proves conclusively that she is the person or
dog mentioned therein, and no questions
asked. The second duke is routed after a severe
engagement, and flies in great disorder, leaving
his ammunition behind. Alfred Rolfe rather
takes this paper’s fancy as the second duke.
He has a martial bearing and the black tuft
of a wicked life is on his chin. Alfred
Dampier takes the hero in a serious spirit, and
gives a fine rendering of a big, manly adventurer
who badly wants his hair cut on the Spanish
frontier. John Forde, Howard Smith, and Miss
Blanche Clements also deserve honorable mention
as persons who keep a wildly-humorous play on
its feet, and various nicely-clothed individuals and
some able and conscientious scenery are included
in the bill. The “ Duke’s Motto ”is a refreshing
drama to meet. People who are grey and weary
with the struggle to invent probable stories to
account for non-payment of the rent and other
things, find rest and soul-food in a dashing tale
that isn’t probable and doesn’t want to be.

The insulting Dutch gentleman who leaves
the Flag on the floor at Sydney Royal is still
engaged in his many nefarious projects—among
them the stealing of Baker’s infant and the arrest
of Miss Frances Ross on suspicion by a very brief
policeman. And the burglar who left his wife at
home with a black eye for he# only asset defeats
some of his projects, and states so proudly that it
is the duty of every soldier to defend a woman in
distress that his own battered missus drops quite
out of sight. The rest of the angry Boer’s schemes
are wrecked by Baker, and the Boer comes to a
bad end. The writer forgets the exact nature of
the end, but he fancies that the wicked Boer talks
himself to death on the veldt, or hangs himself
with a strip tom off a kopje.
36 36 36 36
The Anglo-American Bio-tableau is giving
quiet entertainments to moderate audiences at Syd-
ney Lyceum. The arrangement does not scintil-
late overmuch, but sometimes the picture gets up
and shakes itself and lies down again in a differ-
ent place, and on one occasion a horse appeared in
the middle and went out where it stood instead of
running down to the front and disappearing as a
very large horse making straight for the orchestra.
The want of perspective m Cinematographe
arrangements seems to point to a Chinese origin.
Between times Carden Wilson gave imitations of
well-known actors, some of whom are getting
shady in the public recollection. An outrage on
public feeling was committed by the programme
which said, “ Please turn over,” and when you
had complied, gave you no valuable advice about
Soap or Beans, or anything whatever.

The “ Only Genuine Biograph ” of the
Boer war is to open at Sydney Palace on Saturday.
The management swears that its pictures don’t
wobble, and also that it doesn’t descend to
the practice of having bogus battle scenes
photographed in a circus while supernumeraries
with false whiskers fall down for a small con-
sideration and some beer. In view of the hilarious
jokes contained in some-pictures recently inflicted
on Sydney’s suffering city, the writer has an in-
tention of getting up a genuine biograph of the
fight between David and Goliath, taken on the
spot by a Philistine photographer, but has post-
poned the thing for lack of time.

** * *
Sydney Tivoli seems gradually resolving
itself into a creche, where the infant phenomenon
is produced wholesale. The latest, Baby Parkes
to wit, is a clever, energetic youngster who flashes
a large, irresistible smile at the front stalls.
Unfortunately all the youngsters seem to know
only two songs, the one about the dolly and the
other about the coon, which coon the singer claims
to be herself. There is too much coon altogether
in Rickards’ show, seeing that the animal, how-
ever familiar in America, is absolutely unknown
to our baekblocks. What Rickards ought to do is
to give up his concert first part altogether, and
tack half the songs therein on a little playlet —the
call-boy could write it in his odd moments. It
need only be Australian and up-to-date, with
allusions to the bunyip and George Reid and other
local institutions.
** * *
Incidental to the big Nance O’Neil craze in
Melbourne came a matinee benefit performance of
“ Hedda Gabler ” last Friday. “ Hedda Gabler ”
is one of the least-unacted of Ibsen’s plays, and
the performance had sufficient interest of a dull
semi-cerulean sort to hold the attention of the
audience. The ingenuity of the stage-craft is
here very striking, even more so than in “The
Doll’s House,” which is saying much for “ Gabler.”
The dramatic effect made by the dropping of a
letter into the “Doll’s House” letter-box is re-
membered as an instance of the art that
wins a measure of toleration for Ibsen’s humor-
less “bluffs.” The Scandinavian pedant con-
structs all his plays cleverly, and, as a rule, he
brings in some interest at the front door. It is
strong interest as regards odour, but there doesn’t
seem to be much money in the game. Hard to
say whether good dramatists, such as Pinero, who
write to please an intelligent public, are not
actually better stage-craftsmen than Ibsen, the
pfoseur who presents bizarre humbug for the ap-
proval of a pretentious cult. Probably the chief
virtue in Ibsen’s work gets undue credit because it
(the virtue) stands almost alone. Nothing looks
so green as an oasis in a desert.
» » * *
Hedda Gabler is the young bride of a
simple-minded literary grafter who won a money
prize for an essay on Civilisation, and believes (like
Ibsen) that his little Scandinavian circle includes
the great bulk of the world rather than merely a
small sample of it. Hedda married her tame
student because she was curious, as Oscar Wilde
would say. She doesn’t care for him a little bit
when they come home after the honeymoon, bub
her distaste surely can’t be so glaringly appa-
rent as Nance O’Neil makes it, otherwise
her husband and her adopted relative would
not continue to call her a dear girl. The
Hedda whom Ibsen thought of must have been
a somewhat dissimulating cat. Before her marriage
she used to discuss forbidden things with a
bachelor friend, whom the unpleasant spinster
ultimately repulsed with a revolver simply because
she lacked courage to follow her desires. All this
is revealed in conversation after Hedda’s marriage,
the stage business at this point being very good-
much too good for the conversation. The gentle-
man (by name of Lovborg) has been converted
to teetotalism and is writing a book on
civilisation (they seem to be whales for
civilisation in Norway) under the influence of
another married woman, therefore Heddalures him
back to drink, partly to worry the other woman,
and partly because she (Hedda) wants to see the
man “ with vine leaves m his hair.” To cut short
a long story of small details, Hedda burns the
MSS. of the book on civilisation and tells Lovborg
to shoot bimself beautifully in the head, or breast,
seeing that he gets plain drunk when he ought to
have poetic vine leaves in his hair. Then Assessor
Brack (wbo also talks “blue” to the lady and is
more or less held in check by revolvers) brings a
confidential report to the effect that the other
fellow shot himself—un-Beautifully. Hedda,
after brooding over the un-Beautifulness, goes
behind a curtain and shoots herself Beautifully
through the temple. Her motives _ become
chaotic towards the finish, and her end is thank-
fully received.
* * * •
The Hedda of Nance O’Neil was Magda
over again—except that the massive lady scowled
stonily at the house more often than she turned
her back. No ; on second thoughts, Hedda was
more masculine than Magda. The she-devil pre-
sented by Nance couldn’t possibly impose upon a
husband and an aunt, or get the confidence of
another married woman. The rest of the per-
formance was mostly unimportant, the exceptions
being Plimmer, as the calm Assessor, and Carne
(effectively made-up), as the tame husband.
People laughed once or twice whilst Kingston was
aiming at tragic effect with his eyes and eye-
brows.

On Saturday the Nance O’Neil co. ap-
peared in “Peg Woffington” (nee “Masks and
Faces”) at Her Majesty’s. Charles Reade’s old
comedy has not been played in Melbourne since
Mrs. Bernard Beere shone at the Opera House.
The present scribe gazed awhile at Nance O’Neil
and co. on Saturday night, and he thought with
some regret of charming Mrs. Bernard Beere, and
Herbert Standing as Triplet, and the moderate-
sized crowds that cheered their brilliant perform-
ance.
*# * #
At Melbourne Royal the hollow woefulness
of ‘‘World Against Her” was duly revived on
Saturday when the wife who began to mourn at
8.30 saw the dark clouds passing away at about
11.10 p.m. Two things wanting to make this
melodrama quite like all the others are Blood and
Retribution. Nobody gets murdered, neither do
the sins of the prosperous schemer in the black
silk hat find him out at the finish. He isn’t even
damaged in his bank account. He lifts his
shiny token of wickedness — viz., his hat —to
women he has wronged and strolls off to
wallow in luxury as usual. There is no
sort of poetic justice in the fifth act of “World
Against Her,” but the clouds roll away in a long-
expected manner, and the fact that a girl who fell
from grace before the curtain went up has grown
too respectable to curse the author of her shame
is noted as a point in the drama’s favor. Miss
Eugenie Duggan plays the suffering wife with a
pronunciation peculiarly her own, and which
sounds very weird in this case because she has to
speak nearly half-a-mile of despondent dialogue
during the evening. When she suffers
inaudibly Miss Duggan suits the action to
the silence and is an altogether pleasing
victim. Inman, as the husband; Willard, the
highly-tailored villain; Bert Bailey and Dora
Mostyn, as artless humorists in the Punch-and-
Judy line; Miss Hilda Fraser, and a few other
people help to hold up the clouds that finally
move onward. Miss Ada Guildford is the estim-
able spinster who loses her self-respect without a
shudder, and recovers it cheerfully. In the first
act she menaces the villain with a bright-red sun-
shade until he promises to be less inconstant in
his sinfulness. Later on Miss G. leads a better
life in a blue-green costume of almost the same
shade as her repentance. The drama all the way
through is attentive to small matters of detail, and
twelve scenes do credit to the management.

*# # #
Paul Martinetti and his co-partners in
pantomime, all of whom improve on acquaintance
and seem well suited to their parts, will change
their programme at the Bijou next Saturday.
This is the last week of the furious quadrille, and
the wild, despairing somersault which lands Jack
Strop on the carcase of his mate. The clever
Ranger girls and one or two more “turns” that
look rather new again are back in the bill.
Also the four nice young Williams Sisters,
and especially the spontaneous little ’un, de-
serve a nod of approval for being such genuine
young sisters on the face of them that they almost
restore public faith in , the relationship of some
previous sisters at Rickards’ who didn’t look to
have had only one mother between them. Finally
The Bulletin desires to offer up thanks for the
disappearance of a recent infant “star,” now re-
placed by Alma Gray. The infant in question
was not the only recent attempt to rival Fannie
Powers, but she was the saddest, and the substi-
tution of Alma, now that Alma has ceased to lay
any claim to childhood, is a great comfort. A
blessing, in fact.

At Poverty Point.
The death of Orpheus M'Adoo sets one thinking of the
Fiske Jubilee Singers who came to Australia years ago on
the religious ticket. Opening in Melbourne Town Hall
under the management of wily Loudin (he invested the
boodle in a boot-factory) they were seriously taken for
emancipated slaves touring on behalf of a ’Murkan Negro
University, and that holy man, James Balfour, M.L.0.,
filled the chair at their first concert. Also Loudin went
around the crowded hall during the interval, selling the
beautiful story (bound in cloth) of the Fiske Jubilee
Singers, price 3s. Miss Mattie Allan was the central star
coquettish belle of the co., whilst handsome Mac, her
future husband, figured prominently among the young
men. And when smiling Mattie, with a bunch of roses
pinned to her stylish bodice, sang a delightful solo .you
could hear sad-faced women in the audience muttering,
“ Poor thing! born a slave 1 Uncle Tom’s Cabin!
Praise the Lord ! ”
Dear B.— Re “Poverty Point’’par. about Miss Nance
O’Neil’s reservation of “Hedda Gabler ” solely for the
Hospital-benefit matinee, would it not have been “ fair
dinkum” to have given the management credit for a
desire to secure one big house and assure a fat sum for
the charity? There are even in Melbourne sufficient
people interested in Ibsen to fill a house for one perform-
ance, whereas the theatre might run to emptiness in a
week. Surely The Bulletin, the enemy of stupid con-
ventions, might have had a good preliminary word for the
effort, quite apart from the charity claim, since it gave us
an opportunity for a taste of the dramatic quality of a
writer who, though airily dismissed as a perpetrator of
cranky plays by the sapient paragraphist, has made an
addition to European literature that has had a big influ-
ence on contemporary letters, and has won the profound
admiration of some of the brightest intellects of our time.
—Player.
Regina Nagel, whom The Bulletin has loved as an
adopted daughter ever since she sang “Ben Bolt” in
“ Trilby,” made her debCiton the London concert platform
t’other day, and the cable says she scored a success. Pro-
bably her next success will be a triumph. Miss Nagel
has been taken in hand by Melba, therefore for people
who know more about professional singing than meets
the ear, the debut of the new Australian contralto has
particular interest. Melba is not affectionately disposed
towards Ada Crossley, but Melba, the mighty, likes Nagel
and will use her influence to give Marchesi’s pupil a big
start. At present, Australia’s Ada Crossley ranks among
English-speaking contraltos next after Clara Butt. Butt
—6ft. high and with a voice in proportion—holds a very
strong position. Leaving the popular giantess out of the
question, it seems probable that Nagel will prove at least
equal to Crossley at all points. The latter is a true busi-
ness woman rather than a great artist, and she has done
her own advertising in a great measure, but if Nagel’s
beautiful voice “ comes off ” for all it is worth, Melba will
see that it gets the best kind of newspaper advt., and the
position of the singer will be assured.
Time flies. Clara Merivale, whom The Bulletin remem-
bers as a quite young and pretty prima-donna for the
Finn, had a daughter married in Melbourne the other
day to Mr. Bert Langford—lucky little Bert, of North
Mount Lyell. He was a clerk in Bowes Kelly’s office some
few years back and assisted in floating the Norths, for
which he accepted shares in the co. as his commission,
instead of “ the ready.” The mine turned out trumps,
and Bert rose to affluence. Also he achieved the notoriety
of being one of the executors appointed by the late Jim
Crotty to pay buxom widow Crotty £5OO a year if she
entered a nunnery.
“J.P.”: I want to know —Should an operatic tenor
spread the fingers of his caressing hand when hugging
plump and pleasing operatic actresses ? Should he not
hug with decorously closed fingers ?
Alfred Dampier announced in Sydney a “ reverential ”
production of “ The Merchant of Venice.” The uncon-
scious humorist is the humorist!
One of the several reasons for Harry Furniss’s failure as
a lecturer in Australia was his alleged imitation of an
Irish Member in the Commons. He forgot he wasn’t in
London, where there’s a big audience to laugh at the
stage-irishman. Of every big crowd in Australia Irish
people make a big portion. Harry’s third-rate mimicry
consequently fell flat. The Irish “number” is one in a
hundred atHome,” but here they’re “ plenty as black-
berries.” Furniss ought to have known this—or the
Much Travelled ought to have known it for him.

’WARE IMITATORS.
I GEORGE ELAKELAR, of Kalgoorlie, in
9 the colony of Western Australia, Cordial
Manufacturer, do hereby apologise for having
infringed the trade mark of the Zetz Spa Pro-
prietary, Limited, of Sydney, in the colony of
New South Wales, by selling a mineral water
bearing the name of “Zetz Spa” in labelled
bottles. I undertake not to continue selling water
labelled in any manner resembling the “ Zetz
Spa” trade mark. I have agreed to pay, and
have paid, the legal expenses incurred by the
Zetz Spa Proprietary, Limited, in connection
with the said infringement, and have authorised
the Manager of the Company to make use of this
apology as he may see fit.
Dated this sth day of July, 1900.

flotation of several important mining properties. Mine
owners who desire flotation are requested to furnish par-
ticulars at once.*

such as Queensland and Tom Coventry, Telegraph Cham-
bers, Queen-st., Brisbane, can supply. The deduction is
obvious.*

Commercial World, July Ist, 1899, writes of
the Colonial Mutual Life Assurance Society : “To simul-
taneously increase the business and reduce the ratio of
expenditure is therefore always regarded as a sure indica-
tion of capable and most efficient management.”*
The carelessness of certain steam-users in not
looking after their boilers and the results of neglecting to
clean them regularly when incrustation takes place has
often been pointed out in Tiie Bulletin. A disencrustat-
ing boiler compound known as “ Litofuge,” with a world-
wide reputation, is being placed upon the market by W.
and J. Lempriere, 506 Little Collins-st., Melbourne, who
upon application will be pleased to forward catalogues to
those interested. “Litofuge” compounds are powders
without soda, fatty matter or injurious chemicals, which
injure rather than improve boilers. Its action is mechani-
cal and its introduction into a boiler causes no foaming.*

Mr. Mephan Ferguson, Engineer, Ironfounder,
&c., of Melbourne, Adelaide, and Perth, reports that the
great demand for spiral riveted pipes has necessitated the
increase of manufacturing facilities at his Footscray
works, and he is now in a position to supply orders at
very short notice, for all sizes of pipes from six to twenty
inches in diameter, with either patent cast-iron bolted
joints, flanged joints or the ordinary spigot and faucet
lead joints. Mr. Ferguson also manufactures boilers,
pumps, fluming, patent Nelyambo earth-scoops or tank-
cleaners, rabbit exterminators, refrigerators, ice-making
machinery, bolts, nuts, forgings, and all sorts of iron-work.*

The Wild Cat Column.
The Commercial Banking Co., of Sydney, in
view of increased earnings for the half-year to 30th June,
1900, raises its dividend another point. Results for the
recent half-year and some previous ones are :

The Commercial Banking Co. of Sydney was a 25-per-
cent.-dividend-paying institution for 17 years previous to
the disastrous ’93. It is gradually climbing back towards
that old gorgeous situation, though whether there will
ever be 25 per cent, banking dividends in Australia again
only a prophet could tell. The earnings are equal to £5
Is. 3d. per cent, per annum on the shareholders’ funds
(capital, £1,000,000 ; reserve fund, £1,010,000).
In addition there is an undivided profit balance
of £20,152 ; the institution has paid away its
earnings so accurately in dividends that in six years it
has added exactly £5BlB to its accumulations out of pro-
fit. The Commercial Banking Co. grows larger each half-
year. It has now over £11,000,000 of deposits, and cash
and liquid securities amount to £5,197,820—equal to over
40 per cent, of all liabilities to the public. Of Government
securities alone it holds nearly £1,660,000 worth. Alto-
gether it may fairly claim to be, next to the 8.N.5.W.,
the boss financial institution of Australia, and in some
respects it even gets ahead of its big competitor.
* * * »
The City Bank of Sydney presents its usual
monotonous report for the half-year to 30th June, 1900 :
Dividend Added

£73,271 £72,000 £1271
The profits are equivalent to £3 4s. 3d. per cent, per
annum on the shareholders’ capital and reserve-fund.
According to the figures it is the worst half-year
that the City Bank has had for apparently 20 years
and probably somewhat longer —the figures which
the writer has by him at the moment only go back
20 years. But the drop in profits doesn’t necessarily
mean anything—at least anything more than usual. Some
time ago it was admitted that the City Bank was secretly
applying part of its earnings (amount unknown) to writ-
ing off an unspecified sum in the way of depreciation, and
the inference is that it is>still doing this. This admission
was equivalent to a statement that the balance-sheet did
not accurately represent the bank’s condition, but people
are used to that kind of thing in Australia, ahd take it in
a meek and humble spirit. The alleged and real assets
apparently differed by the amount which, in the board’s
opinion, had to be written down for depreciation—what-
ever that amount was. The shareholders funds were not
real intact funds, but minus an amount unspecified. The
profits as shown were not real profits, but only the
amount which the management thought fit to show after
setting aside some other amount, unspecified, to reduce
the depreciation or losses, also unspecified. As the
amount which the directors reckoned it necessary to pro-
vide is a mystery and the amount being provided is
another, the shareholders are not informed when the pro-
cess is likely to be completed. And up to now they have
taken this great lack of information about their own
affairs quite calmly. Most Australian shareholders do.
As compared with six months ago the City
Bank’s note circulation has increased by £579. Deposits
have diminished by rather over £<49,000. Cash in hand
has decreased by i. 9921, but still stands at £319,342, and,
reckoning also £25,000 of N.S.W. State securities, the
City Bank’s cash and liquid assets are equal to the very
solid figure of 3l£ per cent, on all liabilities to the public.
But though its shares have improved in price during the
half-year they are still valued by the market, in propor-
tion to the dividend paid, lower than those of any other
N.S.W. or Vic. dividend-paying bank. Last Saturday the
new issue were obtainable at prices which gave a return
of about £7 7s. per cent, on their cost. The public, so far
as can be guagedby these figures, takes a rather depressed
view of its Oitv Bank, and it seems questionable if an
absolutely frank statement of the position might not pay
better. Also what are balance-sheets for except to state
the position with absolute frankness !
The great improvement shown by the Commer-
cial Bank of Australia last half-year has not been main-
tained, the profits for the six months to 30th June, 1900,
being only £58,265. The doings of this bank since the last
reconstruction and since the establishment of the Special
Assets Trust Co. have been as follows :
The profits are equal to £3 12s. 9d. per cent, per annum
on the shareholders’ funds (capital, £3,202,323 ; reserve
fund, nil). Or, if it is assumed that the £30,000 trans-
ferred to the Special Assets Trust is required to make
good the deficiency ©n the Trust’s operations for the half-
year, then the profits are £28,265, equal to only a miser-
able £1 15s. 4d. per cent, per annum on shareholders’
funds. The management ascribes the fall in profits to
“ the reduction in the volume of export-business in the
first half of the year,” which is, perhaps, feasible enough.
At all events, the Commercial Bank of Australia is now
bound by the terms of its reconstruction to pay 3 per
cent, to preference-shareholders ; it seems also to find it
necessary or advisable to aid the Assets Trust with £30,000
each half-year ; and the last half-year’s earnings don t
quite cover these two items. There is a decline 1
deposits, but not a serious one the re c °nstrixctecl
posits of the Commercial Bank itself (as disti g _
from those of the Assets Trust) are now down to £965,4^,
there is a solid reserve of cash and liquid securities
amounting to £1,713,096 ; and, in a • ’
bank itself (as apart from the Assets Trust) looks reason
ably prosperous.
About the Assets Trust there is, as usual, prac-
tically no information. This Trust is as much a part or
the bank as the bank itself. Its deficiency,
one, must be made up by the bank, and if it P a very
large one it may even wipe out all the ordinary P •
If it is worse than a very large one it may, mpe out part
of the preference capital as well. If the income from
assets is £30,000 less for the half-year than the outgo in
expenses and interest on the Trust deposits, then
profits of the whole bank are only lf itsi mcome
is £40,000 less than outgoings, then the net profit ot tne
bank is only £18,265. In either of these two latter cases
the bank wouldn’t earn the amount it pays away in prefer-
ence dividend, and a big trouble is accumulating lor tne
shareholders and things are getting much worse.
Assets Trust pays its way or has a half-yearly deficit wfiicn
is less than the £30,000 contributed by the bank, tnen
part of this £30,000 is evidently available to write down
the assets and things are getting better. The directors
flatly refuse to tell the shareholders how one-third ot their
property, and that the part of the property about which
there is most doubt, is getting along. When they are
asked to state what are the income from the property,
and the outgoings, and the loss or profit, they generally
reply that they can’t give the value of the property—
which is just as relevant to the income question as if
they said they hadn’t got the plague. They give no
reason for the refusal of information —the statement that
they think it best to refuse isn’t a reason. Whether they
are saving up a great, beautiful glad surprise for the
shareholders in the shape of a big surplus which will re-
store a whole lot of the lost capital, or whether they are
waiting in hope that a merciful Providence may call them
hence before the dreadful shock is made public, the pub-
lished accounts give no indication. The Commercial
Bank of Australia’s Assets Trust is a co. with between
three and four millions of liabilities. It. is by itself a
bigger concern than either the Bank of Adelaide, Bank
of North Queensland, City Bank of Sydney, Colonial
Bank, Commercial of Tasmania, National of Maonland,
the two Royal Banks, or the Westralian Bank. And this
huge concern doesn’t even publish a full statement of
revenue and expenditure. Yet the whole question of
whether the ordinary shares of the Commercial Bank are
worth a lot of money, and have good dividends in view at
an early date, or are worth nothing, or less than nothing,
hangs on this problem. The value of the preference
shares largely hangs on to it also.
The board, however, condescends to tell each
half-year how much remain of the deposits for which the
Trust is liable. It is gathered from the published reports
that they have heen paid off at this rate :
i-vear to June, 1897, £27,907 £-year to June, 1899, £26,525
„ „ Dec., 1897, £41,949 „ „ Dec. 1899, £20,682
„ June, 1898, £45,474 ~ „ June, 1900, £12,715
„ „ Dec., 1898, £56,591
It appears, therefore, that out of a liability of £3,642,001
to its depositors the Assets Trust has only man-
aged to wipe off £231,843 in 3£ years, leaving
£3,410,158 still owing. This is pitiably slow progress.
And this is despite assistance from the bank to the extent
of £233,000. Up to December, 1897, the bank’s balance-
sheet showed that the Trust had money on deposit in the
bank—which might possibly be surplus earnings—but no
such entry has appeared since. The amount of deposits
paid-off each half-year is growing exceedingly small—
possibly because the Trust’s assets are'so bad that they
won’t sell, or possibly because they are so good that it is
worth holding them for a better market. In fact, the
Commercial Bank’s Assets Trust is one of the financial
mysteries of the age, and by reason of it the Commercial
Bank is a mystery also. Why the shareholders endure
this flat refusal on the part of their paid servants, the
directors, to tell them aboift the state of their own pro-
perty' passes comprehension. And what is the good of the
new Victorian banking law, which prescribes so rigidly
how banking accounts are to be presented, if a bank can
transfer a tenth or a quarter or a third or a half or three-
quarters of its business to a separate account, and give no
useful details at all about that ? Is the Commercial Bank s
method legal under the Victorian Act.?
The prosperous-looking Bank of Victoria, hav-
ino- n ow paid off all its reconstructed deposits, and having
just closed the most profitable half-year since 1894, has
resumed paying dividends to its ordinary shareholders.
The Victoria lost none of its capital in the struggles of
’93, but its reserves (£259,380) all disappeared, and it
started afresh. The following is a summary of its career
in the bad times that followed :

The Bank of Victoria’s profits are at the rate of
£3 12s sd. per cent, per annum on the shareholders’
capital and reserve fund. It has accumulated a new
reserve of £lOO,OOO, and the undivided profit balance of
£29 605 makes total reserves of £129,605. As already
mentioned, the reserves which disappeared in the great
financial convulsion were £259,380 ; so considering the
progress that has been made towards rebuilding the
reserve fund, and the fact that no capital was lost and
that the Bank of Victoria paid the fuIP4J per cent, on its
reconstructed deposits all through, it came out of the
trouble better than any of the other reconstructs, the
Commercial Banking Co. of Sydney alone excepted In
fact the reconstruction, though a loss to the old share-
holders in the way of dividends, was a handsome profit to
the depositors. They got 4>- per cent, on most of their
deposits for six years, during greater part of which
time money was only worth about 3£. The rest of their
deposits were turned into preference shares, which yielded
them 5 per cent, when money was only worth 3 J( and
which promise to keep on yielding 5 per cent, indefinitely.
This isn’t pleasant for the old shareholders to think of,
but it is too late to do anything save throw a, brick at
the forgotten J. B. Patterson Government, which might
have averted the trouble if it. had possessed the neces-
sary intelligence. At all events, things look well with the
Bank of Victoria for the present ; and if the directors set
themselves resolutely to build up a new reserve-fund of a
million or so, instead of the very inadequate i-million
which satisfied them in the old days when 12 per cent,
dividends were of more account than solidity, it should
have a great future before it. f

ness in Sydney and next to none in Adelaide, all on
account of London market. Saturday was a holiday ln
London, and the Stock Exchange has to wait to know
what London was doing on Monday. Great Boulders,
£1 i2s fid. The best-developed mine in the field and
within a month of starting its big sulphide plant. Re-
cently developed a large quantity of oxidised oie,
hitherto unknown. A general all-round improvement
in the mine, but the market has not budged
sixpence. Lake View Consols on the fringe of
£l4, or nearly three millions a higher valuation
than the Gt. Boulder, though it is well known
that the ore at the 600 is poor in many places, and at the

month’s output will be equal to that of last, by reason of
the gold that will be got from cyanide solutions, scrap-
ings, and a general clean-up of the cyanide plant.
Rumoured, too, that some of last month’s gold came from
cleaning the plates of the 60-head mill; now it will pro-
bably be impossible to keep up the output beyond this
month, and it might reasonably be expected to be
not more than 6000 oz. next ; writer would not be
surprised to see it not more than / OOOoz. this
month. The shares are undoubtedly too high. Ivanhoe
South Extended, £2 10s. This is a joke ; they would be
dear at 10s. Perseverance, £ll 10s. They are a good
10 per cent, investment on £l2 a share. Golden Links,

ciateds, 70s. Only a million-and-three-quarters for amine
that is losing thousands of pounds per month, and the plant
of which will shortly shut down to give time to find some

loads oversold. It is now a gambling fight between

fers, 12s. 6d.—would be dear at 2s. 6d.
Chaffers is on the Horseshoe line, and is looking
for Horseshoe lodes, and the public are quite unmindful
of the fact that, for the best of reasons (that they aren t
worth working further), the Horseshoe ceases to work
its lodes fullv 300 ft. before they reach Chaffers boun-
dary. South'Kalgurlis, 975. A fair price if the plant was

proved at 270 ft. in one Shaft, and 400 ft. in
another more than 100yds. away, shares have fallen.
Why I Brownhills, £8 12s. There is nothing to prevent
the Brownhill crowd sending them to £l2 Bs. if they like
to waste a lot of money in so doing, and they have wasted
much in buj'ine blocks not worth a tenth the amount
paid for them—True Blue lease to wit. A few weeks ago
the “ backwardation ” charged on Brownhill shares was as
hiu-h as 3s. per share, which means that some beam
had to pay these rates to carry-over every fortnight.
This was kept up for a few settlements and had to stop.
It gave the whole show away.

The Queensland National Bank completed, on
30th June, 1900, its sixth half-year since the last recon-
struction, when there was a partial wiping-off of old
losses, and the State took the shattered institution in
hand. Results so far are as follows :
The nominal net profit of £12,000 is equal to £5 13s. per
cent, per annum on the shareholders’ funds (capital,
£412 773, and reserve fund, £12,000). But everything is
so vague and uncertain about the Q.N.B. that the
matter is hardly worth calculating.
* * * *
In view of the fact that the Queensland National
Bank is doing at least a little in the way of paying off its
accumulation of old losses, it would be a fair thing it it
issued a supplementary balance-sheet (or put its ordinary
balance-sheet in a very different form), showing
the estimated deficit still remaining each half-year.
Then the shareholders could at least have the
satisfaction of knowing exactly where they are and
how fast the shortage is being reduced. The debts
against which the bank has no assets, apart from its
capital(£4l2,773) and reserve-fund (now raised to £15,000),
are understood to be these: £427,581 owing to the
Queensland Government ; an unknown sum, probably
rather over £1,000,000, still owing to private depositors
and these other losses (amount unknown) against which
the bank has already written off £74,352 of its profits
in three years, leaving a balance (amount unknown)
still to be provided for. A curious-looking “asset
in the balance-sheet reads, “Private ledger accounts,
debts in suspense, pending realisation of securities,
£662 100*” and it is presumably towards the losses
on this account that the £74,352 mentioned above
has been applied. If the losses on this account go as high
as £400,000, then it would appear, at a rough guess, that
the Queensland National Bank has £1,800,000 of deficiency
to make good out of earnings before it gets properly on its
feet again. And last half-year it earned 12/,447. At this
rate the process of recovery will apparently take 33 years,
and it will be about that time before the shareholders can
expect any dividends, unless a rise in the value of
property and an increase in banking profits accelerates
the recovery. Even then everything depends on the Bank
not going on the boom drunk again before the effects of the
last boom are cleared off. At the same time things may be
a little less awful than they look, and on the principle that
the shareholders should know fully how their property
stands, and that it can’t stand worse than many of them
suspect, and that the policy of hush-it-up is a bad policy
at all times, The Bulletin reckons that the Queensland
National Bank’s balance-sheet should be drawn up very
differently. It should show, in the first place, the full
liability to private depositors, including the £1,000,000 or
so which is repayable out of profits, and which, though it
remains a liability, is altogether dropped out of the
liabilities as shown by the bank’s statement. The £427,581
which is repayable to the Government out of profits is
included as a liability, though it is admitted that there
are no real assets against it, and the similar debt to
private depositors should be included likewise. Then all
the assets should be put in as near their real value as
can be estimated, and . the balance should he put
down as plain, honest, unvarnished shortage of £1,000,000
or £1,400,000 or £1,800,000 or whatever it may be. The
present Q.N.B. balance-sheet doesn’t convey, to a
person unacquainted with the complicated circumstances,
the slightest semblance of a true story of the bank’s
position. Read by itself it creates an impression that
things are between one and two millions better than they
are. It creates an impression that the capital and reserve
fund—£424,ooo odd—are intact, whereas they would
probably only about one-quarter cover the deficits. In
fact the balance-sheet, by itself, represents the Queensland
National Bank as solvent, whereas it is utterly and in-
credibly insolvent, and has merely made an arrangement
whereby its creditors give it time to earn the money to
pay off its deficiency. „ „ *
The Q. N. Bank has a small but increasing new business,
and a reserve of cash and liquid securities (£1,771,551)
which is equal to about 85 per cent of all the now current
liabilities, and as all new business takes precedence of the
reconstructed deposits, it is as safe for the new depositor
as any other bank. But its balance-sheet is a hideous
farce' all the same—the sort of farce which would be
unlawful if there was a decent Banking Act and it was
decently * *

A balance of £3696 was brought forward from last half-
year, and the surplus of £1721, which remains after pay-
ing the dividend, added to this leaves £5417 undivided
profit. The management creates a further paper asset of
£l4OO by writing up the nominal value of 2800 £5 prefer-
ence shares of the Australian Deposit and Mortgage Bank
(previously written down from £5 to 10s. per share) from
10s. to £1 per share. This makes £6817 available. Out
of this, £5OOO is set aside as the beginning of a new re-
serve-fund (the former reserve of £llO,OOO having dis-
appeared during our old friend, the Depression) and
£lBl7 carried forward.
• * *
Despite the very great improvement in business
it would have been much more solid finance if the
Trustees, Executors and Agency Co. had not written-up
any of its assets just yet, and had transferred £5OOO to
reserve wholly out of profit, and been content to carry
'£4l7 forward. As things are now, its holding of Austra-
lian Deposit and Mortgage Bank securities stands in the
balance-sheet thus :
£16,000 of deposits, valued at 20/- in tne £ .. £16,000
2800 preference shares of £5 each, at £1 .. 2,800
£lB,BOO
Whereas market values are about these; —
£16,000 of deposits at 16/6 in the £ .. .. £13,200
2800 preference £5 shares at 31/6 .. • • 4,410
£17,610
Taking the two items together, they were under-valued
very slightly before the writing-up, and by putting
£l4OO on to their paper value, merely to make
the balance carried forward look larger than it
need be, they are now over-valued by £ll9O.
It is highly probable that the A. D. and M. deposits will
be worth 20s. in the £ some day, but it isn’t sound finance
to put in the balance-sheet as a present asset mere
probable future values. The present value is the matter
at issue. Still the Trustees, Executors and Agency Co.
pppears to be over the worst of its troubles. Its income
is steadily increasing. Its investments (including £lB,OOO
in State securities) yield at the rate of £4 7s. 3d. per
cent, per annum, which seems to suggest that the mort-
gage part of them is reasonably sound. These two items
of expense, “ difference in amount paid and received on
lease, 37 Queen-street,” and “ difference in amount paid
in connection with mortgage on premises and received on
investment of £21,400 held against mortgage,” are
diminishing. And a small beginning—a very small one,
certainty—has been made at rebuilding the fine reserve
fund of £llO,OOO which evaporated so completely m the
bad days.

The N.S.W. Governor’s salary will be re-
duced to, probably, £5OOO. Little will be gained
if Parliament doesn’t insist on cutting off inciden-
tals. Gov. Beauchamp on small heads draws
about £2OOO more than his nominal pay.
Victoria gives £7OOO, but allows no extras.
Queensland gives £5OOO, but allows £5OO for
visiting through the province, £5OO for uni-
forms, and other items. S. (Australia and W.
Australia give £4OOO each and allow no extras, and
Tasmania gives £3500. In Gov. Hampden’s day
Premier Reid thunderously assured the Laborists
that he was about to make a Democratic example
of that pensive potentate, but after all-night
clamor, he ran thiough a little Bill that left the
Governor financially just about where he was.
N.S.W. Gov. has his town house, his country
house, his £7OOO, his paid secretary, paid assistant-
secretary, paid orderlies and other paid appurten-
ances.
* * * »
Casual readers of the Melbourne papers
would imagine that the people of Victoria were
just frantic with joy over the appointment of Lord
Hopetoun to the Governor-Generalship. As a
matter of fact the populace takes no frenzied
interest in the slim weakling in question, who
during his regime was known to the vulgar vari-
ously as Little Lord Fauntleroy, Lord Hopeless, or
Lord Helpus. Butthelank, friendly-mannered lord-
ling did very well in the social sense after his few
pathetic fainting-fits, and made a point of being
awfully naice to half-pint swells and the aristocracy
of the counter. This affability to the “social”
paragraphist accounts for much of the newspaper
gush his appointment has inspired. He actually
took one or two tame scribblers away with him,
when he desolated Melbourne by ceasing to repre-
sent England’s greatness there.
When the Chinese in 1870 attacked the
French Convent at Tien-Tsin, some of the nuns
they impaled, some they cut in half, out of one
they tore the eyes and heart, while still alive,
another they scalded to death, and all they hor-
ribly and shamefullv mutilated.
* * * *
The recent Chinese slaughtering should
cool the missionary ardour a bit; and, even if
there shall still be found amongst us foolish
enough men and virgins who would bid for “ mar-
tyrdom’s ” crown upon the same horrible terms, it
will probably be long era- they get the chance of
doing what, after all, would in that case be in-
tended as. an ultimate benefit to themselves, by
procuring them immortal felicity at the
expense of the Chinaman’s soul. Yet, fail-
ing either the absolute partition or the ab-
solute abandonment of China by Europeans,
nothing is more certain than at intervals will be
repeated such diabolisms as, for instance (during
the ’7O Tien-Tsin massacre), the literal roasting-
alive of European women and children by holding
them by arms and legs upon the fires ! It is said
that the miserable husbands and fathers of the
lately-stormed Pekin Legations made certainty
that their wives and little ones—whom they shot!
—should not fall into the hands of such
fiends unspeakable. So hideous, upon the whole,
have now become the Chinese possibilities to
European women that The Bulletin would
almost excuse the man who—having sister or
daughter bent upon daring the chances of the
China mission-career—should shoot her himself in
the first place. He would then, at all events,
know the worst!
Queensland London Missionary Society-presi-
dent Jones gets-back on one Schmellitscheck for
asserting that “ the missionaries are at the bottom
of all the Chinese trouble”—by explaining that
the way the missionaries led to the present state
of things was by pioneering the country and thus
opening it up to the devils of trade and greed. It
is humorous, by the way, to find one lot of mis-
sionaries absolutely denying that the missionary-
business has had anything to do with the present
ferocious Chinese outbreak, while another lot avers
that it is the Roman Catholics, not the Protestant
missionaries, who are to blame. Of course, if you
asked the R.C.’s, you would get a dolorous story
about the wickedness of the Protestants in foment-
ing discord. ,
* • • *
With quite unconscious humor the Chinese
Reform residents of Sydney have decided to
forego a “ big celebration ” in honor of their
Emperor’s birthday. They will dissemble their
loyalty and “pride of race” until the present
Little Unpleasantness has blown over.

Six years ago there was but one Chinese-
laundry in Sydney. Now there are 20, and they
are growing at the rate of 10 a year. The des-
cription given by officials of the filthy dens in
which clothes are washed and made up should
nerve the Health Department, or some other
authority, to start a rooting-out crusade.
* • • *
Some confusion has been caused by the
publication of news from China, owing to the fact
that the Russian or old style of calendar is appar-
ently used in dating some of the messages from
the East. The Gregorian or “new” style of
reckoning, as adopted by Western civilisation, is
now 14 days ahead of the Julian, or old style. The
difference in the two calendars is that the Gre-
gorian one does not reckon century-years indi-
visible by 400 (as 1700, 1800, 1900) as leap-years.
On March 1, 1900, another day was jumped by the
new style. This explains the seemingly irrecon-
cilable statements as to dates which have appeared
in the cables re the Pekin Legations and other
incidents of the China crisis.

“J.A.C.”: Noteworthy that no Sydney
Jews caught the plague. Also in all the great
pestilences of Europe and Asia the Jews have
escaped comparatively ,easy. During the ’49
cholera only 13 Jews died of the epidemic out of
the 20,000 in London, although the total cholera
deaths numbered 12,837.

The common cabbage Chow seems to look
upon his country’s present predicament as a huge
joke. When told of a recent massacre of Celes-
tials, Ah Hop remarked, “ Too muchee fence in
China. You see, my countlyman lun away, an’
get shot climbin’ over fence.”
“Brahmin” to The Bulletin on the
results of mission-work in the East:
Asa boy in India I remember white men standing under
trees, with hats off, and black Catechists at the side,
preaching. The Missioner would speak m English and
the Catechist in the vernacular, but what was said in
English—quite untranslatable into the vernacular—would
return home, feed on the best, and report “souls saved.”
All “ Christian ” Societies want is a good average ol con-
verts. Then the money rolls in. Indian famines bring
much increase in the convert-crop. Starving Hindus go
from Mission House to Mission House and when one
mission cannot supply food, there is a stampede to the
nearest one that can. Wesleyan Hindus embrace the
tenets of the Church of England—and their loaves and
fishes ; and Baptist converts who run short of supplies, go
for the London Missionary Society; and so on till they
have exhausted every source of supply for their stomachs
on their heavenly road. And of such is the Kingdom of
Heaven 1

What we call “the Devil’s number”
appears also to have an ill reputation in China.
The fact, that the current Chinese year has 13
months is considered ominous of some great
national calamity near at hand.

In view of the slump in the missionary
industry which will probably follow the recent
hideous Chinese slaughterings, the views of Malie-
toa, of Samoa, are interesting. He said, not long
ago:—
The missionaries who graced our country with their
holy—or unholy—presence introduced the same religious
differences and hatreds against each other as obtain at this
hour in civilised States. The missionaries live in palatial
concrete houses, with all the luxuries their country can
afford, and charge us for Bibles and prayer-books, which,
we understand, are sent as free offerings.

Premier Holder thinks it “somewhat
unfortunate that New Year’s Day, which is
always kept as a public holiday, should be
observed as commemoration day for the Common-
wealth. Commonwealth Day was worthy of a
special holiday.” Patriotism takes all shapes and
sizes. Because the best day in every year, which
next year will be the opening day of a new
century, is already one of the appointed occasions
for bank-closing, Mr. Holder considers it an
unsuitable date for the birthday of a new nation.
What an unsentimental and bank-clerical person
is Premier Holder 1

Capt. Wallington had, it transpires,
already booked his passage for England when the
news of his appointment as Gov-Gen. Hopetoun’s
Private Sec. arrived. Wallington, by the way,
has served under five Australian Governors,
Carrington, Duff, Hopetoun, Brassey and Tenny-
son, and lie was Private Sec. to Des Vceux of Fiji
20 years ago.
* * * •
Tasmania already begins to regret one
feature of its recent Jingo enthusiasm. Of the
£lO,OOO Patriotic Fund which the island collected,
£5OOO was sent to England and the other £5OOO is
going there, all by way of showing Tasmania’s
unbounded faith that Britain will look after the
widow and the wooden-legged Tas. soldier, and see
that they didn’t want. There are already some
war-widows to look after, but the Imperial Pat-
riotic Fund, which swallowed up Tasmania’s
mite, is hanging on to the mite. The London
Daily Mail fund also received contributions from
Tasmania, and it has sent enough back to keep
two distressed war-sufferers going. But the big
Fund maintains a death-like silence. Probably it
thought that the Tas. contribution was intended
for British soldiers. The idea of paying the cost
of sending the money to England, merely to
incur the further expense of sending it back to
Tasmania, certainly looked rather ridiculous, and
the British people may be excused if they didn’t
quite understand.
* * * •
Sydney citizens elected their Mayor at
one period. Octogenarian George Thornton, still
alive, was so chosen. That old municipal system
was wound up and Govt. Commissioners took in
hand civic affairs. When the municipal system
was again inaugurated Mr. Thornton was elected
first Mayor by the aldermen. Thus, the veteran
is likely to see four great revolutions in the muni-
cipal life of his native city. Mr. Thornton thinks
the earliest the best system and the Govt.-Commis-
sioners’ era the worst.
* * * *
An aboriginal (S. Q’land) recently, in
conformity with native custom, killed a deformed
piccaninny. He was promptly arrested, and on
trial was condemned to death—a sentence since
commuted. Was the aboriginal so very much
wrong after all ? His method of procedure was
followed by certain highly-civilized nations of an-
tiquity, and it has also been recommended by more
than one eminent philosopher of comparatively
modern times. Future sages will probably decide
that Warrigal was really a tender humanitarian.

N.S.W. Premier Lyne stated in Parlia-
ment t’other night that the Supt. of Navigation
“ was unaware that any vessels had been placed
in peril through the recent Sydney harbor fogs.”
The S. of N’s. “ unawareness ” must have been of
great weight and thickness —considering that one
mter-harbor steamer found herself, when a recent
fog lifted, right outside the Heads, in the open
Pacific.

The Melb. Scotchbyterians have been dis
cussing the drinking habits of the community,
and one rev. gentleman, Gibson by name,
solemnly advised “ that the efforts of the
church should be directed against the
practice of shouting.” This prefer-
ence on the part of the church for soli-
tary drinking has certainly some busi-
ness points about it. The Bulletin can
show no less than
seven good
Presbyterian
reasons why
shouting is a
curse. If you
go in with a-
nother man
and shout there
is no guarantee
that he will shout
back, and if he disna
shout, you pay Is. and
only get one saxpenny
drink. If you are with four
other men and are perishing
for a drink, it costs half-a-
croon to shout, and there
is no guarantee that the
other four will all shout
in return. If they do
shout on the spot
you lose the flavour
of the last two
drinks — when five
drinks are taken in
quick succession the
delicate aroma of the
last two or three is
sacrificed. If they don’t
shout on the spot the
waste of time, an-
xiety, and boot -
leather in shepherd-
ing them till they
do shout is some-
thing terrible.
Shouting, o f
course, means
drinking in
company
(you can’t
tell the shou-
ter to post
your share of
the whisky
to your address), and the other people
will talk over the liquor. This distracts
your attention from the whisky and you lose the
flavour. If the other man shouts first he
may lead you into a public-house where they
five watered liquor. Also the other men notice
ow much you take and that often makes you take
less; if you are alone you start pouring out the
drink, and say you will take soda, and while the
bar-lady has her back turned getting the
soda you fill up, grab the water jug hastily,
tell her you won’t take soda, and by the
time she turns round again she doesn’t know
how much is whisky and how much water.
There are so many economies in drinking with the
flies that the Kurruk’s preference for having its
whisky by itself is quite understandable. At the
same time the Kurruk must have drunk a good
deal to know about these economies.

a Great Historical Work. It depicts bookmaker
Alf. Joseph in London as the confidential guest of
the future first Governor-.General of Australia.
The Bulletin begins to fancy that Fat Joseph
turned the scale in Lord Hopetoun’s favor. Any-
how, there is no disputing the fact that Joseph,

N.S.W. District Court Judge Murray,
who has just got one technical arbitration case
off his hands, has received intimation of a
couple of others coming. It used to be the
custom to pay Judges fees for sitting as
arbitrators, and some years ago Judge
Murray was invited to render an account,
but he then declined, and has sent no ac-
count since. His salary as District Court Judge
covers, he modestly states, all his services to the
State.

The case of Matilda Carlisle of a W.Q. /
hospital, set forth in a public enquiry.
The doctor certified that Matilda suffered
from typhoid—a very bad case. Ty-
phoid was followed by abscesses which
gave much trouble. The patient (a .
young girl) was beaten by the matron
with a light strap for not taking broth.
The cook testified that the patient was
often cruelly beaten. It was further alleged
that the beating took place while the patient
was being lanced—&c., &c. No wonder we
set to work to civilise and humanize the heathen.
* * m r
Parramatta Benevolent Asylum is in a
condition disgraceful to Government. There are
1100 inmates (about 200 bedridden) in the old shed
of what was once a woollen-factory. The floors
are thoroughly saturated with oil; and the place
is only fit for burning. And it will burn, sooner
or later—with the inmates !
The strictly sober S. Jiff. Herald now
gracefully conducts its business on the site of
the old Red Cow Inn,” a hilarious hostelry of
the early days, much blamed by contemporaries
for watering its rum at the Tank Stream.

other day a witness was excused from kissing the
book” on the ground that said book had, a
few days previously, been vigorously
|smacked” by a man with well-developed
lip-cancer.
Though Cronje and the old Dopper
Boers will keenly feel their imprisonment on
St. Helena, some of the younger ones and
the Hollanders will not be so lonely there.
About half the gay adventuresses of Cape
Town and Johannesburg are St. Helena
girls.
Some practical philanthropists in
Wellington, M.L., make a point on every
public holiday of getting up an evening
entertainment for all the sailors in port.
They keep them going with games,
music, and supper till after 11 p.m.,
pu 0-closing time, f o that Jack gets
aboard his vessel again with no tempta-
tion to look on the rum when it is red.
As a consequence, no sailor has been
fined in Wellington for drunkenness
during the last 12 months.

The English Society for the
propagation of the Gospel in foreign
parts has to date contributed nearly
a quarter of a million pounds toC.E.
missions in Australia. This year
Australia will make its first refund contribution
to the Society.
* * * ♦
Sydney City Fathers must have beeii de-
fossilised to an amazing extent, over the late
bubonic scare. They have now decided to force
1 per 1000 of Sydney’s population to become sani-
tation experts. The 410 applicants for ten Sani-
tary Inspectorships under the Council must pay
10s. 6d. each for a course of lectures at the
Technical College, to last until Dec. next, when
ten will be chosen after examination, and the other
400 let loose to hunt rats. Pity the Council did
not decide to attend the course itself, or pay for
the applicants, or force only these who are chosen
to pay. To thus oblige 410 unfortunate seekers of
a £3 per week billet to disburse 10s. 6d. and wait
six months for a l-in-40 chance, may be the
Council’s idea of Compulsory Education, but it’s
a mighty mean way of making £220" 10s. as a sop
to a Govt, which is on the point of sending them
to hunt rats themselves.
* * * *
Dr. Graham, M.L.A.’s speech on Lyne’s
municipal alleged Reform Bill, was.the best utter-
ance up to date from any N.S. W. public man on
the subject. The doctor and Alderman “Tom ”
Hughes are the only two men in the City Council
who have risen above kerb-and-gutter level.
John Norton is useful, of course, but has
done nothing but scream “Reform” and vote
“Harris.” The choice of Mayor of Sydney on a
popular vote would lie among Graham, Hughes,
and Norton, with the odds largely on the latter.
Then a municipal Donnybrook for a year or so,
and, afterwards, painless extinction of the whole
Council as at present constituted.

S.A. Govt, is so disgusted with the
vagaries of Adelaide Hospital Board that it has
drafted a Bill, under which the Board will be
abolished, in favor of direct departmental control.
