A WOMAN’S LETTER
Sydney, July 24, 1900.
My Dear Myee.—
“ The Rose of Persia ” simply lavishes drapery
and colour-tone, until the most exigent eye is
satisfied. Where am Ito begin ? And presently,
where am Ito leave off? The first act is a
composition in browns. By brown I mean slate,
drab, fawn, terra-cotta, apricot, orange, marigold-
yellow, all rich, glittering, exquisitely harmonious,
with a couple of pale blue frocks to accentuate.
And, away in the background stretched the blue
waters of the open bath. The foreground is the
court in Hassan’s house, scattered with cushions,
lounges, and crowded with lovely wives, until the
desire to be a bul-bul deranges the sanest
Horsetralian intellect. That’s Act 1.
Dolly Vane wears two handsome robes. One is
of black, heavily embroidered in green and blue
stones. The over-mantle is of some lovely,
shimmery blue-green silk, that looks as if it had
been scooped out of mother-o’-pearl. The turban
is opalescent blue. Then, by way of a bold con-
trast, her trousers, and satin slippers are vivid
royal blue. The second is of orange silk, running
in and out of different tones of orange. Dorothy
again represents (or tries to) a plain and passe
female. But what does it matter ? Her next part
will be genuine young bridal, without the comment
that smites her here. “ The thing is plain,” says
the Vizier, handing her in marriage to the story-
teller.
Carrie Moore’s second frock is a poem—a lilac
and a silver lyric. Over palest mauve falls a
violet gauze. Trousers of sapphire-blue just hint
at themselves underneath. A silver crown is set
in her long, red-brown hair, and a veil of deeper
violet floats from it. From the arms fall long,
pale satin sleeves, heavily embroidered in silver,
and a big bunch of lilac hangs from her silver
girdle nearly to her feet.
Another creation, that thrills considerably, is
graced by Brownlow —Brownlow looking all the
world like Henri the Heartbreaker. Brownlow as
King is only to be bettered by Brownlow as
Sultan. In fact, he even filled to a woman’s
satisfaction that beautiful dress of apple-green
satin, lined with drab, and edged with drab, on
which were worked huge pearls, and glittering
green jewels.
And the “ Rose ” herself, Miss Winston Weir ?
I wish I could rave over her, spontaneously, but I
can’t. She wears such an air of not-being-used-to-
her-clothes that you can never regard her as well-
dressed. Pink is the leading note of her frocking,
rich heart o’ rose pink—satin and gauze—and a
leaf-green mantle falling from her shoulders. Out
of the medley of pretty girls, and the whirls of
drapery, stands Cecil Engleheart in a yellow
turban and apricot and orange robes ; the tall
chorus-girl (whom Rumor engages to an operatic
baritone), in a dull brown, with a turquoise girdle
and a rose-lined cloak of brown gauze, the pink
showing through; pretty Miss Joey Cassillis,
dancing in primrose skirts bordered with fine gold,
and making love to the Sultan in gauzy blue, with
a bouquet of forget-me-nots hanging from her
girdle. Also, there is the loveliest ballet, all
gauze, and tunic, and floating veils, and silver.
Miss Minnie Everett was loudly called for after
the ballet. She came out in a silk blouse and
black skirt, and kissed her hands to the wildly-
applauding house.
It was not in the opera. But the d.c. had paid
seven-and-six for its seats, and considered the
extra sensation was due to it.
The extra incident was this way. Carrie Moore
received two big baskets of flowers. She ran off
the stage and left them behind her. Presently, on
came Brownlow, to sing a duet with Miss Weir.
An encore was called for. Brownlow stepped out
again. Then, for the first time, he saw Carrie’s
two baskets of flowers standing at the corner of
the stage. He went forward, picked them up,
smiled, bowed, generously presented one to Miss
Weir, and kept the other himself with a truly sul-
tanic unconsciousness.
By the way, a rumor got round last week that
Brownlow was out of the caste, and that Mr. W.
B. Beattie (his understudy) was to play Sultan.
Crowds of “ Willie Beattie’s” friends rolled up to
see the popular young baritone in his first profes-
sional part. Bohemian Sydney knows him
already well as a writer, singer, playwright, and
actor. His little Japanese Opera played by Ama-
teurs last year was bright and fetching. Lately he
made his hit in “Lady Dolly,” was engaged by
Mr. Williamson, continued hitting the public
with Jingo sojtgs at the Lyceum, and now aspires
to fill the Sultan’s shoes. Up till Friday Brown-
low was still refusing to go on, but somehow the
breach, whatever it was, was healed.
The only painful thing that I took away from
“ The Rose of Persia ” was the discovery of a new
sect. I am afraid I must call it The Persian
Methodist. And lam sorry to say that our own
giddy Lauri is head of the Order. His insignia
are a beard from the chin, a pair of slippers, and
raiment that ought to go round with the plate. I
hope you will notice I am not including his 25
wives among the indispensables of the new
Religion, although they are a lovely lot of
creatures.
To turn to a different kind of a show. The
Mayoress’s Conversazione to the Associated
Nurses brought a happy crowd to the Town Hall
on Friday. Nurses wore their respective uni-
forms, donning white kid gloves by way of a finish.
The kindly Mayoress beamed out of a grey bro-
cade. Miss Cissie Harris was in white and a lace
polonaise over chiffon flounces.
It is fashionable just now to be polite to one’s
guests. Even tiptop society women endeavour to
make things pleasant to their visitors, and a few
bold spirits go to the length of introducing a visi-
tor to the one on the next chair. But there’s still
a feeling of daring about such a proceeding, and
the bravest woman will not introduce one guest to
two people.
Returning to the nurses, what a positively
beautifying profession ! Girls go into it with
sallow cheeks, and emerge with lovely pink-and-
white complexions. Hard work, paififul experi-
ences, and mutton-chop diet —is it tliese that work
the miracle ? At this particular party, Society
scrags, in lace and diamonds, shrank into positive
ugliness beside those hundreds of fresh face 3
under white caps and glossy hair.
By the way, what do you think of this as a
tribute to the healthiness of the profession ? A
reasonably slender young Sydney girl entered a
country hospital only a few months ago. She now
writes down to her mother that her weight—fight-
ing-weight, scrubbing-weight, late-hours-and-early-
hours-weight—-has gone up to seventeen stone !
“ In the midst of Death we are in Life.” Forgive
the grimness of the sentiment. The annual dance
in aid of the Hospice for the Dying will take place
in the Paddington Town-hall on August Ist. The
Dance of Death!
Orpheus M'Adoo’s death leaves a great blank
in the Jubilee Singers. He succumbed after an
operation. At his funeral, on a very wet after-
noon last week, singers and actors and actresses
filled a long line of carriages that passed up King-
street at the hour when everybody was about.
Through the carriage-windows one caught glimpses
of black frizzy hair, golden curls, and crimps, and
violet-powder. An open-carriage was laden with
flowers scattered in generous profusion from the
theatrical world.
The blare of concerts is in the air—recitals,
benefits, and plain, straight, money-making affairs.
Two Sydney girls—Miss Pearl M‘Crossin and
Miss Fannie Walker—gave a private and pleasing
hearing at the Equitable last week, and come
before the public at the Y.M.C.A. Hall on Thurs-
day, August 2.
Speaking of concerts, someone tells me that she
went to one lately under pressure, and entered
just as a serio-comic song of “Baiby” got as far
as these words—
“ Mother’s darling, sweet idol,
Sleeping snugly in his crldle."
As he dashed out he nearly upset a policeman.
Allpress’sbenefit is fixed for August 16. Surely
an overflowing house is preparing to be there.
The well-known fiddler has been in bad health
lately.
The Pretoria Ball, in aid of the Boys’ Brigade,
comes off at Paddington Town Hall next Thurs-
day, August 2.
Oh, these wedding notices ! Their latest. A
Sydney girl was married last Monday week. She
spent her honeymoon at a fashionable hotel, and
on the Wednesday she read how her pretty wed-
ding had come off on the Tuesday ! Like Nanki-
Poo, she shivered and shook when she realised
how she and her bridegroom had stolen into the
hotel on Monday afternoon.
Donald Macdonald, after surviving bullets and
bran-pies of Ladysmith, begins lectures at Cen-
tenary Hall, Sydney. To save the heart-breaks of
hero-worshippers, big Mac. is married. Mrs.
Mac., a well-known Melbourne pen-woman, is a
little, slender, golden-maned woman, with a
passion for cycling. Their one child (Elaine) is a
fine, wolhbuilt girl, just into her teens. Mother
and daughter are planning a trip to the old
country next year, when Mrs. Mac. will cycle
England over and over.
Everyone knows that Lady Lamington is one of
the best whips in Queensland. As the Hon.
May Hozier she was renowned for her good
driving, and in Brisbane is to be seen tooling her
handsome bays along the North Quay road.
Report says she manages his restive Excellency
well—one hears a great deal about his kicking
over the traces, but one never sees anything ! Still
it gives a man an extra fascination to have such a
reputation, and the idea is federated in the Society
of Australia that Queensland’s Governor is a wolf
in sheep’s clothing all the same.
Capt. Farquhar, the longest of the two long
Queensland A.D.C.’s, drives rather a novel turn-
out. He packs his lazy length and a smartly-
dressed lady visitor at Govt. House into a tiny
yellow sulky, adds three spanking chestnuts in a
row, and tears down street at top speed. Some-
times the chestnuts run together, more frequently
they don’t, but prefer different sides of lamp-posts,
and such-like things. of this season’s
debutantes watched the meandering steeds ad-
miringly the other afternoon. Said one to the
other : “He drove one horse on Saturday most
beautifully, and then he had two yesterday. Two’s
called tandem, you know. I wonder what three
in a row is called ?” “I am not quite sure,” was
the grave answer, “but I think it’s triplets.”
What a boon our Governor is—to the other pro-
vinces ! One never takes up a Melbourne, Bris-
bane, or Adelaide paper without coming upon
some paragraph relating to our Ex. We never
follow the doings—doings, did I say ’ much smaller
things than doings—of other people’s Governors.
Of course, it might be answered here that we have
enough to do with our own, but, be that as it may
(and we make no confessions, invite no aspersions),
journalists from over the borders will find a great
blank in their screeds when little Willy is taken
hence. What do you think of the presumption of
the Melbourne letter in an English journal that
takes it upon itself to speak of our Ex. as “ The
. Actiny-Governor,” adding that the facetious sobri-
quet has been bestowed by all Australia ? This is
the first Sydney has heard of it. You have to go
to London via Melbourne to get news of the
prophet in your own country. As a matter of
fact, the title in question was first applied by The
Bulletin to Lieut -Gov. Madden.
The Grand National Bazaar spreads over all the
English papers. No details are missing, down to
the fact that the Princess of Wales refused to take
any change out of a £lOO note. In fact, a long list
of names is published in one journal of all the
Blue Blood buyers who followed Her Highness’
example, and refused to fill their pockets with
small change. Mrs. Baden-Powell did a roaring
trade with photographs of B.P. But one looked
in vain for fresh side-lights on the pronunciation
of the famous name. Following His Ex.’s ex-
ample, Sydney calls Powell “ Poole,” but the fact
remains that 8.-P.’s schoolfellows rhymed Powell
with towel. Anyway, nothing sold like those
photographs. For once, the sales of all the beauty-
actresses were entirely eclipsed by the pictures of
a small, and not remarkable-looking, man, and the
bazaar achieved the huge financial success of
£20,000.
Nicholson and Co. send an Australian edition of
the evergreen “Blue Danube” and five other
Strauss waltzes, the lot for a shilling, which I
warmly recommend. Also from Nicholson’s is a
pretty waltz by Tourjee, “It Still Waves,” pub-
lished by the dashing John Church Company,
who have brought out so many good things lately.
Writes my Melbourne correspondent:—
0 And ‘Hopey’ is to be Governor-General!
So much for winning little ways ! A local humble
admirer relates how the then Governor remem-
bered to enquire for the narrator’s sick child,
casually mentioned at a previous interview.
“Professor Kernot, at a recent smoke-social,
reminisced of a farewell unofficial visit paid to
the Working Men’s College by Lord H. The latter,
says his chronicler, took especial interest in the
engineering students, recalling as they did his own
elder brother, an enthusiastic would-be engineer
who entered iron-works in Glasgow and toiled
there in his old dungarees till he got a fatal chill.
Premier M‘Lean’s mother (her son relates) in her
lassiehood ‘ pu’d the gowans fine’ in company
with the grandmother (one of the leddies Mac-
donald) of the Gov.-General elect.
“ Australian Governors, ’tis said, can’t make
out what people see in Hopetoun, don’tcherknow.
Not that it’s so easy to be popular. Precious few
pro-consuls master the trick. A shining excep-
tion was General Norman. That dear old man,
say Bananalanders, seldom forgot a face and never
seemed hampered by the idea that too much
‘niceness’ to nobodies means not enough to go
round the somebodies. A younger vice-regal per-
sonage, somewhere about, is far less successful.
Taken singly, he’s not intolerable, just as his wife,
‘on her own,’ is inoffensive enough. In double
harness, however, they trample recklessly on
Sassiety’s pet corns. When together they’re like
two green gooseberries on a stalk, sour enough to
set on edge the teeth of a whole province.
“ Melbourne ‘ sassiety ’ is wondering how ‘ dear
Lady Hopetoun ’ likes the idea of a second banish-
ment to Australia. That she was quite out of her
element as a Governoress was insisted by all those
buzzing people who expected the ‘Gov’nment
’Ouse ’ chatelaine to wear an eternal glad smile of
welcome, and to be painfully gratified at the sight
of everybody throughout a long, nerve-racking
function, and through the maddening waste of an
official ‘damce’ or ‘garding’ party. Lady H.
had a wild, free Hibernian rearing, and the
restraints of Australian incivilisation were par-
ticularly galling to her. She was only really
happy when on top of a horse, rattling across
country; then, at least, she could shake off the
bulky Guvmentouse deadheads of the unfair sex,
who regard the vice-regal couple as entrepreneurs,
paid to provide cheap festivities for the ‘very
Naicest ’—and to be dooced smart about it.
“What show has Lord Richard Nevill for a
place on the Gov.-General’s staff? He sup-
planted Wallington as Brassey’s private secretary.
Now Wally is on top, and Dick is just an aide to
Madden. Do you think that if Hopetoun says,
‘ Shall we take on Nevill,’ Wallington will rub his
chin and say ‘ Better not ? ’ What would you do ?
“In view of the fact that the heir-apparent to
the Austrian throne has just renounced all claims
to the Crown for the sake of a governess, local
Naicest find it hard not to lie awake ’o nights,
pondering the marked attentions shown these
times by ;a titled tenderheart to a belle of the
boards. -
“Among the supposed victims of the alleged
Chinese massacre is Miss Ethel Halley, the mis-
sionary-daughter of a well-known local Indepen-
dent clergyman. When Miss Halley first went
to China, her parents went with her, and stayed
till they saw her settled in a ‘safe district.’
Later, she visited her people in Melbourne, and
took back with her as travelling companion to
Pigtail Country her sister, Dr. Gertrude Halley,
who enjoyed her peep of the Celestial Land, and
returned somewhat reluctantly to practise her
profession in a Melb. suburb. Now, despite
parental precautions, it seems as if genial Ethel
Halley had dreed her weird in company with the
other foreigners in cruel Pekin.
“ The Churches are not too consistent, as shown
by their time-honored bazaar tactics—raffling and
the fleecing of susceptible male sinners per medium
of pretty saints. Besides speculation and flirta-
tion, stage wiles now find a corner in the up-to-
date apostle’s bag of tricks. A local city mission,
peregrinating from one tabernacle to another,
draws the ungodly to the House of Prayer by dint
of frisky little week-night farces. While the ama-
teur comedian, crouching in the pulpit, awaits his
cue to ‘ pop up,’ a fellow-mummer, in the role of a
reveller (presumably vinous) at an at-home, pro-
poses successively to several sister-guests in front
of the Communion-rail. ‘ Why ain’t I a Mormon?’
wails the amorous one later, with his glance glued
to a group of fetching, disappointed deaconesses,
whereat the congregation titters decorously. These
histrionic evangelists are not ear-marked b?
Bishop Goe. Nathless, the Church has its own
spry way of calling sinners to repentance. Isn’t
tne prevalent sentimental curate bought and paid
for as a petticoat-draw pure and simple?
“By the way, our Anglican Shepherd-in-
Chief still laments the loss of a vicar with
a high gift of training up girls, old and
young, in the way they should go. Men com-
plained that his reverence’s walk and conversation
bristled with allusions to ‘ the bliss of a young
lady’s kiss, &c., couched in Scriptural language.
Women, however, found him ‘ugly but magnetic,’
and feel themselves much further from Heaven
than before What’s His Name gaed awa’.
“All the Naicest rolled up to Nance O’Neil’s
Hospital matinee at Her Majesty’s (July 20th.)
The Lieut-Governor seemed cheery as usual and
found plenty to say to his wife and daughters.
These listeners never appear to find Pa prosy,
which says much for his quality as a conversation-
alist. The average patriarch is habitually taciturn
in the hearing of his own unappreciative flesh and
blood. When he does talk, his nearest and
dearest have the air of wishing that old fogies shed
their tongues along with their teeth.
“In the vice-regal group, Mrs. Osborne, the
new grandson’s mamma, loomed larger than her
own sufficiently plump mother. Sister Ruby wore
a white silk muslin and lace sailor-collar with her
dark frock, and screened her pretty face with a
white veil. Mrs. Percy Chirnside and her sister,
Miss Fenner, affected white furs, turquoise
splashes in their toques, and the prevalent sack-
jackets to the knees. Mrs. Willie Robb, who
came with a Fisken coterie,, had red-brown Eton
suit picked out with white, and gauzy black
toque.
“ On the boards, Nance O’Neil, as that bad egg
Hedda Gabler, was at home when things took a
tragic turn, but as a supposedly-attractive woman
in her own house, she seemed a cold, sharp-
tongued, masterful Yankee baggage. Altogether
this particular group are histrionically unwhole-
some creatures, though decidedly interesting.
Kingston represented a wild-eyed groggy Genius.
Even Carne’s good Prof, was prone to neglect his
own wife and drift into a Platonic comradeship
with a golden-haired grass-widow—whose mar-
vellous power for good over men was apparently
too precious to waste on a mere husband.
“ Josephine Thynne moves one foot slowly up
and down when she weeps (in Hedda Gabler.)
Apparently her tear-pump has a pedal action.
The art of weeping on the stage is apparently not
easily acquired. Mrs. Brough simply hides
behind a lace handkerchief and says ‘boo-hoo,’
which is not weeping. Nance O’Neil has a
genuine sobbing cry. Both Duse and Ellen Terry
can weep wet at will. It is related of old John
Ryder, a sterling English actor, that when an
actress expressed surprise at another actress being
able to shed real tears on the stage, he said, ‘ Look
at me, my dear,’ and instantly rolled a big tear
down his cheek But one cannot feel sad when
Miss Thynne lifts her foot to weep; it is too
amusing.
“ The black theatre-pelisse deserves encourage-
ment, because its wearer looks less huge than in a
gay-colored garment built on the same lines.
Lately, scribe saw a black-satin specimen, just
from ’Ome. Yoke and high collar, sprinkled with
designs in lace applique, were edged with fur and
a lace flounce. Down the fronts ran broad laid-on
bands of lace and chiffon frills, in black and white
respectively.
“ A large photo reproduction in a recent Melb.
Punch was an excuse for the following legend:—
‘ Miss May Thompson, of Ararat, who made her
debtit at the Prahran Mayoral Ball, and wore
cream silk, the bodice and skirt veiled with gauze,
richly embroidered with, &c., &c., and carried one
of Miss Blank’s shower bouquets.’ And still there
are people who complain that Punch is not a very
humorous paper.
• “ Some day the artistic conscience of Melbourne
will arise and demand that the paddocky portraits
of local drapers, grocers, etc., which occupy so
much space in the National Gallery, shall be
burnt. A few of a man’s friends pay for his por-
trait to be painted, send it to the Gallery, and it
is. there stuck up to be an eyesore. Thus you come
across several yards of portrait of Benjamin Cow-
deroy, a big square of John Blyth, and so on. The
worst offence in this way is a picture of two grown-
up daughters of a rich squattin g family—a big canvas
representing two young women, got up in full
evening-dress, in a garden at noonday. It is a
fearful thing in pink and green, but hung in the
midst of a group of masterpieces of painting. How
hideous the taste that permits of these offences to
the eye being hung in a public gallery !
“And when is somebody going to tomahawk
that unspeakably-awful Vic. National Gallery
portrait of the Queen —by no less a personage than
Hubert Herkomer ? Talk about treason—no such
treason was ever committed as the hanging in
public view of such a portrait of a monarch, in

The discussion as to whether or no the
plenteous Nance hath the fire and soul of the true
artist is still raging hotly. Everything in these
days of liberal interchange of opinion becomes a
subject for discussion save and except the one
fact universally admitted by the “cultchawed”
that the P.D. Corset bestows the highest possible
charm and grace upon the wearer. Be she a
Nance O’Neil, a Mrs. Brough, a Brown Potter, or
a divine Sara there is a little something left for
her to gain from the P.D. God bless it!

The plague has had its advantages for some
people, though not for most. The Sydney Safe Deposit,
for instance, has had a great accession of business during
the last few months, consequent on persons having de-
cided to prepare for the worst by placing their papers,
&c., in safety. The fear is that when a house is quaran-
tined valuables may go astray. It is well to be on the
safe side, and the Safe Deposit affords perfect protection
against either fire or burglars. It is well worth a visit,
anyway. The Deposit is situated in Ash-street, near to
Paling and Co’s.*

Thomas Bracken, Maoriland’s dead poet
and legislator, should have turned in his grave
when prosy Anderson, of Waterloo, presented
him to N.S.W. Parliament as an advocate for
women’s franchise rights. Bracken was-a Liberal
in most respects, but woman’s claim to vote he
scornfully repelled. Anderson didn’t quote
Bracken fairly.
Genial sportsman Mat. O'Shanassy (Vic.)
recently deceased, was the last surviving son of
the oldtime Parliamentarian, and his death ex-
tinguished a family ambition. His father be-
queathed him the bulk of his fortune, believing
that it would stimulate the young man to enter
political life and achieve a baronetcy. But Mat.
was contented with the pleasures of the turf.

rently only set him up temporarily. He was
medically advised to visit California, but time w r as

Delegate Deakin is very complimentary to
Kingston, and says he is a born diplomat. “ But
we had to curb him,” says Deakin—“ he was
always trying to put sentences into our letters
purposely to stir people up. But we struck them
out, and Kingston had to go on his own for a
fight.” “Charlie” —according to the letters he
sends to his friends in Adelaide—is determined to
“ stir-up ” a certain high official when he comes
back.
A mere scratch on the leg, leading to
blood-poisoning, killed Abb6 Schurr in Casino
hospital, at the age of 74. The abbe made a
brilliant start in his clerical career. His linguistic
knowledge was marvellous, and in every way he
appeared cut out for an ideal University professor.
He practised a while at this on the Continent and
in Dublin, but suddenly emigrated to N.S.W. and
lived among timber-getters and other pioneers till
death.
Victorian Dr. Morrison, the Times corres-
pondent, recently killed at Pekin, was a great
favourite with David Syme up to the time of the
New Guinea Expedition. David was not quite
satisfied with Morrison’s exploration of Papua,
and sent out a second expedition—remarkable in
its history of disaster. David Barclay Walker
was the journalist entrusted with keeping diary,
and so wonderful were some of his entries that
Editor Fitchett, of the then-existing Melbourne
Daily Telegraph, declared in his paper that if
the entries were correct, the expedition must have
worn “ seven-league boots” to cover the distance.
Walker sued the Telegraph for libel, and got a
verdict for £IOO.

One of the victims of the Mafeking-drunk
night in London was Capt. Frank Robinson,
nephew of Australia’s old friend, Sir Hercules.
While the captain was cheering, in a hilarious
state of wine, a ’bus ran him down. He was
diagnosed at the hospital as merely drunk and
locked up—to be found dying of skull-fracture in
the morning. It is a question whether the relief
of Mafeking didn’t cause far more deaths than the
siege did.

paths of calamity. Byrnes died—and that was
easy ; but M‘IIwraith walked long among the
shadows before disappearing. Griffith and Nelson
got safely housed by an exercise of canniness that
looks like craft. But Morehead—poor Morehead !
Donaldson—poor Donaldson ! Pattison — poor
Pattison ! Buzacott —-poor Buzacott! And so on.
And Palmer died before the crash came.

The appointment of Chaffey Baker as a
Q.C. has provoked a smile in Adelaide. He is
not known to have any standing as a pleader ; he
hasn’t appeared as a counsel in the courts half-a-
dozen times in the last 20 years. During the very
week of his promotion he gave a highly technical
ruling as Leg. Council President, which was
laughed at by the profession. The Chief Justice
practically has the making of Q.C’s, and Baker’s
appointment has been construed by the public as
having something to do with the recent incident
re Privy Council appeals.

on the matter ?

Pumpkin Smith, to Canterbury (N.S.W.)
electors, who, he hopes, will return him to the Leg.
Assembly:—
He might be forgiven for referring to a matter of a per-
sonal character. When the terrible accidents occurred in
connection with the coal-mines while he was Minister for
Mines he had been promptly on the spot, and, although
repeatedly warned not to venture below, he was deter-
mined to see exactly what was the cause of the trouble,
and had gone right down to the scene of the accident and
seen for himself. He did not consider the danger to him-
self. What the miners had to stand he was prepared to
stand. (Loud applause.)
Pumpkin Smith must, indeed, be a man of nerve.
Imagine the nerve of a person who skites like
that !
The late R.C. Bishop Lanigan, who was
a Templemore man, was present at a banquet held
to celebrate the completion of a certain big N.S.W.
country church. Many healths, including the
Bishop’s, had been honored, when an excited in-
dividual named Murphy, or something like that,
laboriously scrambled to his feet and stated in a
thick voice that he had “ wan more toast to pro-
pose, ” and he proceeded to fire-off—
Fill yer glasses, ivery man agin :
Here’s to Lanigan, the only Lanigan,
The flower av Timplemoore.
Then he fell under the table amidst great enthu-
siasm, through which the prelate’s thin treble “Sit
down, Murphy,” was plainly audible.
What Chamberlain thinks of Alfred Deakin
and what A.D. thinks of Eyeglass Joe it would
be interesting to know. In introducing the Com-
monwealth Bill in the Commons, Chamber-
lain studiously refrained from mentioning
Deakin’s name, though he found occasion to speak
of Barton, Kingston, and others. Since Deakin
has been back, he would rather not discuss Cham-
berlain. He has called him “ that brilliant states-
man,” but interviewers cannot draw him on the
subject of Cambcrlain the man. This reticence
does not make for sparkle and spice in public life.
In the interests of newspapers and for the delight
of theirreaders, Deakin ought to be thumbsorewed
until he makes candid expression of his opinion of
the ambitious Brummagem screw-and-orchid man.
When Sir George Bowen (an adept at
“butthering-up and slithering-down”) was Go-
vernor of Victoria he had once to preside at some
function in a Godforsaken country township, and
in the course of an oration he said : “ And when
I look around on your beautiful—your beauti-
ful” In the howling wilderness, stretching
on all sides, there was nothing the eye could rest
on, save a miserable little burying-place. “On
your beautiful,” went on Bowen, “ er—cemetery,
I feel as if I should like, when my course of life
is run, to lay my weary head in peace within its
calm seclusion ! ”
Seddon is the most misrepresented man in
politics. He is advertised as being rough in
speech as in appearance, and is depicted by the
Tory papers of M.L. as a savage. In reality, lie is
a pleasant-mannered, kindly-spoken gentleman,
with a handsome face and a look of distinc-
tion carried by no other Australian Premier,
In an assemblage of the world’s statesmen,
Seddon would at once be singled out as a leader.
His appearance and manner proclaim him a born
ruler of men. He is well named “King Dick,”
for, were a plebiscite taken to elect a monarch in
M. he would be king in reality next week, as he
is king in effect to-day.
N.S. Wales has supplied at least three
artillerists to the Chinese—Gapt. Close, Staff-
Sergt. Naughton and Artillery-Instructor Miller.
Capt. Close was regarded as a brilliant young
.artillery officer, but having—as will be remem-
bered —got into trouble over a petticoat, was
court-martialled, and left the service. He went at
once to China, and was appointed a Colonel of
Artillery. Instructor Miller had graduated with
credit at Shoeburyness, but the petty jealousy of
N. officers forced his resignation, and Cli na
promptly gave him also a commission. Staff-
Sergt. Naughton, a machine-gun specialist, left
Svdney in somewhat similar circumstances to
Miller, and was at once employed by the Chinese
Govt.
Mr. John Rae, Sydney’s first town-clerk,
who died the other day at the venerable age of 87,
leaves three ex-town-clerks behind him, of Whom
Mr. C. H Woolcott, who held office in the sixties,
seventies, and eighties, and whom Mr. Rae has
nominated as one of his executors, is the eldest.
Mr. Broughton, Mayor of Sydney in 1847, will
celebrate his 90th birthday next-month. Hon.
Geo. Thornton, first Mayor under the then re-
formed City Council, has just passed into his 81st
year. Of Sydney ex-Mayors there now remain—
one of the forties (Broughton), one of the fifties
(Thornton), one of the sixties (John Woods), two
of the seventies (M. Chapman and C. J. Roberts),
four of the eighties (J. Harris, Hardie, John
Young, and Riley), and four of the nineties
(Manning, Lees, Ives, and M. Harris).
A Bulletin contributor alleges that" ex-
Anarchist Andrews (in whom this paper takes a
mild interest because he’s a decent and very in-
telligent fellow who has been hardly treated) is
the grandson of an English baronet and grandee
of tne Court of Spain who threw off his title and
frills when he settled in Australia—which he
thought (poor dreamer !) would be free for ever
from European ostentation and European degra-
dation. And Sebastian Bach, Andrews’ faithful
Pylades, is a lineal descendant of the father of the
symphony, Sebastian Bach. So extremes rub
shoulders in Australia just as in older lands where
a Russian-caste count is the modem apostle of
equality ; a Russian prince the protagonist of
anarchism—where Mirabeau of the noblesse de-
stroyed the noblesse; and the Royal Due d’Or-
leans voted death to his king and brother and
called himself Egalite !

A deso’ate ending ! A youth named
Crump was engaged at a Sydney labor-agency for
Yethong Station (abo it 40 miles west from Mt.
Hope), but after about two months was dismissed
as incompetent, being offered two weeks at scrub-
bing to obtain some money to return home. It
was about four miles»from the homestead into
desolation. A youth who assisted Crump to fix
camp said at the magisterial inquiry, afterwards
held at Mt. Hope, that Crump remarked, “Any
man would go mad here before two weeks.” He
was accordingly, not long after, found by a station-
hand dead under a tree, having hanged himself

“G.”: Re (8., 30/'6/’00) concerning the
briar pest. Does not the blackberry do greater
damage ? On Bulli Mountain, Illawarra, it has,
in great tangled masses 20ft. high, overrun hun-
dreds of acres of good land, spoiling many selectors’
and farmers’ holdings. It has blocked and
diverted many bush-tracks, and has so encroached
upon even Govt, roads that in some places two
vehicles can hardly pass. Neither grubbing nor
burning will kill it off, and but for the fact
that it, like that other pest (the rabbit), can be
turned to good account, numerous holdings would
have been abandoned through it. City jam-
factories now buy scores of tons of Bulli black-
berries every season at good prices. It is a shock-
ing thing to see the burly miner-out-of-work have
to take to blackberry-picking, but the game serves
to keep his body and soul together during a bad
time.
“A.E.C.” on the eradication of briars
“G.,” B. 30/6/’00)
Grubbing means hard, continuous labor, but “ bulloek-
ing,” besides being much easier, is more effective, if done
when the ground is soft, as after heavy rain. Required 6
or 8 bullocks, a strong 12ft. chain with a hook on one end
and a ring on the other, and a species of boat-hook for
working the chain around the briars. When the chain has
been attached to the briar as close to the ground as pos-
sible, bullocks are “ hooked ” on, and, in a twinkling, out
comes the bush, with so much root (much more than the
best grubbers get) that generally nothing remains to be
done but to tackle the next. On an alluvial flat from
which hundreds of bushes were taken in this way, there
were only one or two trifling root-recoveries, and though
several years have since elapsed, the ground is entirely
free from briars.

Harry Stockdale to “H.G.” (8., 14/7/’00);
Horses with peculiar formation generally
with low withers, more than ordinary power
of belly-distension, and a mean “ brisket ” —can at
times get rid of saddle, bridle and girth—girth re-
maining buckled and unbroken. I once had a
hackney mare that could buck out of saddle, even
when a surcingle was added to the girth, and, by
some peculiar wriggle, slip the crupper also; and
I have known other horses do the same. A fall
with the saddle is often a very ugly one; you
come down so helplessly, and, in the majority of
cases, get trodden-on or kicked by the horse,
which generally lets fly a parting shot at the hated
gear.

Dear Bulletin. —None of your critics of
that ploughing sketch mentioned that the plough,
as depicted, turns its furrow to the left, an
arrangement which I have never yet seen. Syd-
ney Art Gallery also has a picture in which the
driv.er of ploughing bullocks is on the “off” side,
which would hardly be so called if that were the
driver’s usual position.—B. [Other letters to simi-
lar effect.]
“ Pascoe ” : Re turtle re-turning (“ Long-
hurst,” 8., 5/5/’00). A turtle cannot re-turn itself
on a hard (or if level) on any surface. It can do
so, however, on a sandbank with a slight incline.
Mechanically working its flippers as protest against
unusual position, sand will be scooped away on
the lower level, forming a hollow, into.which one
side of turtle gradually sinks till final kick does
the business. Also re Rougetnont’s alleged riding
and steering of turtles. Niggers out in canoes,
without turtle spear, sometimes come across a
sleeping turtle. One man will then jump either
beside or under the reptile and grasp its neck and
push up, thus forcing it to or keeping it on the
surface till his mates assist him to turn it over and
secure it.

“ W.M.F.” : The lady-porcupine does lay
eggs—one at a time ; the integument, as with
reptilian eggs, being leathery instead of calcified.
The mother stows the egg in her pouch, which is
apparent only during this season. When the
youngster appears the mother removes the shell,
and the young one lies blind in the pouch. The
milk is not so much suckled as licked—the
“ porky,” like the platypus, not having true teats.
The young one remains in the pouch till three or
four inches long, when its quills begin to appear.
The mother then digs a place in the ground, and
leaves her progeny there to be visited at pleasure.

“Bathurst” : /e a horse slipping his girth,
&c. (8.7/14/’00). When a horse is bucking tl.e
saddle is balanced on a hump, and the exertion of
bucking tucks the horse up, i.e. shrinks his
stomach and intestines thus allowing sr.ddle and
girth to slip back. In the buck-jumping contest
at Bathurst Show last year, the crupper on one
horse broke, and ti e man and saddle went over the
horse’s head, the girth remaining buckled and un-
broken.

“C.P.”: Re native names and their cor-
ruptions. “Gabo” is really the effort of the
aborigines to pronounce the already-given name,
“Cape Howe.” “Wam>«>,” a place 40 miles west
of Port Lincoln (S.A.), should be called Warmw
(gum-trees), because the last gums for 1060 miles
along the west coast are found there. There are
hundreds of similar corruptions—the first man to
hear the native name being usually a bushman
with no ear.
“P.H.C.”: Noteworthy that the Aus-
tralian aboriginal names of birds and animals
generally have a similarity in sound to their notes
or cries—have natural “ onomatopseia, ” in fact.
Water-wagtail Dirri-dirri
Crow Wa-a-gan

Wood-duck Goonarroo

“ Outpost” : When bike-touring a remote
Upper Murray region lately, I dismounted to ask
my way of a person in orthodox flannel and moles
felling a tree in a paddock near the road ; but, as
I scaled the fence, the worker dropped the axe and
went over to where a coat was lying with a bundle
on it. And presently I was thunderstruck to see
the axe-person sit on the ground, take up the
bundle, unbutton her flannel shirt, and proceed to
give baby its natural nourishment. I fled incon-
tinently, not even looking back to see if my retreat
had made the lady aware of my presence. Fur-
ther on I learned that she is the bread-winner,
husband being a hopeless cripple. She, however,
only wears the trousers to work in.

“ Matabele ” : In answer to “N.Y.A.”
About 12 years ago, at Berowra Creek, I shot the
biggest goanna I have seen or heard of. Mea-
sured him with gun-barrel, sft. 2in. long, standing
about 18in. high at head, with fore legs (which
ADVICE BY THE WAY.
THE GOOD CLERGYMAN : “ You man, pick up those reins. If that animal
were to bolt and break your neck, you’d be more careful in future.”
specially attracted my attention) about 12in. in
circumference. First saw him in the water, where
he was quite at home, and thought I had struck a
little alligator. While on goannas : As a young-
ster, I lived out from Picton, thick scrub around,
and the plentiful goanna ate egg and chicken
wholesale. One day, got a great fright on
seeing a 4ft. goanna with a fowl’s wing
protruding, fan-shape, from his mouth. It
seemed to me that, after swallowing the rest of
the bird, the elbow of the wing had jammed in his
throat. Anyhow, he got away into the scrub
before I had sufficiently recovered from my
astonishment to investigate.

“ Cairnsbrook ” : Here at Widgiemooltha
W.A., we have thousands of crows. ( “ Widgee,”
many; “mooltha,” a crow.) I have never yet
found a crow’s nest, nor do I know of anybody
who has discovered one between here and the S. A.
border. Have seen lots of nests in Yic. and
N.S. W. Any reader explain this mystery ?

“ Scotty ” : All the recent press talk about
the contingenteering Bushman’s handiness and
resourcefulness is tommy-rot. I also was at
Langwarrin, and, as far as I know, the men there
were more and better bushy than the Cornstalk
lot. I don’t mean “bushman” in its ordinary
acceptation; that term is applied to timber-
getters in general, except when tracking, muster-
ing, overlanding, or Major-Mitchelling is the sub-
ject. But the horse-breaker, drover, station-hand
(mounted), kangarooer, brumby-runner, or mail-
rider is not in the same street as the bullocky,
groom, rabbiter, and the generally-useless, in the
matter of wrinkles. Granted they can ride, and
that the dingo and ’roo-scalper can shoot; where
are they when stranded ? They can’t cook beyond
damper and boiled meat; they can’t really make
tea; they are beasts when short of tobacco; and
they are abominably selfish compared to the man
with a team, a mate, and a road to meet people
on. They don’t read, and never have anything to
say. That, ye “patriotic” idiots, is what you
imagine to be the material of your contingents.
But the contingenters are a better sort, even the
yard-stick soldiers who got others to “pass their
riding.” Cockneys and other costers make good
soldiers ;so do country louts. Whereas, the most
intractable, conceited, impossible recruit in a
cavalry regiment is the man brought up amongst
horses. Now then ; my scalp is ready !
* * * *
“ Tacke ” : Re aboriginal tree-climbing
(8., 26/5/’00). Forty years ago, when I was a lad,
the blacks used a tomahawk and a pointed piece
of iron, about £-in. thick and 30in. long. When
chopping out the hole for the big toe they held on
to the iron bar, and when the hole was finished,
the tomahawk was driven into the tree and the
bar taken out and fixed further up, and so on.
The trees were eucalyptus, soft-barked and tall
and straight.

[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
How did you break yourself, Birdie f What brought
you
So still on the ground where I never had sought you 1
Were you tired and .fell with no playmate to tend
you f
Ah! never mind, Dovey, for Dada will mend you.
My Dada’s so good at all that; you remember
He built this great arbor of vines last December,
And the cot you flit out of, the green peas to gather;
One little white bird isn’t much for my father.
It’s not long to wait, dear; the shadows are falling;
Far down in the swamp there the curlews are calling;
Mamma by the gateway is smiling and humming,
And that’s how I know that my Dada is coming.
Supposing— supposing!, though Father is clever—
That somehow—he can’t make you fly again ever ;
Supposing . . . there’s something . . . that no one
can lend you
I won’t ci y; I know that my Dada can mend you!

Peculiar Experience of a Farmer’s Wife.
A farmer’s wife’s life may be thought regular and
devoid of incident, but for 17 years the life of
Mrs. Hannah Eliza Nevin, the wife of a farmer
residing at Oakwood, Inverell, has been so full of
peculiar incidents as to possess extraordinary
interest.
“I am 53 years of age,” she said when inter-
viewed, “and have lived in this district all my
life. TJp till 1833 I could always attend without
difficulty to household duties, but I then began to
suffer greatly from rheumatism in my hands and
fingers. First the fingers became numb and
wasted away. Then my joints swelled, and my
body and limbs ached all over. Later on shooting
pains came in my head causing intense headaches,
and my sleep was often broken. I consulted
doctor after doctor; they diagnosed my case as
chronic rheumatism, and neuralgia in the head,
but no treatment cured me. Then I tried an
electric battery, and many mixtures supposed to
cure rheumatism, but without effect. I read in a
pamphlet how Dr. Williams’ pink pills cured a
case resembling mine, and I commenced them.
A fter a few weeks I felt so much better that I was
encouraged to continue them, with the result that
a few boxes entirely cured me. The swellings in
the joints have gone, the fingers are not bent and
shrunken as before, and the pains have entirely
left me. I do not fear the Winter now, for my
system has been so strengthened that a change of
season does not affect me.”
Cold and damp do not cause rheumatism.
Rheumatism is caused by a vitiated condition of
the blood, and cold and damp then bring on the
pains. But Dr. Williams’ pink pills make rich
red blood and thus drive the foundations of
rheumatism and other diseases from the system.
By their direct tonic action they have cured
paralysis, consumption, locomotor ataxia, skin
diseases, lumbago, bronchitis, asthma, dyspepsia,
anaemia, and nervous disorders of men and women.
Sold by the Dr. Williams’ Medicine Co, Queen’s
Place, Sydney, six boxes fifteen and three, post
free, and by chemists and storekeepers—But mind
you ask for Dr. Williams’. Treatise free to all
who write.*

who need not the stone-axe to aid them in climb-
ing, nature having specially adapted them for
such feats. The men of this Moi race climb trees
like monkeys; and, in descending, apply the soles
of their feet to the bark. They have very accen-
tuated ankle-bones—suggestive of the spurs of a
cock—which aid them in climbing and descending,
and a, protrusion of the base of the spine—which
certainly looks like the remnant of a tail—is sup-
posed to be a further aid. There are compara-
tively few of this race in existence to-day, as the
neighboring nations seem to have taken a delight
in destroying these remarkable people, wl.o are
believed to have occupied, primitively, the whole
Indo-Chinese peninsula.

“P.H.C.’ : lie aboriginal tree-climbing.
Our blacks can climb rough iron-bark trees with-
out cutting notches for the big toe ; but in the case
of a smooth gum one end of a piece of scrub-cane
is looped for hand-hold ; a notch is cut in the tree
and the cane passed round the trunk, the looped
end being held in the left hand and the other in
the right. The climber then puts his toe in the
notch, and gives the cane a hitch up the tree,
resting on the toe, while the cane keeps him from
falling backwards. The end of the cane held in
right hand is then either transferred to the left, or
held by the toes of the unoccupied foot till a notch
is cut as high as can be conveniently reached for
the next step. The cane is then again hitched-up
and the other toe placed in the notch —and so on.
The cane is sometimes passed round both tree and
man’s body,.the ends being held in one hand.

A new industry—of enormous possibilities
to the fertile imagination of the originator—has
just failed. A Muswellbrook (N.S.W.) farmer
was suddenly inspired with the notable
idea that, if brine would convert dead pork
into bacon, it should do the same
with the live article, and thus save immense
“ The Manoeuvres of Jane ,” at Melbourne Princess’. (Robert
Brough as Lord Bapchild and Mrs. Brough as Constantia
Gage.)
Photo, by Talma, Melb. and Sydney.
trouble. All the “grower” would have to do
would be to kill and clean the pig—and there
would be his bacon all ready bar the smoking.
But, alas ! the pig experimented upon gave up, on
the third day, an extremely briny ghost, and the
puzzled genius is still wondering where was the
weak spot in his theory.

A big lorry loaded

to crawl through the

and got jammed, and
blocked more busses,
while fresh arrivals
behind piled up and
prevented anything
like a strategic move-
ment to the rear.
The people on the
pavement mostly
wanted to get to the
trams, and the only
way to do so was to
either climb over or
wriggle under the
’bus horses and
they didit. Those
w ho didn’t missed
the trams, as the horses got clear of the rails
before getting on their feet, and the tram-drivers,
anxious to make up lost time, started at top speed,
and sailed off leaving a cursing crowd watching
them helplessly. To ride down Oxford or Pitt on top of a
’bus is like shooting the maelstrom on a cork it is a vast
repetition of thrills.
Was in a George-street tram t’other morning. The
cautious driver slackened off steam every time he neared
a cross-street. A tall, dark passenger gibed at him and
asked him why the Dept, didn’t hire the cars for funeral
processions. The driver shook with anger, but kept his
lower jaw tight. Every few yards the tram had to stop
while lorries, loaded with high-smelling skins and ingots
of copper and cases of merchandise, hauled themselves
slowly out of the way. All these things go along George-
street, for it is the only street that goes right through
Sydney, and George-street is like unto the neck of a
bottle. The tall, dark m»n became frantic. He threatened
to get out and walk. The driver
still kept his lower jaw up firmly.
We stopped eight times between
the Cathedral and Market-street,
to let the huge drays crawl out
of our way. Then we had rather
a good time, and only met five
drays in 100yds. Suddenly, a
nice long reach of empty street
presented itself, and, egged by
the tall, dark passenger, the
driver “ let her go.” The wind
went whistling past our ears,
when suddenly, out of the wall,
as it were, a horse and cab
darted from a narrow lane, that
would disgrace Constantinople,
and, slipping on the streaming
blocks, went down all of a heap
right across our track. The
driver set his teeth, while his
face went white —there was a
suppressed yell on every lip, and
all hinds clutched wildly at
everything and anything. We
catne along on the wings of
lightning, and, just as we seemed
to spring at them, there was a
grating shock —and the outside
passengers spilt all over the
street. The tall, dark man
nearly knocked the car’s front
railing into next century. “He
picked himself up, and seemed
dubious as to whether he should
laugh or cry. The driver broke
out into a furious bark : “ You
wanted her let go, and now,
you, see wotcher got! Serve you
blanky well right, yer for a
howling fool ! I’d have been
reduced by Is. 6d. a diy and put
back to conductor, if I’d bumped
’em ! ” The tall, dark man felt
that he was in the|wrong, and he
apologised and tendered some
coin, but the train-man refused
it indignantly. Evidently, he
was really angry !
“Fancy Free” : Maori-
land Chief-Justice Stout’s
decision that a man is not
drunk so long as he is cap-
able of asking for another
drink, is good. Lately saw,
seated in a chair in an up-
country pub., a very fuddled
gum-digger. His mate was
pouring some beer from a
bottle down the digger’s
throat, and, though unable
to stand, he was just capable
of swearing whenever the
drink spilled over his coat.
Not being speechless, he
could, of course, ask for more
drink ; in fact, I heard him
do it in language I wouldn’t
like to repeat. Yet, accord -
ing to Chief-Justice Stout, he wasn’t drunk, and
he had a legal and moral claim for more whisky.
The Newtown non-sectarian orphanage at
Albury, founded 17 years ago, has in that period
received 255 children, and shows a record of only
one death in the institution. The orphanage is
conducted by the Sisters of Mercy, and receives
no subsidy from Govt.
In Camp: EVENING.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
At Busk there was a whisper in the Trees
That fringe the water-way;
But now the spirit of the lonely bush,
Broods o’er the darkling bay.
Softly from stem to stern the ripples glide
In gleams of flickering fire,
As in our hearts for ever ebb and flow
The pulse-beats of Desire.
No sound disturbs the tenor of my dreams,
Save, through the gathering gloom,
Like cannon from a distant 'battle-field,
The muffled breakers’ ■boom.
Dimly, from out the night, ghost-faces shine,
Old loves, long long forgot,
And old-remembered kisses chill my lips
That once were burning hot.
A cheery hail resounds from shore to shore,
The ruddy camp-fire gleams,
“ Ahoy! Ahoy! What are you loitering for,

handed in ; and in no case will MS. be returned un-
less stamps (of any province) accompany.

The Premier has received the following cablegram from Mr. Chamberlain
“ German Government has requested permission to purchase horses and mules in
Australia for service in China. Her Majesty’s Government would be glad if
your Ministers will afford every facility in their power to agents for German Govern-
ment.”
“Farewell, farewell, my Arab steed !
Go forth in alien lands to bleed,
Or hap’ly die ’mid battle’s roar !
And we will meet — ah !—never more ! ”

Days, months, a year sped quickly by ,
Some tinned stuff “ made in Germany ”
You here behold before him stands ,
He opens it with eager hands,
And when he’d got the cutter through it—
They met again, though jneither knew it!

When there is no Desire for Food there is Need of
KUTNOW’S POWDER, which Restores the Appetite by
Restoring Health and Activity to the Liver.
It matters little how the appetite is tempted, when inclination for food is gone little food is likely to be
eaten and assimilated, and, consequently, the body and brain are insufficiently nourished, and the usual languor
and insomnia, headache, nervousness, drowsiness, and craving for stimulants result. When the appetite for food
has been absent for any length of time it is evidenced by the complexion becoming sallow, dark blue rings form-
ing round the eyes, which become dull and sunken, and by various degrees of emaciation.
A repugnance for food usually arises from some derangement of the liver and other digestive organs, and
hence, in order to recover a vanished appetite, it is necessary to restore the liver, stomach, and kidneys to a
healthy working condition. They must be cleansed and strengthened, and nothing accomplishes this better than
Kutnow’s Improved Effervescent Carlsbad Powder, which possesses all the purifying, healing, medicinal properties
of the noted health springs of Europe, minus their drastic, lowering, nauseous features.
Kutnow’s Powder restores lost appetite by removing the cause. In other words, it expels the impure
matters which have choked up the system and rendered the digestive and assimilative organs disordered and
sluggish, and restores the entire body to a clean, healthy, vigorous condition.
Kutnow’s Powder has a healing, cleansing, and strengthening effect on all the digestive, assimilative, and
excretory organs, neutralising acidity, increasing nutrition, purifying the blood, and enabling those that use it to
work harder, sleep sounder, and enjoy life better.
Take Kutnow’s Powder, and the sick headache, lassitude, nervous exhaustion, depression, low spirits,
drowsiness, biliousness, and the various other symptoms of a disordered digestion will disappear, and you will
receive a new store of strength, health and vigor.
SOLD BY ALL CHEMISTS.
Kutnow’s Improved Effervescent Carlsbad Powder may be obtained in capsuled bottles from all Reliable Chemists and
Medicine Vendors. See that the Registered Trade Mark, “ Hirschensprung ” (or Deer Leap), and the autograph
facsimile signature, “S. KZJTN OW & CO., Ld.,” are on the label and carton. Only these ensure genuineness.
Insist on “KUTNOW’S,” and firmly refuse substitutes. They may be worthless, probably injurious.

The Acetylene Miners’ Lamps, introduced by
the Acetylene Gas Co. of Australia, Ltd., have proved a
great success g,nd are now largely used in the mines
throughout the colony. The company has lately been
rushed with orders for lighting up gold-mining dredges
owing to the many advantages offered- by the light for
that purpose. It would be well for mine and dredge
owners to ascertain full information as to cost, etc., from
the Head Office of the Company, 36 Pitt-street, Sydney.*

POLITICAL
POINTS
Kenneth Mackay’s “Yellow Wave” begins
to roll, and Armageddon seems within measurable
distance.
Ex-Premier Dawson, of Queensland, is
already spoken of as the leader of an all-Aus-
tralian Labor-party in the Federal Senate.
The joke of the month in Queensland is
the plaint of Burns, Philp and Co. that they were
slighted in the matter of a certain Govt, tender.
This is real humour.
Alfred Deakin struck a quaint figure of
speech when he told a Melb. Town Hall crowd
that the local Parliament would have to be their
political skirt while the Commonwealth was their
political toga.
That spineless body, Q. Civil Service
Board, will probably snuff out from November
(only 10 months’ salary was voted last year). The
Board had a stiff backbone for small abuses, but
the very big ones found it as blanc-mange.
Thomas Glassey absented himself from
the llquse on the day of the opening of Q. Parlia-
ments?:" His enemies allege that his absence was
due to'the fact that he couldn’t get anybody to
introduce him. His friends, however, corrected
the error by explaining that he simply couldn’t
come because his new top-hat wasn’t ready.
The academic discussion as to where the
Gov.-General shall plant himself while signing
official documents between now and the establish-
ment of the Federal Govt, suggests that all Acts
of Parliament should wind up with a statement
“that everything in this Act is to be taken in a
commonsense way.” Then if a dispute arises let
it be put to a jury of 12.
The Victorian schools 'were allowed to fall;
into disrepair to save revenue in the days when
revenue was desperately scarce. Now that revenue
is more plentiful the M'Shiels Ministry thinks of
repairing them—with £lOO,OOO of loan-money !
In financial matters this country has about as
much conscience as a South American republic or
a nigger kin£ on the African coast.
A TOUGH GARMENT.
ISAACTON : “ Vy you didn't make a sale to dot
yustomer? ”
NEW SHOPMAN: “ He wanted a pair of moles,
avid we had none to suit him.”
ISAACTON: “ Veil, vot aboud it! Ven I was a
young man, if a yustomer vanted moles and I
got none to suit, s'elp me, I sell him a cross-
cut saw. You'f got to learn peezness.”
More rot ! “N.S.W. Premier Lyne has
promised to adopt a suggestion by Dacey, M.L.A.,
to erect some lasting structure to mark the land-
ing of the Governor-General at Circular Quay.”
In the wordsof Wallington, “ Better not.” Better
wait till a memorial can be erected at the spot
where the last Governor-General stepped off Aus-
tralian soil and left us to govern ourselves without
any flummery.
The attempt to enact in S.A. that a mem-
ber of the Assembly shall not forfeit his seat
through absence if he is away from the country
helping to shoot Britain’s enemies was lost, despite
Govt, support. This looks like another slump in
the contingent business. When it gets down to
punishing the Jingo M.L.A. by forfeiture of billet
and salary when he goes contingenting, a frost
must be setting in.
Frank Farnell, ex-M.L.A., proposes that
N.S.W. candidates for the Federal Parliament
should be provided with a free railway-pass apiece
while canvassing their proposed electorates. The
writer doesn’t actively approve or disapprove. He
merely states that he has some business to do
around Bourke, Cobar, and Tenterfield about that
time, and he reckons it will be a great deal cheaper
to be a Federal candidate than to pay his railway-
fare.
Big John Forrest, despite his solemn
declaration to the contrary, will be a candidate
for the Federal Parliament if Westralia makes up
her mind to join the union, and, judging by his
sudden and complete conversion from parochialism
to nationalism, Hackett, M.A., editor of the
West Australian and bear-leader to Forrest, has
his eyes in the same direction. Hackett in ’77
stood in the Radical interest for Sandridge against
Chief Justice Madden.
“ Kiwi ” from Maoriland :
Another sample of “ British fairplay ” as taught in
Maoriland. Hogg, M.H.R. for Masterton, has been for
years a member of the Reporting Debates Sessional Com-
mittee of the House af Representatives. This year he has
not been appointed there, the Govt, preferring to fill his
place with a member who is known to be in favour of dis-
missing Grey, the chief of the Parliamentary reporting
staff, now on trial before the committee for daring to
write disapproving of the great capitalistic shambles in
S’Africa. Hogg openly declared his belief that Grey, as a
free-born Britisher, had a right to express his views, and
it was apparently thought better to replace him by a man
who could be relied upon to vote for Grey’s dismissal. A
clear case of bench-packing.
It was last week cabled that the Parliamentary
committee had recommended Grey’s dismissal.
G. W. Russell’s Bill authorising women to
sit in Maoriland Parliament and to hold all public
offices has just been rejected by the House of Rep-
resentatives. Old Capt. Russell forgot his usual
ladylike manners over the measure, and denounced
its supporters as “ sloping-shouldered, soft-
hearted, and broad-hipped creatures, more like
women than-men,” and G. W. Russell indignantly
replied that his hips could stand inspection quite
as well as the captain’s if they both had their
clothes off. The captain didn’t offer to undress in
the refreshment-room and settle the question.
The Broad-hipped Brigade mustered 25 votes, and
was beaten by a majority of only five, so its time
may not be far off after all.
M.L. House of Representatives has decided
to sack the chief of Hansard staff for publicly
expressing the opinion that there was a Boer as
well as a British side to the.war. When a shabby
little act of tyranny like this is done the defence
always is: “What other country would allow a
public servant to openly declare his belief in the
justice of an enemy’s cause?” Possibly no other
country would. But that argument merely
amounts to saying: “We are not any meaner or
more tyrannical than Dutchmen, Russians, Por-
tuguese, Spaniards, Turks, niggars and sundries.”
And that isn’t much of a defence. The Briton’s
constant claim is that he is vastly better than all
these ; not that he isn’t any worse.
The M’Lean Govt., having beaten the
motion of want of confidence,
has now a working majority
to tide it on to the general
election. But, as a Liberal
Ministry, it occupies the
uncomfortable position of
being dependent on the
Tories for its daily bread.
The Opposition has neither a
leader nor a policy, and the
disruption of parties is in
favo£ of the present Govt.
A hew Graham Berry would
soon be the uncrowned
king of Victoria, but
they don’t grow Graham
Berrys now. And they
don’t deserve to, con-
sidering how they are
treating the veteran in his
extreme old age.
Maoriland Govern-
ment proposes to adopt an
idea that The Bulletin has
long advocated : National Option in
place of Local Option in liquor
matters. The question of the same
public-houses, or less public-houses,
J>r no public-houses at all will thus be
settled for the whole country by refer-
endum, and if the no-public-liouse
party wins, the undermining three-
penny beer will cease all over Maori-
land. That will be an experiment
worth trying, and worth witnessing.
Local Option, at the best, only meant that
a man aad to fetch.his. beer from- the next town.
It wasn’t Teetotalism—only Pedestrianism.
A N.S.W. Leg. Assembly return shows
the amounts in fees and allowances paid to mem-
bers of the Public Works Committee during the
present session. One member (Laborist
Watson) drew “perks ” at the rate of nearly £4OO
a year, altogether apart from his £330 salary.
Parliamentarians propose to permit a member of
the Federal Parliament to sit also in the local
Legislature. If this idea is carried out it will be
possible for a member holding the dual position
to wriggle on to the P.W. Committee, in which
event the lucky man would look down pityingly
upon a member of a State Ministry whose salary
would not total up to his, and who would have to
work three times as hard for the money.
“ Via ’’ : Compared with the purblind city
fathers (and grandmothers) of Brisbane Municipal
Council, Dogberry was a perfect Solon. Some
years back they had the chance of buying for a
few thousands the rights and assets of the mori-
bund tramway co., but foolishly let it slip ; to-day
that co. and its accrued rights are probably worth
a million. Now, with this shining example before
them, the Council purposes bartering away the
city electric - light privileges for 42 years to a
private co., which generously offers the city in
return a few lamps free for the concession. A few
years hence the Brisbane Dogberries will probably
find that they have fooled away another million.
Then they will no doubt try to give away their
bridge and their river to a syndicate.
A PROCESSION OF THREE.
Thousands of British soldiers have already died
of fever in S’Africa, and over 12,000 have been
invalided home. — CABLE.
Hutchison, M.L.A., of South Australia
has a measure before Parliament providing that
every adult male employed by S.A. Govt, or on
S.A. Govt, contracts shall receive not less th&n7s.
a day. The adult female person appears to be un-
accountably omitted. Considering that she is
often, in the Education Department for instance,
expected to do a man’s work for half a badly-paid
ma,n’s wage surely she wants a minimum wage
quite as much as the superior animal who smokes
and says “damn!”
Brisbane Telegraph’s latest tears and
lamentations are to the effect that the near ap-
proach of Federation has blighted Queensland’s
credit in England, hence its most recent London
loan will cost it £3 10s per cent, per annum. The
poor little paper may dry its eyes and cheer up.
Before Federation was mooted the cheapest loan
Queensland ever floated in London cost about £3
13s. per cent. So, apparently, the approach of
Federation has already lifted up the broken credit
of Queensland a point or two.
N.S.W. Public Service Tender Board deals
with stores amounting to about half-a-million
annually, and its work has hitherto been aided by
the advice of the chief officers of the departments.
These officers used to receive fees for this help,
but lately the occasional guineas have been stopped.
It thus comes to pass that the heads of depart-
ments refuse to give further advice, and the Board
laments. One highly-salaried official says his
time is too limited to allow of his giving any of it
to the Board. But he gave it while he got fees.
The new Leader ofQ. Opposition, William
Browne (otherwise “Billy” Browne), resembles
the late leader, Anderson Dawson, in being the
possessor of a considerable fund of shrewd humor.
Is also similar to Dawson in the matter of a con-
stitutional chest-weakness which often militates
against his political usefulness. Browne is popular
in the House and respected abroad. Is eminently
practical and eminently non-idealistic —the very
antithesis of the Paraguay-pilgrim type. . . .
Dawson’s resignation was a purely voluntary
matter: his Parliamentary colleagues speak of
him most cordially and kindly.
Shark ” . Attorney General Wise has
been away from Sydney for a fortnight—at time
of writing he was still absent. Meanwhile, his
official sanction is, by law, required to the filing
of a statement of claim in Equity praying an in-
junction and immediate relief. It is extremely
important that the injunction be granted at once,
but, as sanction must be personal, proceedings are
delayed for the A.-G.’s return, there being no
Solicitor-General to act for him. Is it not time
that the office of Attorney-General be made
non-political, and that he be compelled to attend
to his duties and earn the salary he receives ?
Yic. Premier M ‘Lean has been explaining
how ex-Premier Turner kept in office five years
and became the Right. Hon. George. Nobody
wanted office at the time. Patterson (Turner’s
rival) had died after being turned out. The
“boom” reaction was getting greater; salaries
had to be cut down and men retrenched, and there
was a likelihood that office at the time would
mean political death. “Both sides of the House
supported Mr. Turner,” says MLean. Of course
they did. Nobody wanted his billet till he had
made things pleasant—and then he was chucked
out. If there hadn’t been depression Turner
wouldn’t have kept office a year. But Hielan’
Mac. had his eye on the billet all the time.
Sydney Telegraph excuses Premier Lyne’s
small apologetic action in partially reforming one
out of Sydney’s 41 municipalities, and letting the
other 40 slide, and hanging up the idea of muni-
cipal consolidation indefinitely, on the ground
that:
At present many of the boroughs are altogether at
variance as to the desirability of establishing a large
council with great powers that shall be something on the
model of the County Council of London.
Are they ? It reads like a weary or incompetent
man’s excuse. Will the Lyiie Govt, draft a
measure for the consolidation of the 41 munici-
palities on a fair, workable and democratic basis,
and then submit it to a referendum of the inhabi-
tants of the 41 municipalities, and see if they are
at variance? The Bulletin ventures to bet it
will be accepted by them all. The councils are
certainly at variance, and there is disagreement
among the mayors, town-clerks, dog-catchers and
inspectors of nuisances—but that isn’t quit# the
same thing.

fore when Vic. Anti-Sweating League

London Outlook proudly sug-
gests, as an idea quite new and
altogether its own, that, in view
of the enormous number of default-
ing trustees, a Public Trust Office,
with a State guarantee behind it,
should be established in England.
It says there is no such thing as
yet in existence, and it has laboriously
thought the idea out all by itself
in its own enormous think-reservoir.
And Maoriland not only thought
out the idea years and years ago, but
has been successfully running a Public
Trust Office for the best part of a
generation.
Sydney Sabbath Times, which seems to
delight in causing G. Republican Dibbs and others
of its pets to look absurd, makes that angry old'
man say last Sunday :
Those of our public men who allowed the provisions
regarding the Capital and the exclusion of New South
Wales to go in the Bill
OUGHT TO BE KICKED TO HADES.
I don’t care who they are. They should have stood up for

Considering that the Capital clauses in the Bill
deliberately include N.S.W., and exclude Vic-
toria, Queensland, and all the rest of the provinces,
this last snort from the profane leader of the
Cabbage-tree mob is as wanting in sense as it is in
good manners.
The state of shock affected by several
“ leaders of opinion ” over the novelties of N.S. W.
Attorney-General Wise’s Compulsory Arbitration
Bill argues a very limited acquaintance with the
economics of industry. Labor legislation is as
old as the British Parliament. The Statute of
Laborers of Edward 111. fixed a wages scale and
punished the employer who offered less and the
employe who claimed more. In 1800 an Act was
passed providing for arbitration between master
and man. Either side could demand arbitration,
and if either objected to the award, fine or im-
prisonment was imposed. In fact the endeavour
to settle disputes between employer and employed
by the machinery of a tribunal which can enforce
its decisions by fines and imprisonment, has for
centuries been considered to be within the sphere
of practical politics by leading thinkers in England;
and France.
T. M. Slattery, of Sydney, lawyer, poli-
tician and anti-Federalist, points out that State
Parliaments can’t enact that their members shall
be ineligible to sit in the Federal Parliament, as
the Federal Constitution gives no State power to
disqualify anybody from sitting in the Federal
Parliament. It is probably true —but it doesn’t
matter in the least. The States have power to
disqualify anybody they please from sitting in
their own State Parliaments. It is illegal
to enact that “a person sitting in the State-
Parliament shall not be a Federal member
Or candidate,” for that is imposing qualifi-
cations and limitations on Federal members. But
it is quite legal if it is put the other way: “A
Federal member or candidate shall not sit in the
State Parliament.” That is only putting re-
strictionson State members. One is-tweedledum,
and the other is tweedledee. Tweedledum is
unlawful.
A letter from Maoriland :
Exit John M’Kenzie. Perhaps one of the saddest
Houses for many years past greeted the reading of poor
Jock M’Kenzie’s valedictory letter to J. G. Ward, convey-
ing Iris resignation. I think it is one of the most intensely
pathetic documents ever read in M.L. Parliament. The
big, rugged, blundering, but able Scotsman whose fierce
hatred of land monopoly and its attendant injustices was.
imbibed.with his first view of a crofter’s eviction in his
native Highlands, writes from his bed of sickness, wasted 1
unto death, but still fiercely militant for his Land policy,
and bitterly bemoaning the fate that “ downed ’’ him in
the midst of fresh efforts for Land Settlement. Seddon
and Ward have for long- past taken the work off M’Kenzie’s.
shoulders and wished to continue this longer, but
M'Kenzie stubbornly and characteristically refuses. He-
pathetically observes“ The days have lengthened into
weeks and the weeks into months. Seddon is ill and I
will-not further hamper him.” He conveys an item of
news not generally known re a practice which might with,
advantage be copied by other Govts. When Ballance first
formed his Liberal administration the Cabinet deliberately
adopted the practice of going about freely among the people
partly that they might judge for themselves the needs of
a district, and partly that the people might freely speak
to them and see what manner of men they were—this in
contradistinction to the “ haw-baw standoffishness” of
previous Governments. Old John exultingly.adds, “ This
practice, whether right or wrong, has become established,
and I do not think any Govt, can change- it now.
The people have got used to meeting Ministers freely
and would resent any change that would make them,
inaccessible.” There is not the slightest doubt that M‘Ken-
zie has been the hub upon which the Land policy
of the present Govt, has revolved, and the weary hours he
spent mastering every little departmental detail relating
to his work have had a lot to do with his final breakdown.
He begs his colleagues to seriously consider the question
of appointing Ministerial Under-Secretaries to relieve
Ministers of detail work. He conveys a note of warning
to Ward, our present robust and energetic Pooh-Bah, who
has been bending Atlas-like under the present big Liberal
burden. The big Scottish chief who invariably entered
upon a debate with his Highland war-cry and a swinging
claymore, careless as to whose head he hit so long as he
could drive home his arguments to burst up big estates
and help closer settlement, will be sadly missed, and
there was scarcely a dry eye when his letter was read.

The case of the girl M'Lean, who died
recently in the plague conveyance at Brisbane,
seems to have been a sad one. Her sister declares
that she herself had a much worse attack of what
appeared to be a bad, feverish cold, which in her
case was cured by simple household remedies.
The younger girl, who afterwards fell ill with
exactly the same symptoms, was a nervous and
delicate creature, and had always been frightened
of plague—which the doctor who was called in
pronounced her to be suffering from. When the
plague ambulance was brought the sick girl be-
came terribly affrighted, and told her sister
that she would die at once if put into the
dreaded conveyance. And, sure enough, she did
die —almost immediately after being placed in the
vehicle, but whether of fear or of plague is hard
to say.

Last week the N.S.W. Commercial Travel-
lers’ Association held its 16th annual dinner, the
president, Mr. G. C. Balls, in the chair. Gov.
Beauchamp was a guest, and in proposing the
president’s health, quoted from the association’s
organ, the Traveller (why not call it the Drum-
mer ?) :
Regret is often expressed by the business community
that Mr. Balls has not entered the political arena,
although he is gifted to a wonderful degree with a know-
ledge of political and social economics. Possibly it is
because of the degeneracy of our governing bodies that
he has held aloof, but this is an unwise policy, especially
when so much is at stake. It devolves upon such men as
George 0. Balls to stem the tide, and the neglect of this
duty to the country cannot possibly be condoned by a
sense of individual responsibility. The future under
federal unity is promising. Let us hope that such bodies
as the Commercial Travellers’ Association, with their in-
domitable pluck and perseverance, wisdom and honor,
will push forward their best men to grip the bull by the
horns, and thwart the plans of retrogression that hold
sway in our governmental centres.
“It devolves upon such men as George 0. Balls!”
How like a commercial-travellers’ organ !

Q. Premier Philp says that his policy for
the coming session is the establishment of one-
man-one-vote and the building of lots of railways.
Where it won’t pay the State to build railways
private syndicates are to be permitted to do so.
Philp, in the guilelessness of his soul, actually
professes to imagine that where the State (which
can raise the money at 3| per cent.) can’t afford to
build a line a syndicate (which pays 5 per cent, or
more for its money) can afford to do it on the same
terms and can make money out of it.
S.M. Herald, on receiving the news that
the Rhodes crowd in S’Africa proposes to repay
Australia for its assistance in the war by robbing
it of some of the best (in a physical sense) of its
population, and is offering inducements to the
Contingenters to remain (some hundreds of the
Australian troopers have already joined the
Rhodesian police):—
The allowing of some thousands of our young men,
specially selected for their physical advantages, to be
lifted bodily out of the province and transported to
another continent was a result we certainly never con-
templated when we took in hand the patriotic business of
sending suitable men to South Africa.
Well, The Bulletin gets ahead of the S.M.
Herald there. It did contemplate that result.
That was why it proposed, when the first Contin-
gent was being formed, to give the unemployed
and the Domain dossers first show.

Mr. William Cornish, late headmaster of
Balmain (Sydney) Public School, now promoted
to an inspectorship, stated at a banquet the other
day that he lost his father when young and had to
sell newspapers in the street. He is a Balmain
native, and received all his education there and at
Fort-street, Sydney.
Mr. A. H. Nelson, who for 33 years has
been in charge of South Grafton (N.S.W.) Public
School, mentioned, at a recent presentation to
him upon his retirement from the_ service, that
during all that time he had not missed a single
day at his post. Surely a unique record for
length of service in one school —and also for what
a Big Scrub Inspector used to call “total abstin-
ence from absence.”

plague-infected quarters of Sydney, Obtainable
