In an Eastern tale, the beautiful Princess
Babe-bi-bobu could not be married unless the
happy man was without scar or blemish, and as
her lover was marked with certain moles it was
necessary to determine whether moles were to be
considered as scars or blemishes. The question,
after much discussion, was referred to the mufti.
These sages handled it, and turned it, and twisted
it, added to it, multiplied it, subtracted from it,
and divided it, debated it fasting and full, nodded
over it, dreamt on it, slept on it, woke up with it,
analysed it, criticised it, and wrote 48 folio
volumes, of which 24 said moles were blemishes
and 24 said they were not. The only conclusion
which they could come to at last was that moles
were moles : and the beautiful princess remained
unmarried. In like manner Melb. Marine Court,
after an exhaustive three months’ enquiry into the
causes of the foundering of the s.s. Glenelg, has
determined that a leak is a leak ; that the vessel
was perfectly seaworthy, that she went down
because of an unaccountable orifice, and that no
one, from the official surveyors to the cabin boy,
was to blame. Thus the public will have to con-
tinue to put to sea, backing its luck, and without
any satisfactory guarantee that rotten ships are
not allowed to leave port.
Infirmities have at last a Conqueror

“The 800 men captured at Lindley and
Roodival, whom the Free State Boers put through
the Drakensberg passes—being unable to provide
them with food as prisoners—were nearly starved
to death when they got to Natal. Their uniforms
had been stolen by their captors, and they were
dressed in filthy and ragged Boer clothes.' —Cable.
Eight hundred hearty British braves
Surrendered to the hungry Boers,
Who sneer at Rulers'of the Waves
And spurn the “ etiquette ” of war :
The captors’ garments, thin and old,
Hung ragged round their shrunken forms.
The captives, smiling at the cold,
Wore woollen winter uniforms.
Those villains bold
Who felt the cold,
And wanted shelter from the storms,
They dared to swap their garments old
For captive “ Tommies’ ” uniforms.
Now when the captors (this was mean)
Had no more food to give away
They bade the soldiers of the Queen
Live on to fight another day.
Then plodding o’er the mountain track
With chilblains on their weary feet
The poor ex-captives wandered back
To where they’d get enough to eat.
Tom, Bill, and Jack
Went toiling back,
Pale victims of their own defeat;
The Boers had put them on the track
That led to Liberty and Meat.
The cable told the shocking tale
Of how the stubborn “ brutal” Boers
Ignore when togs and vittles fail
The etiquette of modern wars,
And Jingo British bosoms throbbed
In Britain and beyond the sea,
The hungry Boers had Starved and Lobbed
Their captives e’er they set ’em free.
Our bosoms throbbed,
We sat and sobbed. I
And heaved a sigh of sympathee .
For those brave fellows “ starved and robbed
To clothe the fiends who set ’em free.

At 10 o’clock p.m. of Friday, 20th July, at
Breelong, N.S.W., a small party of aboriginals,
who had been ring-barking for a Mr. Mawbey,
broke into his house—he being elsewhere at the
time —and, with .tomahawks and native clubs,:
cruelly murdered three young women and a lad of
15, leaving also Mrs. Mawbey and a fourth girl
for dead. The unfortunate females were not out-
raged in any other way, and the only thing dis-
coverable, up to date, in the way of motive is that
Mawbey, it is alleged, had refused to allow the
white wife Qf one of the blacks to associate in-any
way with his own womankind. In any case, the
whole affair—by suddenly presenting to us the
blackfellow in all his pristine savagery—gives a
rude shock to our customary present-day estimate
of him as a harmless and rather comic anachronism,
with a perennial craving for baccaand “tickpen.”
Be this gruesome occurrence, Mr. Harry Stockdale
writes to The Bulletin :
There is little doubt, from the statement of Joe Gover-
nor’s white wife, that the original intention of the blacks
was to kill Mr. Mawbey ; and that, finding other people
in the hut with him, they resolved to slay some of his
kindred, acting thus in accordance with the primitive
aboriginal law that every member of a family or tribe is
responsible for the action of any one member thereof.
As one well acquainted with the aboriginal charac-
ter, I have, personally, no doubt that, rightly or wrongly,
Governor and the others must have considered themselves
as in some way grievously wronged. I also fully believe
the statement of Governor’s wife, that on her husband’s
return to camp he said, “ We’ve killed all the s ;
and if you don’t clear out we will kill you”—-
as showing racial hatred roused to such an
extent that anybody white was in danger of
being murdered by these suddenly “ reverted ” savages.
A somewhat similar escape to that of the little boy Jack
Mawbey occurred nigh on 40 years ago, in Queensland,
when the Dawson River natives attacked the Fraser home-
station. The station was crowded with visitors, princi-
pally women, when a large bodv of blacks broke in and
killed all except one of the brothers Fraser. He escaped
through rolling off between the bed and the wall, after
receiving a club-stroke on the head ; his brother being
killed in the bed. In this case, however, the women were
all outraged prior to death ; and, though nothing of that
sort appears to have occurred at Breelong, in both cases
the heads of the victims were horribly battered in by
native bludgeons, some of which, like the hangeel,
will cut a man down as effectually as a sword’.
Unlike the Chinese, however, the Australian blacks never
torture. The man Governor, by the way, has white blood
in him ; and, if he is anything like as active and daring as
some of the sort I have known, he may take a lot of
catching; but, for so far, the murderers haven’t shown
any special activity or cunning. And finally, in view of
the many Australian outrages by whites upon whites, it
is preposterous to talk of this crime as meaning a general
uprising in the district (of about 20 or 30 inoffensive
natives !) to exterminate the whites.
[Since above was in type, news has come along of
at least one more murder by the dusky des-
peradoes, who, as they are “in for it” in any
case, are probably now “letting themselves go.”]

“The Times” Enterprise
It would be difficult to overestimate the value
of the _ service which the London Times is
doing in the interests of general education
in New South Wales by placing within reach
of even the humbler homes that greatest of
all reference libraries, the Encyclopaedia Britan >
nica. ' One of the leading educators of our times
has said : —;“ In this age of specialists, when each
separate branch of human knowledge is traced to
the farthest limits that a lifetime of study and in-
vestigation can penetrate, true education has come
to mean, not the storing of one’s mind with facts
and theories, but rather the training of one’s mind
to a proper use of the ‘ tools ’ of information. The
truly educated man is therefore one who knows
where to find the answer to every question that
may arise in his daily reading or in the pursuit of
his own business or profession.” If true education
consists “not in the storing of one’s mind with
facts and theories, but rather in the training of
one’s mind to a proper use of the ‘ tools ’ of in-
formation,” then every home which boasts the
possession of the Encyclopaedia Britannica becomes
a, university, for this international standard
library of reference is in very truth the epitome of
all human knowledge. When the Times offers to
place this library in any house on the preliminary
payment of a single guinea it is putting within the
reach of all the best products of the best minds of
our age.
Melb. Australasian, in a brief account
of Sir Robert Hart’s life and services in China
(after quoting a statement that Hart is a “ typical
Irish gentleman ”) winds-up with the remark that
it is a matter for pride that this remarkable man
should be an Englishman. If a man is of small
account, he remains, for the English commentator,
Scotch or Irish as the case may be ; but, let him
make a name, and he at once becomes English—
“ in a comprehensive sense, doncherknow.”
Somebody sends The Bulletin the draft
of a bill to be laid before Q. Parliament, which
preambles that* “ the punishment of death is irro
vocable, and therefore the trials of all persons
charged with offences punishable by death should
be conducted with the utmost care and delibera-
tion ”; and proceeds to enact—
No sitting or continuation of a sitting of the Court for
the trial of any person for any offence which by law is
punishable by death shall be held except between the
hours of nine o’clock in the forenoon and six o’clock in
the afternoon : Provided that if the examination of any
witness has been commenced before the hour of six
o clock in the afternoon such examination, and any cross-
examination and re-examination consequent thereon may
be continued and concluded after that hour, but the
Court shall thereafter forthwith adjourn until its next
sitting day.
And if the lawyers don’t oppose it, this excellent
precaution may quite likely become law, as a sop
to the Cerberus of Honest Government, which
does not conflict with the Ministry’s commercial
speculations. By the way, who introduces the
measure ?

SPORTING NOTIONS.
Veteran pug “Mick” Dooley (now verging on
44) and Bill Doherty—who knocked a heap of self-esteem
out of 6ft. 3in. Peter Felix last week and gathered in Austra-
lian premiership bruising honors—are matched to fight in
Sydney, Aug. 6. Same couple have already come together
four times, .and each has two wins to credit. Dooley
could only get three weeks to train, which writer doesn’t
think near enough, especially as Doherty is in finer trim
than ever before. The champion wouldn’t give a day
longer, and told Dooley he must either fight then or not
at all. Looks as if Bill is getting even. A few months
ago Dooley licked big Fremantle navvy Anderson.
Doherty challenged the winner. Dooley made it a sine
qua rum that they must shape-up three weeks later.
Doherty demurred—he couldn’t get into fix by then.
Dooley was obdurate. The battle eventuated within
the time stated, and the Bth round proved the end of
Doherty’s tether : he had to cry a go. Positions are
changed now : this time Doherty is getting Dooley on the
hop.
* * * *
“Pads.”: Announcement that Dooley and
Doherty are to have ’em on for N.S.W. heavyweight
supremacy sets one thinking. Though not actually
champion, Dooley was close up before Jackson, Slavin, or
grizzly Goddard were heard of ; he subsequently formed
the .stepping-stone to world-wide repute for each in turn.
They went abroad, he stayed at home, yet Dooley (eldest
of the lot) has outlived them all, and is in the thick of it
still, while Peter, Paddy, and Joe have dropped away,
and are among the one-timers.
* » * •
Peter Jackson had a few words to say at settling
over the recent Doherty-Felix fight. “ Manly fighters
were only found administering blows so long as they
were in danger of receiving blows themselves. They
never punched an opponent who was beyond punching
them.” The famous black spoke from his heart there.
When he won the Australian belt from Lees, and had the
latter sick and sorry, he stopped punching and begged of
Lees’ seconds to take him away. Same thing occurred
with “Chocolate” Godfrey and Patsy Cardiff in’Murica
and Paddy Slavin in England. Peter’s proudest boast is
that he never hit a man more than necessity demanded.
• * • *
Preston-Mick Dunn match at Nathan’s Hall
(Melb.), in connection with which the purse (£5O) was pro-
vided and profits raked in by a local penny weekly, drew
a big house, but didn’t please 90 per cent, of ring-siders.
As often here stated, Preston’s fighting-kit consists of one
hand only—the left—(t’other might just as well he tied
behind for all the use it is) bulk and bull-headed aggres-
siveness. Dunn, clever as any we have in the fighting
game, took no chances but contented himself mostly by
standing off and jabbing when opportunity allowed.
Once a section of the crowd whistled the “ Dead March”
—there wasn’t enough gore in it for them —albeit Dunn
gashed Preston’s left eye-brow in round two. The Bth
term opened with Dunn making play from the jump,
which condition of things completely overwhelmed the
always-beef.v Fred., who dropped his hands ere too much
had been done and cried enough. One report was
amusing—it said : “ Preston did not at all box up to form.
He was slow and unwieldy, and was very backward in
the punch which has knocked all the wind out of
many of his opponents.” Fancy the lumbering Preston
being anything but slow and unwieldy in front of a
champion boxer like Dunn ! There’s a wide difference
between the class of men Preston has beaten and the
crack Sydney middle-weight—old and all as he is. On
Felix’s showing with Doherty t’other night, writer has an
idea that the black-pelted long ’un would go near coming
another cropper if he faced Dunn—difference in height
and weight notwithstanding
• * ¥ *
“Brum.”? Notice erstwhile - Captain’s Flat
(N.S.W.) miner Dan O’Connell is challenging Cribb, and
offers to post £5O. Latter has evidently left off reading
the papers, for he says nothing in response. Dan is just
the sort of chap to whip the Maorilander—he’s as strong
as a horse, always has a weighty punch in stock, and can
bullock with the best at that game. Jack Conlon beat
Dan twice, but the last go proved the toughest of tussles.
Jack stood off his man, contenting himself with long-
range shots, otherwise he would have gone under. Cribl)
isn’t clever enough for that, besides he’s about 101 b.
lighter than Dan.
• * * •
Cables, though telling that Australian champion
sprinter Rowley ran 4th in the British premiership this
month, 3rd in the 100-metres, and 3rd in the 6'J-metres
events of Paris Olympian Games, are very silent re details.
Athletic authorities here can only guess as to who finished
in front of Rowley, but general opinion favors the Yankee
contingent, which was strong numerically and athletically
and embraced reps, from every big scholastic institution.
Bar the’Murkans, the only man likely to give Rowley a
close call would be the Bengal amateur Pritchard.
» * • *
Joe Darling has been treating for the purchase
of a station property near Albury (N.S.W.) ever since his
return from England, but as owners declined his price he
bought a sheep-run in North Tasmania. He writes to
friends in Adelaide that unless he can get practice big
cricket will see him no more.
’ * * *
Darling’s retirement is a greater blow than that
Australia suffered when Murdoch gave up the game—for
J6e' is not 30 yet, and may be considered in his prime.
When a college boy in ’B5 Darling made 252 for Prince
Alfred against St. Peter’s College, but he went up country
on his father’s farm and he hardly played again till ’93,
when he returned to Adelaide and started his cricket busi-
ness. In ’94 he made 117 for S.A. against Stoddart’s team
in Adelaide. His average in big matches is 38 (4681 runs
for 134 innings) and in interprovincial cricket 33 (1560
runs for 49 innings). He has made 11 centuries in big
cricket and 10 in other matches. The biggest score of his
career was made last season for his club, against Port
Adelaide, when he topped his college record score hy 7
runs. Writer reckons the best innings he ever saw was
Darling’s 178 in the third Test against Stoddart’s team on
Adelaide Oval in ’97. When Joe was 94 he completed his
century by lifting Briggs out of Adelaide Oval for six,
and the ball went fully 30vds. into the adjoining planta-
tion. S.A. will suffer most from Darling’s retirement, for
Giffen is declining, Jones has visibly deteriorated and
Clem Hill is right off. As for Australian cricket, the
place cannot easily be filled of the man who had top aver-
age with the last team in England and the highest aggre-
gate of runs by any Australian in the Old Country.
Darling, by-the-way, has ever been an absolute teetotaller
and non-smoker.
¥ ¥ ¥ ¥
Darling was within an ace of throwing up
cricket on the eve of the last Australian tour in England.
He felt very much the attitude of the S.A. public towards
him over Geo. Giffen’s exclusion, but what upset the sen-
sitive Joe most of all was the hooting of the crowd when
he came out to bat against, the Rest of Australia in Ade-
laide. In addition, one press writer, who strongly cham-
pioned Giffen’s cause, seemed to have carte blanche as re-
gards personal abuse of the left-hander. The feeling in
Adelaide over the Darling-Giffen trouble was so strong at
• one time that numerous cricket clubs actually boycotted
Darling’s sports business.
**. • ¥
Cable tells that Oxford ’Varsity and Worcester-
eounty bat R. E. Foster compiled 102 (not out) and 136 in
successive innings for Gentlemen v. Players, last week—
the second time in present season that Foster has hit up
two centuries in a match. Playing for Oxford v. last
Australian 11 Foster shaped brilliantly for 80. Foster is a
, man who has his days ont. Last year (for Worcester), in
one match, 134 and 101 (not out) stood opposite his name.
¥ ¥ ¥ *
Australian tennis-champion Kearney made his
mark at ’Ome quickly. Last mail tells that he figured
among contestants for Whitehouse Cup (virtually Scottish
supremacy) at Edinburgh and met holder (Rowan) in the
final ties. Kearney scored in first set 6—l; and Rowan
took the second 7—5. Kearney got home in third hy
6—l; and fourth by 7—6. Kearney and a fellow
Australian, J. G. Glassford, subsequently gathered in the
Gentlemen's doubles premiership.
* • • •
Second match N.S.W. v. Q. (Saturday) drew
7000 to Sydney Cricket Ground despite adverse weather.
Cornstalks won by 11 to 9. Both teams played with nerve
and vigor all through. N.S.W. defence was strong to a
man. Q. scrum-work showed a falling off compared with
that of two previous matches, but forwards of either side
were well up to the mark. Sodden and slippery turf con-
sidered, the match was very fast and exciting, and indi-
cated that N.S.W. will need to send her very best to
Brisbane in September.
* » * *
Saturday’s football struggle was the 42nd ’tween
Q. and N.S.W. The latter has a2to 1 majority of wins—
-26 to 13. Three matches were drawn. Initial match
eventuated at old Sydney Association Ground, 18 years
since, when home side won by 28 to 4.
* * * *
Much jealousy has arisen among the Sydney
Electorate football clubs owing to what they consider the
disproportionate representation of North Sydney club in
the interprovincial matches, that club being only one point
above Glebe in the local premiership competition and
two above Western Suburbs. N. Sydney has five members
selected to play in the first encounter against Queensland,
six in the second, and five in the match against the
Metrop. Union ; altogether half of their first 15 were
chosen to represent the province. The credit of booming
his club is due to J. R. Henderson, so long boss of North
Shore football in the old Pirate days ; he appears to
have the largest say in the selection committee. In the
final fixture the country players were allotted three
places, North Sydney six, and the rest of the Sydney
clubs six among them. The Western Suburbs barrackers
are the most annoyed, as their club, though well up in
the badge competition, did not have a solitary representa-
tive. They sarcastically refer to the N.S.W. team as
“ North Sydney, with a couple of pick-ups.” But the
result of the three contests justifies the Selection Com-
mittee.
# • * *
Cabled last week that little, white-headed Jack
Hellings, erstwhile of Woolloomooloo Bay, and once first-
flight Australian sprint swimmer, won the 500yds. City of
London championship in 5.40 1-5. Judging by footnotes
to the wire in various dailies, writers are mystified over
the 5.40 1-5, in face of the fact that the world's prof, best
is 6.281 and amateur 6.45, and blame the cable-man for'
blundering. One Sydney paper took it upon itself to
make the time 7.40 1-5. Truth of the matter is (and few
seem to know it) that the City of London championship
is swum in the Thames with a strong current; hence the
speed, which (under the conditions) would, of course,
never be allowed as a record. This is the third time in
succession Hellings has owned the same honor, and
always as a member of the fashionable Ravensboume
(London; S.O. In ’9B he travelled much faster, and got
home with a lot to the good—the figures then being
5.30 4-5—9 2-ssec. quicker than the feat that has proved
so bewildering to many people.
* * * *
Rosebud, the rank outsider who pulled off the
Grand National Steeplechase at Flemington, was a dis-
puted case in Melb. law-courts a while ago. The mare’s
original owner, a policeman, sued the next purchaser (J.
Burke) for breach of agreement after she had won at Men-
tone. The action failed, and Burke suDsequently sold
Rosebud for £35 to her present owner. Seems that this
same Rosebud won a number of up-country races what
time Burke was training her for the policeman.
*** , *
£76 div. was paid for 10s. invested on Rosebud,
winner of V.R.O. G.N. Steeple, by the “ tote” promoted
among the members of Melb. Commercial Travellers’ Club.
* * • »
Dear Bulletin.—You ask, “ What would Rose-
bud have paid on the‘tote’?” Well, on a well-known
Sydney “ tote ” Rosebud did pay (paying first, second and
third) over £9 for 55., making her odds—pay first only—
about 75 to 1. Aquarius, too, on same “ tote ” paid over
£6 —first only would mean about 50 to 1. Nobody on the
course could have got more than 33 to 1— the Leger
would probably have had to take 20’s or 25’s.—G.F.B.
* * * m
“ Steve” Callinan established a record at New-
castle (N.S.W.) last week, when he “ rode the pro-
gramme”—five winners in five races—three favorites and
two second favorites. The starting prices were 2to 1,
4 to 1, 5 to 2, 7 to 2, and “ even money ” in the respective
races. Ikey Mo will doubtless not forget Callinan for a
while—especially at Newcastle.
» * » *
Bookmaker Bill Wyatt won the Vic. Club
Billiard Tournament the other day. There was not much
straight out wagering, for the Israelites had not yet re-
covered from the shock last year’s winner (Prosper) gave
them, and they were afraid to risk many pounds against
anybody's chance. As a bettor on billiard-matches and
tournaments Ikey Mo is seen at his worst.
* * * *
“Jim Crow”: Already noted as a matter-of-
fact Ikey Mo’s price about the Grand National “ double ”
was only 100 lo 1 in most quarters.

The much-travelled Paris is again in work, as
sound as a bell, and people in the know say that he may
again pull off a big handicap coup. What a record it
would be!

* • * *
Frank Lawry’s Bill in M.L. House of Reps.,
establishing State-guaranteed lotteries—reputable men
being licensed to promote them under bond—has once
again been defeated—by 11 votes. Lawry thinks that the
large sum posted away every year should be retained in
the country.
* • * »
“ Leash ”: Recent Australian Waterloo Cup
decided at Moonee Valley (Vic.) was the first properly so-
called since the latest revival of coursing. N.S.W. and
Victoria are the only provinces in it. S.A. wouldn’t join.
Winner (Transit) is in her third season and was specially
kept from previous year—so were others. Transit has
run 20 courses, and only failed to raise a flag in two.
Worth mentioning that in the so-called Australian Cup at
Bendigo, last season, she and Procyon were drawn
together and latter won. Strangely enough same pair
met again in initial round of Waterloo Cup and tables
were turned. On night of draw hundreds to 4 were
offered against Transit, but her owner (said to be a
Corryong selector) wanted the odds to 2J, and not being
able to get that price he allowed his bitch to run “ loose.”
Others were not so foolish. As evidencing what fine
fettle the hound was in, it might be mentioned that ere
the first course came on she scaled exactly 501b5., and
only lost a few oz. right through, though some of the
tests were severe enough, as most of the hares were
strange to the Plumpton. Veteran mother province-
courser Sam Bladon (Rooty Hill) appropriated the purse
and plate with Booty and his sister Beeline respectively.
Same sport owns Braddon’s Blot, winner of recent bif
N.S.W. N.C.A. Challenge Stakes.
* * *
John Roberts, who can still speak as boss artist
.at the game saws bias of jealousy, reckons young Steven-
son will be the English billiard champion anon. The com-
paratively new star promises to excel Dawson, says “old
man ” Roberts, and to anyone capable of judging Daw-
son’s play in Australia by comparison with the “ form ” of
Roberts a few years previously, there was evidently
plenty room for a Stevenson to come between them.
Dawson, for one thing, lacked the reach necessary for
many of those nursery cannons which the big champion
could manipulate without requiring the “rest.” But
how about a future Australian champion of the 12 x 6ft.
game ? One time it looked as though the climate was not
favorable to the development of delicate skill and cold
nervy temperament in billiardists, but the steady pro-
gress of Weiss and Co. has almost disposed of the climate
argument. After all, the deftest hand workers in the
world are native to hot countries.
• » * •
“ Alex M.” : To “ Theo B.” (B. 21/7/’00) I ss*y
ditto ! These hustles-for-a-knock-out (I won’t call them
“fights”) are weary exhibitions for those who remember
the days of clean, straight hitting by men who stood up
to it like men, instead of “ mooching ” and crouching and
slinking round after this knock-out, which, as “ Theo.”
says, is mostly part, and often whole, “fluke.” This is
doubtless as good a way as any other to decide who shall,
scoop the pool; but, as boxing, it is miserable ; while, as
sheer “ fight,” it is as likely to give the victory to the
cocktail as to the game-un. Corbett’s recent showing
with Jeffries was a return to the science of boxing —as
distinguished from the pitiful art of swing-sneaking ; and
with such splendid head and foot-work and straight-out
hitting as Corbett put-up, combined with a little more
power behind the blows than the Pompadour appears to
possess, a man would, at anything like equal weights,
simply wipe-out anything we’ve seen here lately—
phenomenon Cribb not excepted. Cribb’s only a
phenomenon, anyhow, because he has never yet struck a
high-class out-fighter, with legs and a head' on him as
well as things to punch with.
• *• » «
People who knew, smiled at the prospect of
sending sprinter Rowley to England—even though the
English amateurs are now somewhat below old standard.
It was a tax on the sporting community, and engineered
by people who have an axe to grind in booming the game.
Owing to times credited him by watch-holders—bunglers
or axe-grinders—Hempton of Maoriland appeared to be
faster than Botany and Carrington pros. Yet
“Wally” Williams donkey-licked him in a
private spin. The axe-grinders carefully kept
this out of print. So Hempton was soon after
sent to England at considerable cost, and did noth-
ing to speak of.
* * * *
And people who know smile at the lOsec. for
100yds. credited to Rowley and Co. At one time it used
to be 10 1-5, whether the course was good or bad, or the
day wet or fine. When London Referee was started it
offered £lOO for any man in the world who could
run 100yds. in lOsec., with this proviso, that the
timekeeper must be—not the amateur official—but one
who held the watch at the big Sheffield handicaps.
Petley, of Finchley, the second-speediest sprinter ever
seen in England, on public form at any rate, was specially
trained for the event, but his party wanted a second try
in case weather, &c., proved unfavourable, and the affair
fell through. Petley, of course, would have laughed at
doing 120yds. in 12secs.—he must have run 126yds. in
12secs. when second to Hutchens in Doncaster Handicap
of 1877—but even-time for the shorter distance is another
matter. While a runner can keep at top speed he is
making up for loss in first 10yds., hence the better time
result.
* ♦ • V
Tas. turf lost a strong supporter through the
sudden death of Mr. R. G. Talbot (Malahide) recently.
Deceased was 44, and had been prominent in racing 20
years. He owned an interest in Darebin when it won
V -R.C. Derby in ’Bl. Caulfield Cup winner, Blink Bonny,
was his, but he only landed £2OOO, as, though previously
he had determined to throw in for a big stake, a bad gal-
lop stopped the commission. Lord W’ilton—-beaten by
Blink Bonny—would have suited Mr. Talbot much better.
Same man owned Sheet Anchor, but could do no good
with him—he was sold to Mr. Loughlin for 1000 gs., and
soon after won Melb. Cup. Mr. Talbot had a lot of other
nags which did fairly well.
» * * *
A notable Yic. convert to the tote faith is
Purves, Q.O. At a recent sporting gathering Purves ad-
mitted that he had been bitterly opposed to the machine
before he found out his mistake. Whereupon lawyer
Croker called upon Frank Madden to likewise discover
his mistake, and “ recant his old-fashioned notions,” but
the burly Tory was not to be drawn out.

N.S.W. Public Service Tender Board—
the latest attempt to check extravagance in the
public departments, more especially m the inter-
nal expenditure on stores and stationery has
always been in a chronic state of difficulty over is
secretary. The original salary as fixed was LlOU
a year, and two or three occupants of the billet
have made a more-or-less failure of it. ihe l ui>
lic Service Board has made this an excuse for
doubling the salary attached to the
ditionally, of course, on the approval of Parlia-
ment. The new secretary is a high official from
one of the leading Ministerial departments, and
it is a well-known secret that the reason of this
transfer is the friction which exists m the depart-
ment concerned between the transferee and the
permanent head. The Service is chuckling over
the transaction.

The Drought in Kanakaland.
A pastoralist to The Bulletin oh the Queens-
land drought:—
To the ruinous '6B and ’B4 droughts, unhappy Mon-
golia has now added the big thirst of 1900 ; and of the
trinity the last is the worst. In the back-country the
battle put-up by squatters and cockies in the strenuous
attempt to save their stock is of the pluckiest. Modern
conditions have so changed the land since the droughts of
other times that the fight is now with previously untried
weapons—railway-fodder-importation among them—and
no one can say how far the new mode of defence makes
for success or failure until balancing-day, and that will be
the Lord knows when. About the Mitchell district,
under normal conditions one of the richest areas of pas-
toral country in the province, the destruction is fearful,
and the losses enormous. The grim misery of the gaunt,
naked, rainless downs, with their parched, cracked
bosoms, hangs like a death-pall over all, and gives point
to the drovers’ laconic summary, “ The run is like the
road." Mile after mile, stretch after stretch, the same
lurid horror grips the soul, and bony death stands guard
over heaps of dead sheep, cattle, horses, and even mar-
supials. But the miracle is, not in the dead, but in the
living—cramped into paddocks, existing in dumb agony
on what short grey tussocks of dried herbage remain,
and kept alive principally by the water from the artesian
bore. The rainfall for this district for the 17 months
ending last May was 12'67, or an annual fall of B’BB.
(roughly, 2-sths of the annual rainfall for the previous 20
years, notwithstanding the fact that this latter period in-
cluded the ’B4 thirst and that the 17 months covered the
two seasons at the begining of ’99 and the present year!)
And Fate, with her blind-eyed caprice, threw even this
small fall in scattered showers of no more use to the coun-
try than a cup of water would be to fill a tank. When the
blacksoil country is parched, any fall less than an inch is
less than valueless, and yet in the midst of this plain of
death two stations far apart had each copious falls, one of
them registering five inches in a single night. The latter
flourishes in magnificent harvest, while in a paddock
three miles off thousands of sheep are imprisoned by the
drought and taking their chance of life or death where
they stand. The large waterless areas which could be
formerly used for a short season to shift the stock on to
are all gone, talcen-up and improved, and the initiation of
artesian wells has actually tended to intensify the drought
horrors. Bores, wells, and tanks, in fact, have made
every inch of country available for stocking (and over-
stocking;), with the result that there is not a single pad-
dock that lias not been eaten clean out. The broad acres
of grass met with here and there in the droughts that
have gone before are missing this time, and, by contrast,
the unbroken nakedness of the present is a fearful spec-
tacle. Said one large owner : “ Why, in the *B4 drought
we had bush fires. Now you couldn’t make a bush fire
with 20 gallons of kerosene.” Also, the sweep of the
present drought is wider, and there can be no travel-
ling for grass and water. There is nowhere that
breadth of common-lands that proved such a source
of salvation in previous droughts ; the roads are
as hopeless as the runs, and the runs are hopelessness
itself. The aggregate number of sheep carried at normal
times in the Mitchell District was 1,860,000, but at pre-
sent there are only 670,000. Thus the runs are now only
carrying 86 p.c. of their ordinary complement, a reduc-
tion which corresponds closely with the decreased rainfall
for the last year-and-a-half, as compared with the average
of the previous 20 years. It is estimated that but 260,000
are on agistment at the coast; and, reckoning the sales
made at the beginning of the squeeze, a fair estimate of
the dead loss is 45 p.c The sheep remaining on the runs
also are hopeless frameworks, only kept alive by the
costly process of carting fodder to them for, in some in-
stances, 50 miles. Then there is the cost of transhipping
to the coast, the feeding at the railway-station before
they are sent (rendered necessary by the insufficiency of
rolling-stock); cost of agistment (£l2 per month per 1000
in most cases), and shepherding. Also the losses at the
coast from weather-conditions to which the sheep are un-
used ; the loss of the annual increase and the wool-
harvest, and that from the poor quality and light weight
of the fleeces that will be shorn. The railway figures for
the carriage of fodder give an idea of the expense of
hand-feeding the sheep. For the nine months preceding
May of last year 4942 ions were sent west for feed pur-
poses, but for the same period to May of this year 11,430
tons were sent. And—jade Fortune again ! —the small
owner, who was best prepared to. resist the siege, is the
biggest sufferer. The large stations, with every' foot
stocked to the fullest, were the first to feel the' pinch
through being eaten-out, and they moved first, grabbing
all available means o£. saving the stock. This was easy of ,
accomplishment, the runs being open, agistment cheap,
and the cost of shifting comparatively low. But the ;
lightly-stocked owners held out until a “ perish ” resulted, i
Later, when the brazen sky shall at last yield its
moisture, not much of a pull-round can result for a long,
long time ; and the country will be taxed, even with the
rains falling, to maintain the sheep left upon it. The
fearful spell of dry weather will thin out cruelly the rich
grasses, and, according to one old battler “ It’s
a piece of good fortune for the country that the
drought decimates the flocks, otherwise one big dry
season would rob the western downs of their best grasses
without a hope of recovery.” Nature provides that the
flocks recover their numbers as quickly as the earth re-
covers its powers of sustenance; but, this notwithstanding,
the sheep-industry has received a knock-down-blow.
Very little breeding can be done owing to the high price
ewes will be at, and as the products—wool, mutton,
tallow and sheepskins—will not rise, buyers of fresh stock
will be scarce. It will be a waiting game for all hands,
big and little, and the men who are threatened with total
ruin are facing the prospect with the pluck that is
deserving of reward.
The country far back from Winton has suffered in a
much greater degree than the Mitchell district, but as the
losses on the Barcoo side have not been so great, the
Mitchell offers a fair average of the existing state of
affairs.
“Fab. ” : That Tientsiu-Taku line of 30
odd miles opened within the last six years, is not
the first railway built in China. As an infant in
arms (about 1871) I journeyed from Shanghai to
Woosung, 13 miles, in absolutely the first train to
run in China. After a short and inglorious career
of a few months the local mandarins bought up
the plant, lock, stock, and barrel, and carted same
to Formosa, where in the jungle to this day are
rusting away thousands of pounds worth of ma-
terial. The reason alleged for this was that the
native carriers would be deprived of their liveli-
hood, but the real reason was that the local man-
darins feared the thin end of the wedge of civilisa-
tion—now, by the way, about to be hammered home
by 250,000 men
The Victorian Marine Court, after months
of hard mental struggle, has settled the Glenelg
problem. It finds that the vessel was quite sea-
worthy, and that it sunk owing to an extensive
leak, located in some place unknown to the Court,
and arising from causes which the Court was un-
able to ascertain. Also that “no blame attaches
to anybody.” Which, being boiled down, reads:
“’We find that a perfectly sound vessel broke up
suddenly.” This is about equivalent to a Coroner s
verdict: “The mature judgment of this Court is
that a perfectly healthy man fell down dead.”
A lady signing herself “Ixe” discourses to
The Bulletin on the ears of two honest N.S.W.
politicians who have thriven on the game :
At a woman’s franchise meeting other night ! sat at
back of platform behind lawmakers O’Sullivan and
M‘Gowen. Quite missed the political aspects of the meet-
ing through musing on the intellectual significance of
these gentlemen’s ears. There was, as the cables say, “a
large supply in sight.” O’S’s. auditoriums (a much nicer
word than “ lugs ”) are of the kind approved by Edison.
Very long, somewhat narrow, set close and perpendicular
to the head ; long, tough-looking lobes. When a man
conies to Edison seeking work he always looks first at his
ears, and always vainly for this type of ear. Men so eared
don’t work under other men. They are masters. Mr.
M’Gowen’s pair are unique. Set low, like a cocker
spaniel’s, and standing out from his head in several differ-
ent, but all obstinate, ways. They suited M’G.’sgreat voice
and shoulders and mat of black hair, but they didn’t suit
a friend of the female franchise. If those ears could be
tongues for a minute they would say “squaws” quite
loud. I looked up Max Nordau’s chapter on ears in
“ The Degenerates,” and find that M‘G. is saved mentally
and morally because his pair have escaped a certain angle
.n the setting.

THE OFFER.
The sensation of the bookselling season in Lon-
don, the remarkable offer of “ The Times ’ Re-
print of the “Encyclopaedia Britannica” (9th
edition), is already known to residents in Lew
South Wales who read “ The Times.” No less
than 15,000 copies of the massive work were
printed, but applications were received in rapid
succession for more than 26,000.
Widespread disappointment was occasioned by
the enforced refusal of a great number of orders,
and residents in New South Wales were, owing to
the delay in communicating with London, pecu-
liarly unfortunate in this respect.
What “The Times” did for residents in Eng-
land it now proposes to do for residents in New
South Wales. Arrangements have been made for
a limited number of copies for distribution in New
South Wales. The price and terms upon which
these copies may be obtained are stated in detail
below, and immediate application should be made
by persons who desire to avail themselves of this
extraordinary opportunity.

To select, in this fashion, the names of but one
contributor out of every score who collaborated
upon the “ENCYCLOPAEDIA BRITANNIC A”
is not an altogether satisfactory process. Yet, in
reading over these names, one is at any rate im-
pressed by the fact that the contributors to the
“ ENCYCLOPAEDIA BRITANNIC A ” are not
merely specialists with great information, but
original thinkers. They are men who have them-
selves influenced our thoughts and beliefs, have
directed, even created, contemporary taste and
learning.
PAMPHLET POST FREE.
The “ ENCYCLOPAEDIA BRITANNICA ”
is the only work which can fairly be described as
a complete library in itself, and to own it is, as
Mr. Gladstone said, “ to acknowledge one’s self as
recognising the best there is in literature.” To
have such a work always at hand is a silent invita-
tion to wholesome reading and high thinking.
Only an actual examination of the volumes them-
selves, only actual use from day to day, can dis-
close that. Yet, for the information of the reader,
if you are unacquainted with this splendid work,
there will be sent, post free, upon application, a
handsome pamphlet containing brief extracts from
some seventy or eighty notable articles contained
in the “ ENCYCLOPAEDIA BRITANNICA,”
together with fine coloured plates and other speci-
mens of the ten thousand illustrations with which
the “ENCYCLOPAEDIA” is adorned. The
cover of the pamphlet is decorated with vignette
portraits of thirty-six notable contributors.

MONTHLY PAYMENT.
The instalment system of selling hooks is by no
means a new one, but the plan of sale adopted by
“ The Times ” (London) is a direct inversion of the
familiar process. The old theory of selling a large
book in parts was that the purchaser received the
volumes in driblets, and paid for them in drib-
lets. This plan apparently gave the purchaser a
great advantage—the convenience of gradual pay-
ments —but the gain was only a nominal one. He
did not have to pay t’.ie purchase price in one
lump sum ; but, on the other hand, he got no real
value for his money until he had completed his
payments, for the incomplete parts gave him no
more than a mockery of possession.
“ THE TIMES ” presents the convenience and
eliminates the inconvenience of the old process.
The complete work is delivered as soon as the
purchaser begins to make his real payments; the
only postponement in the transaction is for the
purchaser’s benefit; he enters into immediate and
complete possession of the work, and pays for it
at leisure.
Special attention is directed to the fact that a
stock of the Encyclopaedia Britannica is carried in
Sydney for the filling of immediate orders, thus
saving the purchaser the carriage cost from
London, as well as avoiding the necessary delay
in receiving the work that has heretofore prevailed
when orders were sent to London.
Readers who file their copies of The Bulletin,
and desire to leave this page uncut, may, upon
application to the Manager of “The Times,”
obtain an Order Form similar to that here printed.
THE NATION’S BIG: BOOK.
The “ENCYCLOPAEDIA BRITANNICA”
(Ninth Edition) consists of 24 volumes of 850
pages each, and of a 25th volume containing an
elaborate index to the whole and a detailed list of
contributors. The volumes are Ilf inches in
height, 94 inches in depth, and 2A inches in thick-
ness. The Full Morocco and Half Morocco bind-
ings are of genuine goat-skins, sumach-tanned.
This leather, clear in colour, elastic and soft, yet
firm and fine in grain and texture, “resists wear
and tear and the action of gas and heated air
better than any other leather.” (Ency. Brit. vol.
xiv. p. 538.) The colour of the Half Morocco is a
deep maroon, relieved by the gold stamping on the
back. The Full Morocco binding is of high-class
leather, dark green in colour, with rolled gold
edges, and the Royal Arms stamped in gold on
the side.
In addition to the 30,000,000 words which oc-
cupy the 22,000 pages, the Encyclopaedia contains
338 full-page plates and 071 smaller maps and
plans. There are over 9000 illustrations in all.
For the MS. alone a sum of more than £OO,OOO was
paid to the 1100 contributors, and the printing,
illustrating, and binding, which are of the highest
quality, entail so much expenditure that the En-
cyclopaedia unquestionably is the most costly pro-
duction ever offered to the public.
The ninth edition of the “ Encyclopaedia Bri-
tannica ”is the National Work of Reference. No
steps will be taken towards preparing a tenth
edition for many years, and when a new edition
is undertaken, it will require least 12 or 15
years to complete it. The present edition, with
the supplement now in preparation, will therefore
be the standard work of reference for at least 15
or 20 years to come.
THE SPECIAL BOOKCASE.
Some people may hesitate to take advantage of
“ THE TIMES ” offer, simply because they have
no shelf-room for the volumes. “ THE TIMES ”
cut down the price of the “ENCYCLOPAEDIA
BRITANNICA ” by over 50 per cent., but the
size of the volumes—like their quality—remains
unchanged. And the cost of a Bookcase large
enough to hold the 25 massive volumes would,
under ordinary conditions, be a serious addition to
the cost of the “ENCYCLOPAEDIA.”
For the convenience, therefore, of those who
subscribe through “THE TIMES,” a handsome
Revolving Bookcase has been specially designed to
receive the “ENCYCLOPAEDIA.” Subscribers
who elect to pay for the work in monthly instal-
ments of one guinea each, will, if they desire the
Bookcase, be called upon to make three further
monthly payments (after the payments for the
“ ENCYCLOPAEDIA ” have been completed).
Subscribers who pay for their sets in «ash may
procure the Bookcase by remitting a cheque for £3
The only thing that can deprive anyone of his share in the benefits of this special enterprise of “The Times” is neglecting to apply in time,
for so long as they last these magnificent sets will go to the first who apply. One set is for you if your application reaches us in time. It is a case of “ A Prize
■for the Prompt ” —a prize in sovereigns—sovereigns that go to make up the amount saved by ordering your set of the “ Eneyclopaidia Britannica ” NOW.

ONE HUNDRED AND FOURTH HALF-YEARLY REPORT
—OB’—
The Commercial Banking Company of Sydney
LIMITED.
Presented to the Shareholders at an Ordinary General Meeting held at the Head Banking House, George-street,
Sydney, on Friday, 20th July, 1900.
The Directors submit to the Shareholders a Balance-Sheet showing the Liabilities and Assets of the Bank on the
30th June, 1900, and present the following report :
The balance at the credit of Profit and Loss Account, after deducting rebate on current bills and accrued interest
on fixed deposits, reducing premises account, paying note tax, and providing for land and income
taxes and bad and doubtful debts, amounts to £70,151 12 0
out of which the Directors recommend the following appropriations :
To the payment of a Dividend for the half-year at the rate of 10 per cent, per
annum .. .. .. .. .. .. .. £50,000 0 0
„ Balance being undivided Profit ~ , .. ’ .. .. \. .. .. 20,151 12 0 £70,151 12 0
The Board record, with feelings of regret, the death during the half-year of Mr. John de Villibrs Lamb, an
Auditor of the Bank. The vacancy thus created was filled by the appointment of Mr. Wilfrid Law Docker.
A Requisition having been received from twenty-one members of the Company, holding in the aggregate Five
Hundred Shares and upwards in the capital of the Company, requiring the Directors to convene an Extraordinary
General Meeting of the Company for the purpose of considering certain alterations in the Articles of Association, the
Directors have convened an Extraordinary General Meeting of the Company to be held at the Head Banking-House
of the Company on Friday, the 20th July, at 12.15 o’clock in the afternoon, due notice of which has been given by
advertisement, as provided by the Articles of Association.
This Meeting is called upon to elect a Director, consequent on the retirement, in terms of the Articles of Associa-
tion, of the Honorable Henry Moses, M.L.C., who is eligible for re-election, and has given the requisite notice that
he is a candidate for the office. It also rests with the Meeting to appoint Auditors for the ensuing year.
The Dividend, free of Income Tax, is now payable.
EDWARD KNOX, Chairman.
Dr.

Sydney, 16th July, 1900.
We hereby certify that we have examined the bills, compared the balances, and counted the coin in the Head
Office of Tub Commercial Banking Company op Sydney, Limited, and have compared the returns of the Branches,
and have found the same as specified in the foregoing- Balance Sheet.
F. T. HUMPHERYI
W. L. DOCKER /
Auditors.
The following resolutions were carried unanimously
“ That the Report and Statements of Account be received and adopted, and ordered to be circulated
among the Proprietors.”
“ That the Honorable Henry Moses, M.L.0., be re-elected a Director of the Bank.”
“ That W. L. Docker, Esq., and E. S. Oape, Esq., be appointed Auditors for the ensuing year.”
“ That the thanks of the Shareholders be presented to the Directors, General Manager, and Officers of the
Bank for their very satisfactory management of the affairs of the institution.”
T. A. DIBBS, General Manager.
In moving the adoption of the Report, the Chairman, Sir Edward Knox, said :
I have the pleasure to inform you that the Directors consider that the time has arrived when they feel warranted
in recommending the increase of the dividend to 10 per cent, per annum, a step which, having regard to the times the
colony has passed through since 1893, will doubtless meet with the approval of the Shareholders. It is satisfactory
to note the improved prospects of trade and the increased value of properties generally. Useful rains have fallen
throughout the greater portion of New South Wales and in many parts of Queensland, giving hopes of a better har-
vest and clip of wool. The mining industry is now more systematically and successfully pursued than formerly,
while the. South African war has opened up a new market for our meats, horses and forage, and it is thought by many
that at its termination a still greater opening for Australian trade and enterprise will be established.
Comparing the volume of business with that of twelve months ago, you will find progression marked in every
line of the Balance Sheet. On the debit side the increases have been in the—
Notes in Circulation from .. .. .. .. .. £398,000 to £427,000
Bills .. .. .. .. .. .. 776,000 „ 1,013,000
Deposits, &c. .. .. .. .. .. .. 10,267,000 „ 11,134,000
Profit .. .. .. .. .. .. .. 64,000 ~ 76,000

Or an increase of the business by £1,146,659 during the past year. This also must be satisfactory to the proprietors.
Since our last meeting the Federation of the Australian Colonies has progressed to the extent that the Imperial
Parliament has passed the Commonwealth Bill. Hitherto it has not been the province of the Bank to take part in
political matters, but the Directors were impelled to take prompt action, in the interests of all banks and financial
institutions, with regard to the 74th Clause of the Bill, and joined with three other Colonial banks in a petition to the
Home Government, praying that the right of appeal to the Privy Council, as now existing, shall be maintained in its
entirety. We have now been informed by cable through the public press that the Attorney-General of England has
stated that due provision has been made in the Bill, giving the right of appeal to all of Her'Majesty’s subjects.
I have to advise the receipt of a requisition to the Directors signed by the Honorable H. C. Dangar and twenty
other Shareholders, holding in the aggregate upwards of 2000 shares, to make certain alterations in the Articles of
Association. Mr. Dangar has also given notice that he intends to move certain resolutions to carry out the wishes of
the petitioners. An Extraordinary General Meeting has therefore been called to discuss the resolutions, which will
take place at the close of this Meeting, and I shall be glad if the shareholders will remain in their places till 12.15
p.m., the hour that has been fixed for the Meeting.
I have much pleasure in moving the adoption of the Report.

“Struck Down for Death/"
His Own Words.
Experts said he could not be cured
HIS PERFECT CURE A SURPRISE TO EVERYBODY.
(From the Freeman’s Journal.)
(by our own report**.)
Mr. J. Hawkes, of Hawkesview,
Guildford, N.S.W., is now one of the
strongest and healthiest-looking men one
could meet in a day’s march. We heard
that for five long years he had been a terrible
sufferer, and that a course of Clements
Tonic had completely restored his health,
so this paper (The Freeman’s Journal , Syd
aey) dispatched a reporter to see him.
“ You look as though you’d never had a
lay’s illness in your life,” said our reporter
>n seeing Mr. Hawkes.
“I may look well enough new,” was the
reply, “ and
My Health is Perfect,
but once I was struck down for death. The
doctors have given me up, and it seemed as
though nothing remained for me hut to
settle my affairs of this world and prepare
ior the next.”
**‘You must have had a narrow squeak,
then. What first caused your illness t”
I ** It must have been coming on for a long
time before it showed itself. My ©ocupation
was very trying. I was a market gardener,
and I carted large quantities of vegetables
to the Belmore Markets. This necessitated
getting up very early in the morning, and,
as a matter of fact, I often waited up all
night so as to he first in the field ana get
the best prices. My meals I got at very
irregular hours. I began to get a
Queer, Giddy Feeling,
whioh I can hardly describe, and one day,
after I came back from the markets, I was
seized with a fit. That was the beginning.
I temporarily got better of this attack, but
I was a changed man. Shooting pains
would suddenly dart through my back and
head, after which a feeling of sickness and
Faintness would Overwhelm
ne. My digestion went all wrong, and
vhat little I ate dud. no good, for I got more
ike a confirmed invalid every day.’
“ Did you see a doctor T”
“ Yes, several; but they didn’t know
/hat was the matter at first; but seme of
them said I was suffering from a sluggish
and disordered Hver, and they gave me a
whole host of directions as to diet and how
I was to live, besides any quantity of medi-
cines. K tided hot bandages to my back and
abdomen, but got no relief from thorn, nor
did the doctors’ physic do me any permanent
good. AH day long I was in such a sluggish
state that I eared for nothing. I was in a
sort of sluggish
Stupor or Lethargy,
vhich never left me. I suffered in this way
ior over five years. At last I got into such
a Respondent state that when I wished to
see the doctor I was too nervous to drive to
his house. I was frightened of going off
through the shaking of the ©art, so I was
forced to accept what support I could get
from a friend, and crawl along with him and
’he help of a stick.
I had got so Weak
that I couldn’t walk without a stick. _ My
face was drawn, and anyone could see in it
the pallor of a long-continued disease. What
I have described was my usual state, but
every now and again the shooting pains in
my head and back would become more in-
tense, and then I should have another fit.
Thinking that perhaps I could obtain better
treatment I
Went to the Hospital.
Three doctors there gave me a long and
very careful examination, but they could
not tell definitely what was the matter.
And all their medicines and attention failed
to cure me. Every doctor asked me the
same question, ‘What is the matter with
you ?
This Silly Question,
in my enfeebled state, used to rouse and
irritate me. ‘ That’s what I came to you to
know,’ I replied.”
“ Your case must have seemed hopeless ?”
** Yes; I had very little hope of ever
getting better. I became very low-spirited*
I took jx 9 interest in anything. I
Consulted an Expert
in liver disease. He told me that my livei
was in a very bad state, and led me tc
understand t' at
I Could Never Expect
to be cured; so I gave up all hope of getting
better again. I resigned myself to my fate.
I felt that I was already in the grasp of
Death, and lay in bed expecting my end.
“ You cheated death, however, judging
by your appearance.”
“Yes, and this is how it happened. My
nephew recommended Clements Tonic. I
got some, hoping it would at least relieve
me.” .
“ And did it Mr. Hawkes ?”
“ The first dose was more use than all the
other medicines I had tried. It was very
effective, for it
Struck at the Root
of my complaint. From the first Clement.-.
Tonic had a stimulating action on my liver.
The pains in my back were soon easier, and
my head felt lighter and clearer than it- had
done for a long time.”
“ Did you still have fits ?”
« No, not a fit; it stopped them at once,
persevered with Clements Tonic, and it
was plain to everyone that I was getting
better daily. I
lacked the Doctors and scorned
their prescriptions. Their me-
dicines did not touch my dis-
ease. Clements Tonic cured it,
and all the evils went away without my being
aware of it. My appetite returned, I put
on flesh again, and soon got strong. I lost
all that tired weary feeling of lassitude and
was quite bright and cheerful, and instead
of sitting half stupefied in a chair all day or
Lying like a Log
in bod, I folt lively aßd energetic. A few
weeks’ treatment with Clements Tonic quite
restored me, but, of course after five long
years’ illness one cannot expect to
Rebuild a Wasted System
In week, bat the way Clements Tonic
brought me along was a perfect surprise, not
only to me, but to everybody. The only
regret I have new is that I had not been
lucky enough to strike Clements Tonic at
the start. If I had known its value earlier
that first attack would have been my last,
and I should have saved
Five Lone Years of Suffering*
I am thankful, however, that I came across
Clements Tonic when I did— * Better late
than never.’ It entirely eradicated all
disease from my system, and did what
doctor’s physic could not do in all those
years. In short, it saved my life.”
“ And may I publish your endorsement,
Mr. Hawkes?”
“ Certainly, it is the simple truth that
I have told you ; I am here to prove it, and
all the neighbours know I was
Knocking at Death’s Door
for five years. I have proved Clements
Tonic a genuine article ; I owe my life to it.
Why should I, or anybody else, object or be
ashamed to endorse such a valuable medicine.

A Woman in Earnest!
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
This is the true history of the killing of Arthur
Franklin; founddead, one morning in the seventies,
upon a bush-track in Gippsland. The skull was
fractured over the right ear ; the belongings were
untouched ; the ground was marked with horse’s
tracks—traceable into the Jerap-Jerap swamp, but
not out thereof. Which things-with a ‘ ‘ person-or-
persons-unknown ” verdict—have made, till now,
the whole of the story.
• * * *
"I sometimes think,” the woman said, “that
you are not: —quite right in your head !
The husband—a large, light man, with super-
proximate eyes beneath a narrow tower of fore-
head-looked hard at her for half-a-minute before
out of his carefully-tended russet beard came the
sneering cackle that had rasped upon her soul for
six sordid years of disillusion.
“ Think much about Arty Franklin, now ?” he
said, and the woman —she was brown, and dark,
and’suavely pretty—put a hand suddenly to her
throat before she flashed upon him a glance of
which he fully felt the edge.
“ You needn’t trouble to lie about it 1 ” he said,
as his cheek whitened with an added malevolence.
“Iteowyoudo!”
“Why should I lie about it?” Mrs. Staffock
answered, in the small, cold voice of concentrated
antipathy. “And why should I not think of
Arthur Franklin ?—he is dead ! ”
“As you’d like me to be, in his place, no
doubt ?”
“ As I should like you to be, in his place—yes!
she answered, with a cool accord of eye and voice
that thrust his rancor, for the moment, behind his
blank astonishment. But then, with a beast-like
twitching of the nose, he stood suddenly over her.
“ By God !” he began, and then her face stopped
him—the eye that feared him not, and the mouth
that despised him utterly. His own face took on
the hideous grin of fury forcing itself to a smile.
He went back to his chair, pocketed his hands,
stretched out his breeched and booted legs.
“ Candour,” he said, slowly, “calls for candour
in return. You never knew why Franklin threw
you over ! Shall I tell you? ”
“You ! ”
“Yes, I. I ought to know; for it was my
doing. Ah ! you condescend to be interested
now, do you ? ”
She was strangely white about the nostrils, but
her needle never altered its pace. “Go on,” she
said, in the hushed voice of one about to look upon
a corpse. “ I always suspected ”
“ That I had a finger in that pie ? Quite so ”
putting on by this time a fair show of cynical cool-
n ?ss. “ Rather a good joke, it is—though I’ll
0 :cuse you if you’re not quite able to see it. You
ware good enough, just now, to credit me with
be'ng a bit cranky ! Well, I didn’t exactly tell
Franklin that you were personally off-your-head,
but I told him that the family was queer. Your
late lamented father, I took the small liberty of
saying, had ended his days in straw ! ”
“In straw?” the astonished woman repeated,
mechanically.
“Yes. The worst kind of lunatics, you know,
are bedded in straw, because ”
“What hellish invention is this? Say it is a
lie, Stephen ! I have come to know, too well,
what you are capable of—but, surely, even
you ”
She had risen, and stood, hand-clasped, beside
him —more beautiful, in her agony of questioning,
than he had ever before seen her.
“Lie? It’s true enough! And, by Heaven!
if you always looked as you do now, I’d still say
you were worth the little swindle ! ”
He stretched out, with half-hesitating air of
proprietorship, a hand from which she shrank as
from a hot iron. And then, out of a horror-
quickened comprehension—even in the moment of
stepping back to her seat —there flashed up clear
before her the method of a revenge that called
upon her, straightway, for an acted part.
“Good God!” she moaned, drooping, with
hand-hidden face, from her chair. “How —how
did you come to know ? ”
Staffock jerked in his legs and sat suddenly up-
right, with a dawn of terror in his shifty eyes.
“ Know what ? ” he asked sharply, after a pause.
“That there is— is—mental infirmity in my
family ! That ” here, with fine histrionic
instinct, she dropped her hands and forced her-
self, as it seemed, to look him piteously in the
face —“ my poor father died —as you say—raving
mad ! ”
Into the florid insolence of the man’s cheek
there came suddenly a sickly mottling. He
gasped like a dying fish, and a fierce joy filled the
woman.
“What!” she cried, and smote her hands to-
gether with deftly simulated dismay. “You
didn’t know it? You hit upon the truth by
chance ? And I —unfortunate that lam ! —I have
let out the dreadful secret myself ! ”
The man—when she had fled the room —sat
long with limp arms hanging, and his head
sunken under the weight of the horror that had
come home to perch upon it.
» * • *
“Well, doctor?”
Dr. Krayke—a small, interjectory man with a
stoop and a ferocious protrusion of green-grey
whisker —pulled out a huge red handkerchief and
blew the blast of brief perpension before he an-
swered.
“ I have—ha !—bad news for you, madam ! ”
“ I can bear it! Death? ”
“ Oh, no ! Not—hum !—death, by any means !
But some people would—ho !—say it was worse !”
Mrs. Staffock sat down and fixed him with
black eyes that spoke of no emotion.
“Worse than death ?—kindly explain!” she
said in such a weather-criticising tone that little
Krayke was moved unto dramatic effect.
“Your husband, madam,” he said, “has been
deliberately poisoning himself ! ”
Indifferently she nodded. “ I knew ! Brandy!
He’s been at it for nearly a month, now ! But
he’s pretty tough, isn’t he ? ”
“ Tough ! Oh, yes ; tough enough —physically !
But Krayke rubbed his nose, reflectively, for a
moment with his whip-handle—“have you—
hum ! —any knowledge of your husband’s family
history ? ”
She shook her head. “He has always been
very reticent about himself and his belongings.
He is an Englishman and that’s about all I
know ! ”
“An Englishman—yes ! And from —ha !—my
own county. Consequently, I know more about
him than you do —more, possibly, than he knows
himself; for he has been kept ignorant, I fancy,
of certain facts which have —ho ! —an important
bearing upon his present condition. His grand-
father —to cut the matter short —died, a violent
lunatic, in Hanwell, and two of his uncles were
confined, when I left England, in private asylums.
Pardon me, my dear madam ! ” as Mrs. Staffock
rose suddenly from her chair, “I have —ha!
been injudicious ! This information ”
“ Has not affected me, as you suppose, doctor.
Go on, pray ! The brandy-drinking, you think,
has developed—or is developing ”
“ Mental alienation in your husband! You
hum ! —apprehend me perfectly, madam ! I shall
bring Gunn, of Hutchinson’s Creek, with me to-
morrow ; but I fear there’s not much doubt that
he will —ha !—confirm my opinion.”
“ That my husband is a lunatic ? ”
“Plainly, then, madam —since you—ho ! —don’t
mince matters yourself—yes ! There are already
well-marked delusions. He is strongly possessed,
for instance, with the notion that you —hum !
you will pardon me ! —are—ha ! —not quite right
in your mind !
Her pitying smile was excellently done, and
Krayke, with an answering smirk of deprecation,
went on to say that, though the delusion was quite
a typical one, there was a detail about which he
wished to inquire.
“ You will—hum ! —pardon me again, madam—
but you did not, I suppose, at any time tell your
husband that there had been—ha ! —insanity in
your own family ? ”
With high-raised eyebrows, she looked him
calmly in the face. “Even if I had,” she said,
“ it would not have been true ! ”
“ No, no !—of course not! ” Krayke said—
accepting the implied denial. “ Expect me, then,
with Dr. Gunn, to-morrow; and, in the mean-
time, if you will kindly see that Mr. Staffock has
nothing to—ho !—excite him, the medicine I will
send over as soon as I get home will keep him
fairly well composed.”
Mrs. Staffock, shaking with internal laughter,
watched the little doctor climb upon his long-
legged chestnut; then, with the face of Atropos,
she went straight to her husband’s room.
He was in bed—starting at every sound —thril-
ling with “fantastic terrors never felt before”;
but, as the woman knew, no more mad—yet !
than she was. Quietly she sat down and spoke
without preamble.
“ About five weeks ago I told you a lie. I said
my father had died insane. I need not ask you ”
—with a hard little laugh—“ if you remember.”
With her steady, penetrating gaze she caught
his furtive, wandering eyes, held them, and went
on with slow incisiveness.
“It was, I say, a lie —born of that other mon-
strous lie you told to Arthur Franklin.”
The man, with a twitching hand, covered his
voiceless mouth, but his eyes asked, wildly—
“ Why ? ”
“To make you suffer as you have suffered. To
lay the dread upon you that has destroyed you.
You have not had —I thank God for it ! —an easy
moment since.”
He looked away from her with a shudder—
looked back and said in a husky whimper : “And
now you say it was all a lie. How am Ito know
what to believe ? ”
“It doesn’t matter whether you believe or not.
There is more ! Were you fool enough to think I
would undeceive you without having worse be-
hind ? You have often said you couldn’t make me
out. Do you begin a little to ‘ make me out ’
now ? ”
Her placid, passionless ferocity—more terrible
than any vehemence—held him motionless, with
sinking jaw and horribly questioning eyes.
“ Dr. Krayke says you are a lunatic ! ”
The straight, swift statement—making potent
combination with his own nameless apprehensions
—shook Staffock to the soul. Like a scared child,
he covered his clammy face with the bed-clothes.
“Dr Krayke,” went on the steady, remorseless
voice, “ says your grandfather was mad ! —that
two of your uncles were mad !—that you, yourself,
only needed such a drinking-bout as this to send
you stark, staring mad, also ! ”
“It is false ! ” With a despairing effort to re-
assure himself, he thrust out again his ghastly
countenance —green with terror and streaming
with sweat. “I am not mad ! ”
“No!” the woman said, tranquilly as ever.
“ Not quite ! But you have evidently not far to
travel in that direction ; and ” —lowering her voice
still more, and dropping the syllables with merci-
less deliberation into his shuddering consciousness
—“I intend —that you shall go— the whole of the
way / ”
“Devil!” he screeched, and sprang up with
clutching hands; but the woman’s eye never
wavered. “Just so!” she said. “A violent
lunatic! ”
His tottering sanity warned him against' such a
fatal blunder. Trembling, he stood and looked at
her for a few moments —his mouth working
hideously within his foam-streaked beard. Then
—as his eye fell upon his clothes, and travelled
thence to the window —she divined his unspoken
thought, and knew that her work was done.

“ I’ll have the Gambler run-in and saddled,”
she said to herself, as she left the room. “ He'll
be wanted before long, I fancy 1 ”
*.* • •
Arthur Furnival Franklin, sworn, said:—“l
am an artist, making a sketching-tour on foot.
The remains are those of Mr. Stephen Staffock,
of Yamarroo. I knew him very well before his
marriage, five or six years ago, but I had not seen
him since, until last Wednesday.” To the Coro-
ner : “ Very well; I will describe what happened
as consecutively as I can. On that day I was on the
tramp from Gladstone to Karkarumbah, and
about midway it came on to rain so hard that I
took shelter in an old bark hut beside the track.
In half-an-hour or so it cleared up, and, just as I
was lighting my pipe for a start, I heard the
tread of a horse. I went outside, and saw a man
coming along on a big grey horse. The horse
was tired-looking and soaked with sweat, and the
man had a strange appearance, for he had no hat,
and his feet were out of the stirrups. He was
swinging his arms about, too, and talking to him-
self, and, as he got nearer, I recognised him for
Staffock. A good deal altered, but no mistake
about him ; so I stepped out to speak to him, but,
the moment he saw me, he pulled up short and
glared at me as if his eyes would come out of his
head. No ; I hadn’t time to speak to him before he
screamed out, ‘My God ! —a dead man ! I must
be mad, then ! That devil has had her wish ! ’
Then he wheeled round and galloped away to the
right as fast as the horse could lay legs to the
ground. I ran after him, for I thought he must
soon come to grief at that pace, amongst the
timber, but, all of a sudden, I lost sight of horse
and man over the edge of the gully where they
were found. I climbed down a bit, myself, but I
iibuld see nothing of them, so I went on as fast as
r could to Karkarumbah, and informed the police.
That’s all I know.” To the Coroner : “ Oh, there
was a false rumor among my Melbourne acquaint-
ances, about a year back, that I had been drowned
in Queensland; perhaps that accounts for his
reference to a dead man. No; I have not the
remotest idea what he meant about some devil
having her wish. He didn’t appear to know, him-
self, what he was saying.”
» » • #
“ Arthur! ”
A horse was reined suddenly up beside Frank-
lin, stolidly pursuing his interrupted journey.
He looked up without surprise, and also—as the
woman noted with a freezing of the heart—with-
out interest.
How d’ye do, Mrs. Staffock ? I saw you at
the inquest!”
“Saw me ! —and took no notice ! ”
“No. You see—you have gone out of mv
life ! ”
“I ? It was you who went out of mine—because
of a paltry lie ! ”
His sleepy grey eye lighted up a little. “That
was a lie, then, that Staffock told me about your
family ? I have sometimes thought so since; else
why should he have married you himself ? But,
how did you come to know of it? Staffock
wouldn’t be. likely to let it out, at all events ! ”
Let it out ? He boasted of it! —gloried in it!
But ’’—she leant down from her saddle till the
man almost shrank from the close luridity of her
eyes—“ he has paid for that with his life ! ” .
Franklin stopped and held her rein. “Non-
sense !” he said. “ You didn’t take his life ! ”
“Bah !” She flushed with a fiery impatience
of his literalness. “I took his reason! I drove
him to the madness that drove him to his death !
Now do you understand ? ”
He understood, yes !—that he had, after all,
been lucky in that long-ago lie that had come
between him and this overwhelming woman.
This, then, was the devil Staffock had cried out
about! He shivered a little as he recalled the
ghastly, despairing face, and it was even with a
shade of pity for the dead man that he let go her
rein and formally lifted his hat. “ Let me wish
you good-bye !” he said, stiffly. “ I have a good
many miles to do yet before dark.”
She shrank in her saddle as from a blow—then
wheeled the horse suddenly in front of him and
laughed an evil little laugh. “What, Arthur?—
now that I am free—and ” —with a half-con-
temptuous afterthought—“ rich ! ” *
Her eye frightened him. Really most uncom-
fortable persons to deal with—these women who
were so absurdly in earnest!
“I positively must be pushing-on!” he said,
half-peevishly. “ And, as to what you do me the
honor of suggesting, you will doubtless admit the
superior claims of ’’—with a feeble snigger—“ the
present Mrs. Franklin ! ”
There wasn’t any Mrs. Franklin, but the inven-
tion of one, he told himself, was a fortunate con-
ceit; for, on the instant, without another word or
look, Mrs. Staffock pulled her horse round and
rode slowly away.
For a couple of minutes Franklin walked
straight ahead; then, at a slight turn in the track,
he stopped and looked back. She, too, had halted,
and sat with her back to him, erect and rigid as an
effigy—for him, had he but known it, the Statue
of Fate ! He shrugged his shoulders and went
on. “A miserably foolish thing,” he was repeat-
ing to himself, to be too much in earnest! ”
when, with a sudden rhythmic pounding of the
earth, the horse was back upon him at a speed as
furious as the swing with which the rider’s loaded
whip-handle took him just above the ear.
Forty yards beyond, the “earnest woman”
pulled round, and, riding back through the dust
her horse had raised, looked calmly down at It.
“I had gone out of your life, had I?” she mut-
tered. “ Well, at all events, you’ve gone out of
hers 1 ”
ALEX. MONTGOMERY.
The Rhodes organ, London Express, in a
leading article :
We want Mr. Kruger removed from all further oppor-
tunities of stirring up his countrymen. A commodious
villa in St. Helena, with a large stoep and plenty of coffee
and tobacco, seems to be the legitimate range of his
future ambition.
Mars.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
I am the G-od of War, in banishment I grope,
A mythical monstrosity of Greece,
An ancient red-eyed tramp upon Oblivion’s slope,
A quarry for the Nazarene’s police.
Or graciously they give me understrapping jobs
Among their hybrid dynasties of hell ;
Yet in their tamest blood my passion madly throbs,
Incarnate in their holiest forms I dwell.
Their choicest acolytes, from psalm and pallid lusts,
From knees and weak repentance, I’ve enticed
To make swashbuckler feasts with sacramental
crusts,
A Sunday circus trick-dog of their Christ.
When to my lurid call battalioned Hate arrays
And in the orgy Pity’s senses swim,
My most degrading whims unquestioned each
obeys,
When both sides make a fetish Joss of Him.
And how the inextinguishable laughter still
Through all the halls of old Olympus peals,
When victor Christian Cains retreating Abels kill,
The Lamb of God a-baaing at their heels.
Te Deums to the God of Battles shout the priests !
The maids to soldier-brutes their girdles toss !
All’s well, my dainty Venus, lead me to your
feasts,
The pale God’s dead, the charm has left His Cross !
Melb.
