[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Click, clack ! Click, click, click, click, ting '
Scr .
“ Excuse me a moment.”
The clicking sound suddenly ceased, and the
brown-haired girl turned from her type-writer to
the doorway whence the voice proceeded.
“Could you run off a few letters for me?”
glancing carelessly at the girl’s pale face; “I’m
m an awful hurry to catch the Brisbane mail.”
"Certainly,” she replied, sighing wearily.
Just take a chair,” indicating one beside her
desk. He watched her with interest as she re-
moved the sheet from the machine to insert a
fresh one.
“ How many copies ? ”
“ Two, please. Do you like typewriting ? ”
Oh, yes.” She put in the paper and wrote
the date. “Whom to, please?”
“Brown and Jones, Brisbane.”
Click, click, click.
“Dear Sir. Oh, have you got ‘ dear sir ’ ? Well
—er—Yours of the 6th—no, the 7th—received.
Make that 6 7th instant. ’ You’ve got 4 received 5 ?
Oh, well, never mind. And contents noted—
carefully noted ’ would be better. Thank you.
Have you been typewriting long ? ”
“ Three years. ‘ Carefully noted ’ .”
“ Oh, yes ! Er—l have—just begin a new para-
graph—l have called on the principal business-
houses in this city—er. Have you got that ? And
shown the samples of your goods, but, so far, have
received . How many words a minute can
you write ? ”
“I don’t know. ‘Have received ’ .”
“Have received very few orders. Doesn’t
writing so fast tire your fingers ? ”
For half an hour or so, the girl tapped the keys
rapidly to the man’s dictation. Once he bent un-
necessarily near her in order to read what he had
said, and, when she gave him the last sheets, he
pressed her hand, though she instantly withdrew
it.
Having gathered up his letters and paid for
them, he bowed himself out, muttering “sonicely
written”; “be in again soon,” &c., and stumbled
over the door-mat into the street.
“Another idiot!” murmured the girl, returning
to her machine with a sigh ; and, replacing the
withdrawn sheet, she went clicking on with her
original work.
• » * *
Mrs. Septon was industriously sewing; her
husband was deep in the evening paper.
“John, dear?”
“ John, dear ” merely grunted. They had been
married two years.
“John, do you remember the first time I saw
you ? You came into the office one day to get
some letters typed, you know. You said— ”
“ Why the devil does a woman always want to
chatter when a man is reading the paper I
wonder! ’ broke out hubby, throwing down the
paper, and kicking the cat off the hearth-rug.
Mrs. S. stitched on in silence.
“MARORL."
The humors of the Boer war pass un-
noticed since the Chow trouble began, but many
funny things have been happening in the O. R.
Colony all the same. General Colville’s downfall
was infinitely droll to anyone who understands
the difference between Tommy Atkins plain and
Thomas Atkins colored. Colville went out in
chief command of the Guards, the fine “soft”
men of the British army who illustrate the pomp
and circumstance of war on parade (in London as
a rule) and are generally regarded by nursemaids
as a terror to Hmgland’s enemies. The Queen
hkewise Albert Edward, Prince of Walel the
Princess of Wales, and all the other members of
the Royal Family who get prayed for in the
Church of England Litany, make a point of
pinning their faith to the Guards on the rare
occasions when they are ordered abroad. News-
paper reporters always speak of the Guards with
awe. It is generally understood that the duty of
the well-grown men m the extra-impressive finery
is to wait on the outskirts of a battle until the
General cries Up Guards and at ’em ! ” where-
upon they rush forward and win the day. Well
the value of the boasted Guards in South Africa
can be judged from the fact that Lord Roberts
made an example of their high-toned General by
packing him off to London the other day The
i he is°n°i n^ ny ® fan ?°us Review has been duly
labelled a Fraud, and the prestige of the padded
ornaments of Hyde Park is now at a heavy dis-
count. Another distressing “ take-down ” was
the capture of Colonel Spragge’s Irish Yeomanry
battalion by the nasty, vulgar veldtmen. Dublin
society, said the cable, went into mourning when
the news of that catastrophe arrived. Every
. fading family ” had a dear johnnie or two serv-
ing under Spragge. Anxiety of the most aristo-
cratic character was expressed concerning the wel-
fare of the haughty prisoners. The “best
people couldnt sleep for wondering what had
happened, and now that they know what hap
pened their anguish is worse than ever. Colonel
bpragge’s brdhant amateurs were sent back to
the lines disguised as Boers in the ragged clothes
of their disrespectful captors.
